Fact	ORDER  1.
Fact	Judgment of The Chief Justice, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Fact	Delivered by <NAME>, J.
Fact	<NAME>, J.  Forty and three years ago was founded this republic with the fourfold objective of securing to its citizens justice, liberty, equality and fraternity.
Fact	<ORG>smen of the highest order the like of which this country has not seen since - belonging to the fields of law, politics and public life came together to fashion the instrument of change - the Constitution of India.
Fact	They did not rest content with evolving the framework of the <ORG>; they also pointed out the goal-and the methodology for reaching that goal.
Fact	In the preamble, they spelt out the goal and in parts III and IV, they elaborated the methodology to be followed for reaching that goal.
Fact	2.
Fact	<ORG>, though elected on the basis of a limited franchise, was yet representative of all sections of society.
Fact	Above all, it was composed of men of vision, conscious of the historic but diffic ult task of carving an egalitarian society from out of a bewildering mass of religions, communities, castes, races, languages, beliefs and practices.
Fact	They knew their country well.
Fact	They understood their society perfectly.
Fact	They were aware of the historic injustices and inequities afflicting the society.
Fact	They realised the imperative of redressing them by constitutional means, as early as possible - for the alternative was frightening.
Fact	Ignorance, illiteracy and above all, mass poverty, they took note of.
Fact	They were conscious of the fact that the Hindu religion - the religion of the overwhelming majority - as it was being practiced, was not known for its egalitarian ethos.
Fact	It divided its adherents into four watertight compartments.
Fact	Those outside this fourtier system (chaturvarnya) were the outcastes (Panchamas), the lowliest.
Fact	They did not even believed all the caste system - ugly as its face was.
Fact	The fourth, shudras, were no better, though certainly better than the Panchamas.
Fact	The lowliness attached to them (Shudras  and Panchamas) by virtue of their birth in these castes, unconnected with their deeds.
Fact	There was to be no deliverance for them from this social stigma, except perhaps death.
Fact	They were condemned to be inferior.
Fact	All lowly, menial and unsavoury occupations were assigned to them.
Fact	In the rural life, they had no alternative but to follow these occupations, generation after generation, century after century.
Fact	It was their 'karma', they were told, the penalty for the sins they allegedly committed in their previous birth.
Fact	Pity is, they believed all this.
Fact	They were conditioned to believe it.
Fact	This mental blindfold had to be removed first.
Fact	This was a phenomenon peculiar to this country.
Fact	Poverty there has been - and there is - in every country.
RatioOfTheDecision	But none had the misfortune of having this social division - or as some call it, degradation - super-imposed on poverty.
RatioOfTheDecision	Poverty, low social status in Hindu caste system and the lowly occupation constituted - and do still constitute - a vicious circle.
Fact	The founding fathers were awa re of all this - and more.
Fact	3.
Fact	'Liberty, equality and fraternity' was the battle cry of the French Revolution.
Fact	It is also the motto of our Constitution, with the concept of 'Justice-Social Economic and Poilitical' - the sum-total of modern political thought - super-added to it.
Fact	Equality has been and is the single greatest craving of all human beings at all points of time.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has inspired many a great thinker and philosopher.
RatioOfTheDecision	All religious and political schools of thought swear by it, including the Hindu religious thought, if one looks to it ignoring the later crudities and distortions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Liberty of thought, expression, belief, faith and worship has equally been an abiding faith with all human beings, and at all times in this country in particular.
RatioOfTheDecision	Fraternity assuring the dignity of the individual has a special relevance in the Indian context, as this Judgment will illustrate in due course.
RatioOfTheDecision	4.
RatioOfTheDecision	The doctrine of equality has many facets.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a dynamic, and an evolving concept.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its main facets, relevant to Indian  Society, have been referred to in the preamble and the articles under the sub-heading "Right to equality"-(Articles 14 to 18).
RatioOfTheDecision	In short, the goal is "equality of status and of opportunity".
RatioOfTheDecision	Articles 14 to 18 must be understood not merely with reference to what they say but also in the light of the several articles in Part IV (Directive Principles of State Policy).
RatioOfTheDecision	"Justice, Social, Economic and Political", is the sum total of the aspirations incorporated in part IV.
RatioOfTheDecision	5.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 14 enjoins upon the state not to deny to any person "equality before the law" or "the equal protection of the laws" within the territory of India.
RatioOfTheDecision	Most constitutions speak of either "equality before the law" or "the equal protection of the laws", but very few of both.
RatioOfTheDecision	Section 1 of the XIV.
RatioOfTheDecision	Amendment to the U.S. Constitution uses only the latter expression while the Austrian Constitution (1920), the Irish Constitution (1937) and the West German Constitution (1949) use the expression "equal before the law".
RatioOfTheDecision	( Article 7 of the Universal  Declaration of Human Rights, 1948, of course, declares that "all are equal before the law and are entitled without any discrimination to equal protection of the law".)
RatioOfTheDecision	The content and sweep of these two concepts is not the same though there may be much in common.
RatioOfTheDecision	The content of the expression "equality before the law" is illustrated not only by Articles 15 to 18 but also by the several articles in Part IV, in particular, Articles 38, 39, 39A, 41 and 46.
RatioOfTheDecision	Among others, the concept of equality before the law contemplates minimising the inequalities in income and eliminating the inequalities in status, facilities and opportunities not only amongst individuals but also amongst groups of people, securing adequate means of livelihood to its citizens and to promote with special care the educational and economic interests of the weaker sections of the people, including in particular the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes and to protect them from social injustice and all forms of exploitation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, in a society w here equality of status and opportunity do not obtain and where there are glaring inequalities in incomes, there is no room for equality - either equality before law or equality in any other respect.
RatioOfTheDecision	6.
RatioOfTheDecision	The significance attached by the founding fathers to the right to equality is evident not only from the fact that they employed both the expressions 'equality before the law' and 'equal protection of the laws' in Article 14 but proceeded further to state the same rule in positive and affirmative terms in Articles 15 to 18.
RatioOfTheDecision	Through Article 15 they declared in positive terms that the state shall not discriminate against any citizen on the grounds only of religion, race, caste, sex, place of birth or any of them.
Statute	With a view to eradicate certain prevalent undesirable practices it was declared in Clause (2) of Article 15 that no citizen shall on the grounds only of religion, race, caste, sex, place of birth or any of them be subject to any disability, liability, restriction or condition with regard to shops, publ ic restaurants, hotels and place of public entertainment or to the use of well, tanks, bathing ghats, roads and place of public resort maintained wholly or partly out of state funds or dedicated to the use of general public.
Statute	At the same time, with a view to ameliorate the conditions of women and children a provision was made in Clause (3) that nothing in the said Article shall prevent the state from making any special provision for women and children.
Statute	7.
Statute	In as much as public employment always gave a certain status and power - it has always been the repository of <ORG> power - besides the means of livelihood, special care was taken to declare equality of opportunity in the matter of public employment by Article 16.
Statute	Clause (1) expressly declares that in the matter of public employment or appointment to any office under the state, citizens of this country shall have equal opportunity while Clause (2) declares that no citizen shall be discriminated in the said matter on the grounds only of religion, race, caste,  sex, descent, place of birth, residence or any of them.
Statute	At the same time, care was taken to declare in Clause (4) that nothing in the said Article shall prevent the state from making any provision for reservation of appointments or posts in favour of any backward class of citizen which in the opinion of the state is not adequately represented in the services under the state.
Statute	Article 17 abolishes the untouchability while Article 18 prohibits conferring of any titles (not representing military or academic distinction).
Statute	It also prohibits the citizens of this country from accepting any title from a foreign state.
Statute	8.
Statute	Article 16 has remained unamended, except for a minor amendment in Clause (3) whereas Article 15 had Clause (4) inserted in it by the First Amendment Act, 1951.
Statute	As amended, they read as follows:  15.
Statute	Prohibition of discrimination on grounds of religion, race, caste, sex or place of birth.
Statute	- (1) The <ORG> shall not discriminate against any citizen on grounds only of religion, race, caste, sex, place  of birth or any of them.
Statute	(2) No citizen shall, on grounds only of religion, race, caste, sex, place of birth or any of them, be subject to any disability, liability, restriction or condition with regard to-  (a) access to shops, public restaurants, hotels and places of public entertainment; or  (b) the use of wells, tanks, bathing ghats, roads and places of public resort maintained wholly or partly out of <ORG> funds or dedicated to the use of the general public.
Statute	(3) Nothing in this article shall prevent the <ORG> from making any special provision for women and children.
Statute	(4) Nothing in this article or in Clause (2) of Article 29 shall prevent the <ORG> from making any special provision for the advancement of any socially and educationally backward classes of citizens or for the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes.
Statute	16.
Statute	Equality of opportunity in matters of public employment.
Statute	- (1) There shall be equality of opportunity for all citizens in matters relating to employment or appointment to any office un der the <ORG>.
Statute	(2) No citizen shall, on grounds only of religion, race, caste, sex, descent, place of birth, residence or any of them, be ineligible for, or discriminated against in respect of, any employment or office under the <ORG>.
Statute	(3) Nothing in this article shall prevent <ORG> from making any law prescribing, in regard to a class or classes of employment or appointment to an office under the Government of, or any local or other authority within, a <ORG> of Union territory, any requirement as to residence within that <ORG> or Union territory prior to such employment or appointment.
Statute	(4) Nothing in this article shall prevent the <ORG> from making any provision for the reservation of apointments or posts in favour of any backward class of citizens which, in the opinion of the <ORG>, is not adequately represented in the services under the <ORG>.
Statute	(5) Noting in this article shall affect the operation of any law which provides that the incumbent of an office in connection with the affairs of any religi ous or denominational institution or any member of the governing body thereof shall be a person professing a particular religion or belonging to a particular denomination.
RatioOfTheDecision	The other provisions of the Constitution having a bearing on Article 16 are Articles 38, 46 and the set of articles in Part XVI.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (1) of Article 38 obligates the <ORG> to "strive to promote the welfare of the people by securing and protecting as effectively as it may a social order in which justice, social, economic and political, shall inform all the institutions of the national life."
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (2) of Article 38 , added by the 44th Amendment Act says, "the <ORG> shall, in particular, strive to minimise the inequalities in income, and endeavour to eliminate inequalities in status, facilities and opportunities, not only amongst individuals but also amongst groups of people residing in different areas or engaged in different vocations."
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 46 contains a very significant directive to the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It says:  46.
RatioOfTheDecision	Promotion of educationa l and economic interests of Scheduled Castes, Scheduled Tribes and other weaker sections.
RatioOfTheDecision	- The <ORG> shall promote with special care the educational and economic interests of the weaker sections of the people, and, in particular, of the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes, and shall protect them from social injustice and all forms of exploitation.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is evident that "the weaker sections of the people" do include the "backward class of citizens" contemplated by Article 16(4).
Statute	Part XVI of the Constitution contains "special provisions relating to certain classes".
Statute	The "classes" for which special provisions are made are, Scheduled Castes, Scheduled Tribes and the Anglo-Indian Community.
Statute	It also provides for appointment of a <ORG> to investigate the conditions of and the difficulties faced by the socially and educationally backward classes and to make appropriate recommendations.
Statute	Article 340 reads as follows:  340.
Statute	Appointment of a <ORG> to investigate the conditions of backward classes.
Statute	- (1)  The President may by order appoint a <ORG> consisting of such persons as he thinks tit to investigate the conditions of socially and educationally backward classes within the territory of India and the difficulties under which they labour and to make recommendations as to the steps that should be taken by the <ORG> of any State to remove such difficulties and to improve their condition and as to the grants that should be made for the purpose by the <ORG> or any State and the conditions subject to which such grants should be made, and the order appointing such <ORG> shall define the procedure to be followed by the <ORG>.
Statute	(2) A <ORG> so appointed shall investigate the matters referred them and present to the President a report setting out the facts as found by them and making such recommendations as they think proper.
Statute	(3) The President shall cause a copy of the report so presented together with a memorandum explaining the action taken thereon to be laid before <ORG>.
Statute	Ar ticle 338 , which has been extensively amended by the Sixty-fifth Amendment Act, provides for establishment of <ORG> to be known as '<ORG>'.
Statute	Clause (5) prescribes the duties of the <ORG>.
Statute	They are:  (5) It shall be duty of the <ORG>-  (a) to investigate and monitor all matters relating to the safeguards provided for the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes under this Constitution or under any other law for the time being in force or under any order of the Government and to evaluate the working of such safeguards;  (b) to inquire into specific complaints with respect to the deprivation of rights and safeguards of the Scheduled castes and Scheduled Tribes;  (c) to participate and advise on the planning process of socio-economic development of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes and to evaluate the progress of their development under the <ORG> and any <ORG>;  (d) to present to the  President, annually and at such other times as the <ORG> may deem fit, reports upon the working of those safeguards;  (e) to make in such reports recommendations as to the measures that should be taken by the <ORG> or any <ORG> for the effective implementation of those safeguards and other measures for the protection, welfare and socioeconomic development of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes; and  (f) to discharge such other functions in relation to the protection, welfare and development and advancement of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes as the President may, subject to the provisions of any law made by <ORG>, by rule specify.
Statute	Clause (6) provides that "the President shall cause all such reports to be laid before each <ORG> of <ORG> along with a memorandum explaining the action taken or proposed to be taken on the recommendations relating to the <ORG> and the reasons for the non-acceptance, if any, of any of such recommendations."
Statute	Clause (7) being relevant may also be read  here.
Statute	It reads, "where any such report, or any part thereof, relates to any matter with which any <ORG> <ORG> is concerned, a copy of such report shall be forwarded to the Governor of the <ORG> who shall cause it to be laid before <ORG> <ORG> along with a memorandum explaining the action taken or proposed to be taken on the recommendations relating to the <ORG> and the reasons for the non-acceptance, if any, of any of such recommendations."
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (10) [Clause (3) prior to 65th Amendment Act ] brings in socially and educationally backward classes identified by the <ORG> on the basis of the report of the <ORG> appointed under Article 340 and Anglo-Indians within the purview of the expressions "Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes".
RatioOfTheDecision	It reads as follows:  10.
Statute	In this article references to the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes shall be construed as including references to such other backward classes as the President may, on receipt of the report of a <ORG> appointed unde r Clause (1) of Article 340 , by order specify and also to the Anglo-Indian community.
Statute	Article 335 provides that "the claims of the members of the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes shall be taken into consideration, consistently with the maintenance of efficiency of administration, in the making of appointments to services and posts in connection with the affairs of the <ORG> or of a <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	It is obvious that if the claims of even Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes are to be taken into consideration consistently with the maintenance of efficiency of administration, the said admonition has to be respected equally while taking into consideration the claims of other backward classes and other weaker sections.
RatioOfTheDecision	THE FIRST BACKWARD CLASSES COMMISSION (<ORG>):  9.
RatioOfTheDecision	The proceedings of <ORG> on draft Article (10) disclose a persistent and strident demand from certain sections of the society for providing reservations in their favour in the matter of public employment.
RatioOfTheDecision	While  speaking on the draft Article 10(3) [ corresponding to Article 16(4)] Dr. <NAME> had stated, "then we have quite a massive opinion which insists that although theoretically it is good to have the principle that there shall be equality of opportunity, there must at the same time be a provision made for the entry of certain communities which have so far been outside the administration."
RatioOfTheDecision	It was this demand which was mainly responsible for the incorporation of Clause (4) in Article 16.
RatioOfTheDecision	As matter of fact, in some of the southern States, reservations in favour of O.B.Cs.
RatioOfTheDecision	were in vogue since quite a number of years prior to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was a demand for similar reservations at the center.
RatioOfTheDecision	In response to this demand and also in realisation of its obligation to provide for such reservations in favour of backward sections of the society, <ORG> appointed <ORG> under Article 340 of the Constitution on January 29, 1953.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG>, popularly known as <ORG> ommission, was required "to investigate the conditions of socially and educationally backward classes within the territory of India and the difficulties under which they labour and to make recommendations as to the steps that should be taken by the <ORG> or any <ORG> to remove difficulties and to improve their conditions".
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> submitted its report on March 30, 1955.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to it, the relevant factors to consider while classifying backward classes would be their traditional occupation and profession, the percentage of literacy or the general educational advancement made by them; the estimated population of the community and the distribution of the various communities throughout the state or their concentration in certain areas.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> was also of the opinion that the social position which a community occupies in the caste hierarchy would also have to be considered as well as its representation in Government service or in the Industrial sphere.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to the <ORG>, the causes of ed ucational backwardness amongst the educationally and backward communities were (i) traditional apathy for education on account of social and environmental conditions or occupational handicaps: (ii) poverty and lack of educational institutions in rural areas and (iii) living in inaccessible areas.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Chairman of the commission, <NAME>, however, had second thoughts after signing the report.
RulingByPresentCourt	In the enclosing letter addressed to the President he virtually pleaded for the rejection of the report on the ground that the reservations and other remedies recommended on the basis of caste would not be in the interest of society and country.
RulingByPresentCourt	He opined that the principle of caste should be eschewed altogether.
RatioOfTheDecision	Then alone, he said, would it be possible to help the extremely poor and deserving members of all the communities.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time, he added, preference ought to be given to those who come from traditionally neglected social classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	10.
RatioOfTheDecision	The report made by the <ORG> was considered by <ORG> nment, which apparently was not satisfied with the approach adopted by the <ORG> in determining the criteria for identifying the backward classes under Article 15(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	The Memorandum of action appended to the Report of the <ORG> while placing it on the table of the <ORG> [as required by Clause (3) of Article 340] on September 3, 1956, pointed out that the caste system is the greatest hindrance in the way of our progress to egalitarian society and that in such a situation recognition of certain specified castes as backward may serve to maintain and perpetuate the existing distinctions on the basis of caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Memorandum also found fault with certain tests adopted by the <ORG> for identifying the backward classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	It expressed the opinion that a more systematic and elaborate basis has to be evolved for identifying backward classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Be that as it may, the Report was never discussed by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	11.
RatioOfTheDecision	No meaningful action was taken after 1956 either for constituting another <ORG> or  for evolving a better criteria.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ultimately, on August 14, 1961, <ORG> wrote to ail the State Governments stating inter alia that "while the State Governments have the discretion to choose their own criteria for defining backwardness, in the view of <ORG> it would be better to apply economic tests than to go by caste."
RatioOfTheDecision	The letter stated further, rather inexplicably, that "even if <ORG> were to specify under Article 338(3) certain groups of people as belonging to 'other backward classes', it will still be open to every State Government to draw up its own lists for the purposes of Articles 15 and 16.
RatioOfTheDecision	As, therefore, the State Governments may adhere to their own lists, any All-India list drawn up by <ORG> would have no practical utility."
RatioOfTheDecision	Various State Governments thereupon appointed <ORG> for identifying backward classes and issued orders identifying the socially and educationally backward classes and reserving certain percentage of posts  in their favour.
RatioOfTheDecision	So far as the Central services are concerned, no reservations were ever made in favour of other backward classes though made in favour of Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes.
Precedent	THE SECOND BACKWARD CLASSES COMMISSION ( COMMISSION):  12.
Precedent	By an Order made by the President of India, in the year 1979, under Article 340 of the Constitution, <ORG> was appointed to investigate the conditions of socially and educationally backward classes within the territory of India, which <ORG> is popularly known as Mandal <ORG>.
Precedent	The terms of reference of the <ORG> were:  The terms of reference of the <ORG> were:-  (i) to determine the criteria for defining the socially and educationally backward classes;  (ii) to recommend steps to be taken for the advancement of the socially and educationally backward classes of citizens so identified;  (iii) to examine the desirability or otherwise of making provision for the reservation of appointments or posts in favour of such backw ard classes of citizens which are not adequately represented in public services and posts in connection with the affairs of the <ORG> or of any <ORG>; and  (iv) present to the President a report setting out the facts as found by them and making such recommendations as they think proper.
Precedent	The <ORG> was empowered to:-  (a) obtain such information as they may consider necessary or relevant for their purpose in such form and such manner as they may think appropriate, from <ORG>, the <ORG> Government, the <ORG> Territory Administrations and such other authorities, organisations or individuals as may in the opinion of the <ORG>, be of assistance to them: and  (b) hold their sittings or the sittings of such sub-committees as they may appoint from amongst their own members of such times and such places as may be determined by, or under the authority of the Chairman.
Precedent	13.
RulingByPresentCourt	The report of the <ORG> was required to be submitted not later than 31st December, 1979, which date was later extend ed upto December 31, 1980.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was so submitted.
RatioOfTheDecision	Chapter-I of the <ORG> deals with the Constitution of First Backward Classes <ORG> (<ORG>), its report, the letter of <NAME> to the President, the lack of follow-up action and the letter of <ORG> referred to hereinbefore to State Governments to draw up their own lists.
RatioOfTheDecision	It also points out certain "internal contradictions" in the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Chapter-II deals with the "Status of other backward classes in some States".
RatioOfTheDecision	It sets out the several provisions relating to reservation in favour of O.B.Cs.
RatioOfTheDecision	obtaining in several States and the history of such reservations.
RatioOfTheDecision	Chapter-III is entitled 'methodology and data base'.
RatioOfTheDecision	It sets out the procedure followed by the <ORG> and the material gathered by them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Paras 3.1 and 3.2 read thus:  3.1.
RatioOfTheDecision	One important reason as to why <ORG> could not accept the recommendations of <ORG> was that it had not worked out objective tests and criteria for the pr oper classification of socially and educationally backward classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	In several petitions filed against reservation orders issued by some State Governments, <ORG> and various <ORG> have also emphasised the imperative need for an empirical approach to the defining of socially and educationally backwardness or identification of Other Backward Classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	3.2 The <ORG> has constantly kept the above requirements in view in planning the scope of its activities.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was to serve this very purpose that the <ORG> made special efforts to associate the leading Sociologists, Research Organisations and Specialised Agencies of the country with every important facet of its activity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Instead of relying on one or two established techniques of enquiry, we tried to caste our net far and wide so as to collect facts and get feed-back from as large an area as possible.
RatioOfTheDecision	A brief account of this activity is given below.
RatioOfTheDecision	It then refers to the Seminar held by <ORG> of <ORG> in M arch 1979, to the questionnaire issued to all departments of <ORG> and to <ORG> (the proforma are compiled in Vol.
RatioOfTheDecision	II of the Report) the country-wide touring undertaken by the <ORG>, the evidence recorded by it, the socio-educational field survey conducted by it and other studies and Reports involved in its work.
RatioOfTheDecision	In Chapter-IV the <ORG> deals with the interrelationship between social backwardness and caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	It describes how the fourth caste, Shudras, were kept in a state of intellectual and physical subjugation and the historical injustices perpetrated on them.
RatioOfTheDecision	In para 4.5 the <ORG> states: "The real triumph of the caste system lies not in upholding the supremacy of the Brahmin, but in conditioning the consciousness of the lower castes in accepting their inferior status in the ritual hierarchy as a part of the natural order of things....
RatioOfTheDecision	It was through an elaborate, complex and subtle scheme of scripture, mythology and ritual that Brahminism succeeded in investing t he caste system with a moral authority that has been seldom effectively challenged even by the most ardent social reformers."
RatioOfTheDecision	14.
RatioOfTheDecision	Chapter-V deals with 'social dynamics of caste'.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this chapter, the <ORG> emphasises the fact that notwithstanding public declarations condemning the caste, it has remained a significant basis of action in politics and public life.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reference is made to several caste associations, which have come into being after the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The concluding part in this Chapter, para 5.17, reads:  The above account should serve as a warning against any hasty conclusion about the weakening of caste as the basis of social organisation of the Hindu society.
RatioOfTheDecision	The pace of social mobility is no doubt increasing and some traditional features of the caste system have inevitably weakened.
RatioOfTheDecision	But what caste has lost on the ritual front, it has more than gained on the political front.
RatioOfTheDecision	This has also led to some adjustments in the power equation between the high and low castes and thereby accentuated so cial tensions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether these tensions rent the social fabric or the country is able to resolve them by internal adjustments will depend on how understandingly the ruling high castes handle the legitimate aspirations and demands of the historically suppressed and backward classes.
Precedent	Chapter-VI deals with 'Social Justice, Merit and Privilege'.
Precedent	It attempts to establish, that merit in a elitist society is not something inherent but is the consequence of environmental privileges enjoyed by the members of higher castes.
Precedent	This is sought to be illustrated by giving an example of two boys - <NAME> and <NAME>.
Precedent	<NAME> is a village boy belonging to a backward class occupying a low social position in the village caste hierarchy.
Argument	He comes from a poor illiterate family and studies at a village school, where the level of instruction is woeful.
Argument	On the other hand, <NAME> comes from a fairly well-off middle class and educated family, attends one of the good public schools in the city, has assistance at home besides the means of acqui ring knowledge through television, radio, magazines and so on.
Argument	Even though both <NAME> and <NAME> possess the same level of intelligence, <NAME> can never compete with <NAME> in any open competition because of the several environmental disadvantages suffered by him.
Argument	15.
Precedent	Chapter-VII deals with 'Social justice.
Precedent	Constitution and the law'.
Precedent	It refers to the relevant provisions of the Constitution, to the decision in <CITATION_599701>  Suppl.
Precedent	1 S.C.R.
Precedent	439 and various subsequent decisions of this <ORG> and discusses the principles flowing from the said decisions.
Precedent	It notes that the subsequent decisions of this <ORG> in <CITATION_269704> ; <CITATION_27045> and <CITATION_492135> etc.
Precedent	show a marked shift from the original position taken in Balaji on several important points.
Precedent	In particular, it refers to the observations in Rajendran to the effect that "caste is also a class of citizens and if the class as a whole is socially and educationally backward, reservation can be made in favour of such a caste on the ground that it  was socially and educationally backward class of citizens within the meaning of Article 15(4)".
Precedent	It refers to the statement in <NAME> etc.
Precedent	v. State of Tamil Nadu , to the effect that "a caste has always been recognised as a class."
Precedent	It also commends the dissenting view of <NAME>, J. in <CITATION_1466728> , (wrongly referred to as <NAME>) - General Manager, <ORG> v. <NAME> .
Precedent	Chapter-VIII deals with 'North-South Comparison of other Backward Classes Welfare'.
Precedent	It is a case study of provisions in force in two Southern States namely Tamil Nadu and Karnataka and the two Northern States, Bihar and Uttar Pradesh.
Precedent	The conclusions drawn from the discussion are stated in para 8.45 in the following words:  "In view of the foregoing account, the reasons for much stronger reaction in the North than South to reservations, etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	for other Backward Classes may be summarised as below:-  (1) Tamil Nadu and Karnataka had a long history of Backward Classes movements and various measures for their welf are were taken in a phased manner.
RatioOfTheDecision	In Uttar Pradesh and Bihar such measures did not mark the culmination of a mass movement.
RatioOfTheDecision	(2) In the South "the forward communities have been divided either by the classification schemes or politically or both....
Precedent	In Bihar and U.P.
Precedent	the G.Os.
Precedent	have not divided the forward castes.
Precedent	(3) In the South, clashes between Scheduled Castes and Backward peasant castes have been rather mild.
Precedent	In the North these cleavages have been much sharper, often resulting in acts of violence.
Precedent	This has further weakened the backward classes solidarity in the North.
Precedent	(4) in the non-Sanskritic South, the basic Varna cleavage was between Brahmins and non-Brahmins and Brahmins constituted only about 3 per cent of the population.
Precedent	In the Sanskritic North, there was no sharp cleavage between the forward castes and together they constituted nearly 20 per cent of the population.
Precedent	In view of this the higher castes in U.P.
Precedent	and Bihar were in a stronger position to mobilise opposition to backward class movement.
Precedent	( 5) Owing to the longer history and better organisation of Other Backward castes in the South, they were able to acquire considerable political clout.
Precedent	Despite the lead given by the Yadavas and other peasant castes, a unified and strong OBC movement has not emerged in the North so far.
Precedent	(6) The traditions of semi-feudalism in Uttar Pradesh and Bihar have enabled the forward castes to keep tight control over smaller backward castes and prevent them from joining the mainstream of backward classes movement.
Precedent	This is not so in the south.
Precedent	(7) "The economies of Tamil Nadu and Karnataka have been expanding relatively faster.
Precedent	The private tertiary sector appears to be growing.
Precedent	It can shelter many forward caste youths.
Precedent	Also, they are prepared to migrate outside the State.
Precedent	The private tertiary sectors in Bihar and U.P.
Precedent	are stagnant.
Precedent	The forward caste youths in these two States have to depend heavily on <ORG> jobs.
Precedent	Driven to desperation, they have reacted violently."
RatioOfTheDecision	16.
RatioOfTheDecision	Chapter-IX sets out the evidence tendered by C entral and State Governments while Chapter-X deals with the evidence tendered by the Public.
RatioOfTheDecision	Chapter-XI is quite important inasmuch as it deals with the "Socio-Educational Field Survey and Criteria of Backwardness".
RatioOfTheDecision	In this Chapter, the <ORG> says that it decided to tap a of number of sources for the collection of data, keeping in mind the criticism against the Kaka Kalelkar <ORG> as also the several Judgments of this Court.
RatioOfTheDecision	It says that Socio-Educational Field Survey was the most comprehensive inquiry made by the <ORG> in this behalf.
RatioOfTheDecision	Right from the beginning, this Survey was designed with the help of top social scientists and specialists in the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Experts from a number of disciplines were associated with different phases of its progress.
RatioOfTheDecision	It refers to the work of <ORG> and the work done by a panel of experts led by Prof. <NAME>.
Precedent	It refers to the fact that both of them concurred that "in the Indian context such collectivities can be castes or other  hereditary groups traditionally associated with specific occupations which are considered to be low and impure and with which educational backwardness and low income are found to be associated."
Precedent	The <ORG> says further that with a view to providing continuous guidance at the operational level, <ORG> was set up under Dr. <NAME>.
Precedent	Director General, <ORG> with the Chief Executive, <ORG> and representatives of Directors of <ORG> as Members.
Precedent	The <ORG> sets out the Methodology evolved by the Experts' panel and states that survey operations were entrusted to <ORG> of the concerned States/Union Territories.
Precedent	It refers to the training imparted to the survey staff and to the fact that the entire data so collected was fed into a computer for electronic processing of such data.
Precedent	Out of the 406 districts in the country, the survey covered 405 districts.
Precedent	In every distri ct, two villages and one urban block was selected and in each of these villages and urban blocks, every single household was surveyed.
Precedent	The entire data collected was tabulated with the aid and <ORG> center of <ORG>.
Precedent	<ORG> constituted <ORG> to help the <ORG> prepare "Indicators of Backwardness" for analysing the data contained in the computerised tables.
Precedent	In para 11.23 (page 52) the <ORG> sets out the eleven Indicators/Criteria evolved by it for determining social and educational backwardness.
Precedent	Paras 11.23, 11.24 and 11.25 are relevant and may be set out in full:-  11.23.
Fact	As a result of the above exercise, the <ORG> evolved eleven 'Indicators' or 'criteria' for determining social and educational backwardness.
Fact	These 11 'Indicators' were grouped under three broad heads, i.e., Social, Educational and Economic.
Fact	They are:-  A.
Fact	Social:  (i) Castes/Classes considered as socially backward by others.
Fact	(ii) Castes/Classes which m ainly depend on manual labour for their livelihood.
Fact	(iii) Castes/Classes where at least 25% females and 10% males above the state average get married at an age below 17 years in rural areas and at least 10% females and 5% males do so in urban areas.
Fact	(iv) Castes/Classes where participation of females in work is at least 25% above the <ORG> average.
Fact	B.
Fact	Educational:  (v) Castes/Classes where the number of children in the age group of 5-15 years who never attended school is at least 25% above the <ORG> average.
Fact	(vi) Castes/Classes where the rate of student drop-out in the age group of 5-15 years is at least 25% above the <ORG> average.
RulingByLowerCourt	(vii) Castes/Classes amongst whom the proportion of matriculates is at least 25% below the <ORG> average.
RulingByLowerCourt	C. Economic:  (viii) Castes/Classes where the average value of family assets is at least 25% below the <ORG> average.
RulingByLowerCourt	(ix) Castes/Classes where the number of families living in Kuccha houses is at least 25% above the <ORG> average.
RulingByLowerCourt	(x) Castes/Classes where the source of  drinking water is beyond half a kilometer for more than 50% of the households.
Fact	(xi) Castes/Classes where the number of households having taken consumption loan is at least 25% above the <ORG> average.
Fact	11.24.
Fact	As the above three groups are not of equal importance for our purpose, separate weightage was given to 'Indicators' in each group.
Fact	All the Social 'Indicators' were given a weightage of 3 points each.
Fact	Educational 'Indicators' a weightage of 2 points each and Economic 'Indicators' a weightage of one point each.
Fact	Economic, in addition to Social and Educational Indicators, were considered important as they directly flowed from social and educational backwardness.
Fact	This also helped to highlight the fact that socially and educationally backward classes are economically backward also.
Fact	11.25.
Fact	It will be seen that from the values given to each Indicators, the total score adds upto 22.
RulingByLowerCourt	All these 11 Indicators were applied to all the castes covered by the survey for a particular <ORG>.
RulingByLowerCourt	As a result of this applicat ion, all castes which had a score of 50 percent (i.e., 11 points) or above were listed as socially and educationally backward and the rest were treated as 'advanced'.
RulingByLowerCourt	(It is a sheer coincidence that the number of indicators and minimum point score for backwardness, both happen to be eleven).
RulingByLowerCourt	Further, in case the number of households covered by the survey for any particular caste were below 20, it was left out of consideration, as the sample was considered too small for any dependable inference.
RulingByLowerCourt	It will also be useful to set out the observations of the <ORG> in para 11.27:-  11.27.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the end it may be emphasised that this survey has no pretentions to being a piece of academic research.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has been conducted by the administrative machinery of the <ORG> and used as a rough and ready tool for evolving a set of simple criteria for identifying social and educational backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	Throughout this survey our approach has been conditioned by practical considerations, realities of field conditions, constrai nts of resources and trained manpower and paucity of time.
RatioOfTheDecision	All these factors obviously militate against the requirements of a technically sophisticated and academically satisfying operation.

Fact	Issues for consideration:  26.
Fact	These writ petitions were heard in the first instance by <ORG> presided over by the then Chief Justice <NAME>.
Fact	After hearing them for some them, <ORG> referred them to a Specia l <ORG> of Nine Judges, "to finally settle the legal position relating to reservations."
RulingByLowerCourt	The reason for the reference being, "that the several Judgments of this Court have, not spoken in the same voice on this issue and a final look by a larger <ORG> in our opinion should settle the law in an authoritative way.
RulingByLowerCourt	We have, accordingly, heard all the parties and interveners who wished to be heard in the matter.
Fact	Written submissions have been filed by almost all the parties and intervenOrs.
Fact	Together, they run into several hundreds of pages.
Fact	At the inception of arguments, counsel for both sides put their heads together and framed eight questions arising for our discussion.
Fact	They read as follows:  (1) Whether Article 16(4) is an exception to Article 16(1) and would be exhaustive of the right to reservation to posts in services under the <ORG>?
Fact	(II) What would be the content of the phrase Backward Class in Article 16(4) of the Constitution and whether caste by itself could constitute a class and whether economic cri terion by itself could identify a class for Article 16(4) and whether backward Classes in Article 16(4) would include the Article 46 as well?
Fact	(III) If economic criterion by itself could not constitute a Backward Classes under Article 16(4) whether reservation of posts in services under the <ORG> based exclusively on economic criteria would be covered by Article 16(1) of the Constitution?
Fact	(IV) Can the extent of reservation to posts in the services under the <ORG> under Article 16(4) or, if permitted under Articles 16(1) and 16(4) together, exceed 50% of the posts in a cadre or Service under the <ORG> or exceed 50% of the appointment in a cadre or Service in any particular year and can such extent of reservation be determined without determining the inadequacy of representation of each class in the different categories and grades of Services under the <ORG>?
Fact	(V) Does Article 16(4) permit the classification of 'Backward Classes' into Backward Classes and Most Backward Classes or permit Classification among th em based on economic or other considerations?
Fact	(VI) Would making "any provision" under Article 16(4) for reservation "by the <ORG>" necessarily have to be by law made by the Legislatures of the <ORG> or by law made by <ORG>?
Fact	Or could such provisions be made by an executive order?
Fact	(VII) Will the extent of judicial review be limited or restricted in regard to the identification of Backward Classes and the percentage of reservations made for such classes, to a demonstrably perverse identification or a demonstrably unreasonable percentage?
Fact	(VIII) Would reservation of appointments or posts "in favour of any Backward Class" be restricted to the initial appointment to the post or would it extend to promotions as well?
RatioOfTheDecision	For the sake of convenient discussion and in the interest of clarity, we found it necessary to elaborate them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Accordingly, we have re-framed the questions.
RatioOfTheDecision	We shall proceed to answer them in the same order.
Fact	The reframed questions are:  1(a) Whether the 'provision' contemplated by Article 16(4)  must necessarily be made by the legislative wing of the <ORG>?
Fact	(b) If the answer to Clause (a) is in the negative, whether an executive order making such a provision is enforceable without incorporating it into a rule made under the proviso to Article 309?
Fact	2(a) Whether Clause (4) of Article 16 is an exception to Clause (1) of Article 16?
Fact	(b) Whether Clause (4) of Article 16 is exhaustive of the special provisions that can be made in favour of 'backward class of citizens'?
Fact	Whether it is exhaustive of the special provisions that can be made in favour of all sections, classes or groups?
Fact	(c) Whether reservations can be made under Clause (1) of Article 16 or whether it permits only extending of preferences/concessions?
Fact	3(a) What does the expression 'backward class of citizens' in Article 16(4) means?
Fact	(b) Whether backward classes can be identified on the basis and with reference to caste alone?
Fact	(c) Whether a class, to be designated as a backward class, should be situated similarly to the S.Cs./S.Ts.?
Fact	(d)  Whether the 'means' test can be applied in the course of identification of backward classes?
Fact	And if the answer is yes, whether providing such a test is obligatory?

Precedent	26A.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before we proceed to deal with the question, we may be permitted to  make a few observations: The questions arising herein are not only of great moment and consequence, they are also extremely delicate and sensitive.
RatioOfTheDecision	They represent complex problems of <ORG>, wrapped and presented to us as constitutional and legal questions.
RatioOfTheDecision	On some of these questions, the decisions of this <ORG> have not been uniform.
RatioOfTheDecision	They speak with more than one voice.
RatioOfTheDecision	Several opposing points of view have been pressed upon us with equal force and passion and quite often with great emotion.
RatioOfTheDecision	We recognize that these view-points are held genuinely by the respective exponents.
RatioOfTheDecision	Each of them feels his own point of view is the only right one.
RatioOfTheDecision	We cannot, however, agree with all of them.
RatioOfTheDecision	We have to find and we have tried our best to find - answers which according to us are the right ones constitutionally and legally.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though, we are sitting in a larger <ORG>, we have kept in mind the relevance and significance of the principle of Stare, decisis.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are conscious of the fact that in law certainty, consistency a nd continuity are highly desirable features.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where a decision has stood the test of time and has never been doubted, we have respected it unless, of course, there are compelling and strong reasons to depart from it.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where, however, such uniformity is not found, we have tried to answer the question on principle keeping in mind the scheme and goal of our Constitution and the material placed before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are occasions when the obvious needs to be stated and, we think, this is one such occasion.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are dealing with complex social, constitutional and legal questions upon which there has been a sharp division of opinion in the <ORG>, which could have been settled more satisfactorily through political processes.
RatioOfTheDecision	But that was not to be.
RatioOfTheDecision	The issues have been relegated to the judiciary - Which shows both the disinclination of the executive to grapple with these sensitive issues as also the confidence reposed in this organ of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are reminded of what Sir <NAME>, Chief Justice of Australia once sa id:  Society exhibits more signs of conflict and disagreement today than it did before....
Precedent	Governments have always had the option of leaving questions to be determined by the courts according to law....
Precedent	There are other reasons, of course - that cause governments to leave decisions to be made by <ORG>.
Precedent	They are of expedient political character.
Precedent	The community may be so divided on a particular issue that a government feels that the safe course for it to pursue is to leave the issue to be resolved by the <ORG>, thereby diminishing the risk it will alienate significant sections of the Community.
Precedent	But then answering a question as to the legitimacy of the <ORG> to decide such crucial issues, the learned Chief Justice says:  ....my own feeling is that the people accept the <ORG> as the appropriate means of resolving disputes when governments decide not to attempt to solve the disputes by the political process.
Precedent	(Judging the World: Law and Politics in the Worlds Leading <ORG> - page 343) We hope and trust that ou r people too are mature enough to appreciate our endeavour in the same spirit.
Precedent	They may well remember that "the law is not an abstract concept removed from the society it serves, and that Judges, as safe-guarders of the Constitution, must constantly strive to narrow the gap between the ideal of equal justice and the reality of social inequality."
Precedent	PART - II Before we proceed to answer the questions aforementioned, it would be helpful to notice (a) the debates in <ORG> on Article 16 (draft Article 10 ); (b) the decisions of this <ORG> on Articles 16 and 15; and (c) a few decisions of the U.S.Supreme <ORG> considering the validity of race-conscious programmes.
Precedent	The Framing of Article 16: Debates in <ORG>  25.
Precedent	Draft Article 10 corresponds to Article 16.
Precedent	The debate in <ORG> on draft Article 10 and particularly Clause (3), thereof [corresponding to Clause (4) of Article 16] helps us to appreciate the background and understand the objective underlying Article  16 , and in particular, Clause (4) thereof.
RatioOfTheDecision	The original intent comes out clear and loud from these debates.
RulingByPresentCourt	Omitting draft Clause (4) [which corresponds to Clause (5) of Article 16] the three clauses in draft Article 10 , as introduced in <ORG>, read as follows:  10(1).
RulingByPresentCourt	There shall be equality of opportunity for all citizens in matters of employment under the <ORG>.
RulingByPresentCourt	(2) No citizen shall, on grounds only of religion, race, caste, sex, descent, place of birth or any of them by ineligible for any office under the <ORG>.
RulingByPresentCourt	(3) Nothing in this article shall prevent the <ORG> from making any provision for the reservation of appointments or posts in favour of any class of citizens who in the opinion of the <ORG> are not adequately represented in the services under the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was <ORG> under the Chairmanship of Dr. <NAME> that inserted the word "backward" in between the words "in favour of any" and 'class of citizens".
RatioOfTheDecision	The discussion on draft Article 10 took place on  November 30, 1948.
RatioOfTheDecision	Several members including S/Sri <NAME>, Pt.
Argument	<NAME> and <NAME> complained that the expressions 'backward' and 'backward classes' are quite vague and are likely to lead to complications in future.
Argument	They suggested that appointments to public services should be made purely on the basis of merit.
Argument	Some others suggested that such reservations should be available only for a period of first ten years of the Coustitution.
Fact	To this criticism the Vice-President of the <ORG> (Dr. <NAME>) replied in the following words:  Before we start the general discussion, i would like to place a particular matter before the Honourable Members.
Fact	The clause which has so long been under discussion affects particularly certain sections of our population sections which have in the past been treated very cruelly and although we are today prepared to make reparation for the evil deeds of our ancestors, still the old story continues, at least here and there, and capital is made out of  it outside India....
Precedent	I would therefore very much appreciate the permission of the <ORG> so that I might give full discussion on this particular matter to our brethren of the backward classes.
Precedent	Do I have that permission?
Precedent	26.
Precedent	In the ensuing discussion <NAME> (Bihar-General) supported draft Clause (3) with great passion.
Precedent	He pleaded for reservations in favour of Backward Classes both in services as well as in the legislature, just as in the case of Harijans.
Precedent	<NAME> was supported by another Member Sri <NAME> (Madras-General) and Sri <NAME> (Mysore), Sri <NAME>, in particular, commented upon the Members coming from Northern India being puzzled about the meaning of the expression 'backward class' and proceeded to clarify the same in the following words:-  The backward classes of people as understood in South India, are those classes of people who are educationally backward, it is those classes that require adequate representation in the services.
Precedent	There are other classes of people who  are socially backward; they also require adequate representation in the service.
Precedent	27.
Precedent	After the discussion proceeded for some more time, Sri K.M.Munshi, who was a Member of <ORG> rose to explain the content of the word 'backward'.
Precedent	He said:-  What we want to secure by this clause are two things.
Precedent	In the fundamental right in the first clause we want to achieve the highest efficiency in the services of the <ORG>-highest efficiency which would enable the services to function effectively and promptly.
Precedent	At the same time, in view of the conditions in our country prevailing in several provinces, we want to see that backward classes, classes who are really backward, should be given scope in the <ORG> services; for it is realised that <ORG> services give a status and an opportunity to serve the country, and this opportunity should be extended to every community, even among the backward people.
Precedent	That being so, we have to find out some generic term and the word "backward class" was the best possible term.
Precedent	<NAME> proceeded to state:  I may point out that in the province of Bombay for several years now, there has been a definition of backward classes, which includes not only Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes but also other backward classes who are economically, educationally and socially backward.
RatioOfTheDecision	We need not, therefore, define or restrict the scope of the word "backward" to a particular community.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whoever is backward will be covered by it and I think the apprehensions of the Honourable Members are not justified.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ultimately Dr. <NAME>, the Chairman of <ORG>, got up to clarify the matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	His speech, which put an end to all discussion and led to adopting of draft Article 10(3 ), is worth quoting in extenso, since it throws light on several questions relevant herein:  ...there are three points of view which it is necessary for us to reconcile if we are to produce a workable proposition which will be accepted by all.
RatioOfTheDecision	Of the three points of view, the first is that there shall be equal ity of opportunity for all citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is the desire of many Members of this <ORG> that every individual who is qualified for a particular post should be free to apply for that post, to sit for examinations and to have his qualifications tested so as to determine whether he is fit for the post or not and that there ought to be no limitations, there ought to be no hindrance in the operation of this principle of equality or opportunity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Another view mostly shared by a section of the <ORG> is that, if this principle is to be operative-and it ought to be operative in their judgment to its fullest extent-there ought to be no reservations of any sort for any class or community at all, that all citizens, if they are qualified, should be placed on the same footing of equality so far as the public services are concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is the second point of view we have.
RatioOfTheDecision	Then we have quite a massive opinion which insists that, although theoretically it is good to have the principle that there shall be equality of opportunity,  there must at the same time be a provision made for the entry of certain communities which have so far been outside the administration.
RatioOfTheDecision	As I said, <ORG> had to produce a formula which would reconcile these three points of view, firstly, that there shall be equality of opportunity, secondly that there shall be reservations in favour of certain communities which have not so far had a 'proper look-in' so to say into the administration.
RatioOfTheDecision	If honourable Members will bear these facts in mind-the-three principles we had to reconcile,-they will see that no better formula could be produced than the one that is embodies in Sub-clause (3) of Article 10 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a generic principle.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time, as I said, we had to reconcile this formula with the demand made by certain communities that the administration which has now-for historical reasons-been controlled by one community or a few communites, that situation should disappear and that the others also must have an opportunity of gett ing into the public services.
RatioOfTheDecision	Supposing, for instance, we were to concede in full the demand of those communities who have not been so far employed in the public service to the fullest extent, what would really happen is, we shall be completely destroying the first proposition upon which we are all agreed, namely, that there shall be an equality of opportunity.
RatioOfTheDecision	Let me give an illustration.
RatioOfTheDecision	Supposing, for instance, reservations were made for a community or a collection of communities, the total of which came to something like 70 per cent of the total posts under the <ORG> and only 30 per cent are retained as the unreserved.
RatioOfTheDecision	Could anybody say that the reservation of 30 per cent as open to general competition would be satisfactory from the point of view of giving effect to the first principle, namely, that there shall be equality of opportunity?
RatioOfTheDecision	It cannot be in my judgment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore the seats to be reserved, if the reservation is to be consistent with Sub-clause (1) of Article 10 , must be confined to a minorit y of seats.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is then only that the first principle could find its place in the Constitution and effective in operation.
RatioOfTheDecision	If honourable Members understand this position that we have to safeguard two things, namely, the principle of equality of opportunity and at the same time satisfy the demand of communities which have not had so far representation in the <ORG>, then, I am sure they will agree that unless you use some such qualifying phrase as "backward" the exception made in favour of reservation will ultimately eat up the rule altogether.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nothing of the rule will remain.
RatioOfTheDecision	That I think if I may say so, is the justification why <ORG> undertook on its own shoulders the responsibility of introducing the word "backward" which, I admit, did not originally find a place in the fundamental right in the way in which it was passed by this <ORG>....  Somebody asked me: "What is a backward community"?
RatioOfTheDecision	Well, I think any one who reads the language of the draft itself will find that we have left it to b e determined by each local Government.
RatioOfTheDecision	A backward community is a community which is backward in the opinion of the Government.
RatioOfTheDecision	The above material makes it amply clear that the objective behind Clause (4) of Article 16 was the sharing of <ORG> power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> power which was almost exclusively monopolised by the upper castes i.e., a few communities, was now sought to be made broad-based.
RatioOfTheDecision	The backward communities who were till then kept out of apparatus of power, were sought to be inducted there into and since that was not practicable in the normal course, a special provision was made to effectuate the said objective.
RatioOfTheDecision	In short, the objective behind Article 16(4) is empowerment of the deprived backward communities - to give them a share in the administrative apparatus and in the governance of the community.
RatioOfTheDecision	Decisions of this Court on Articles 16 and 15:  28.
Precedent	Soon after the enforcement of the Constitution two cases reached this Court from the <ORG> of Madras - one under Article 15 and the other under Article 1 6.
Precedent	Both the cases were decided on the same date and by the same Bench.
Precedent	The one arising under Article 15 is <CITATION_149321>  S.C.R.
Precedent	525, and the other arising under Article 16 is <CITATION_1266374> A.I.R.
Precedent	1951 S.C. 229.
Fact	By virtue of certain orders issued prior to coming into force of the Constitution,-popularly known as 'Communal G.O.'
Fact	- seats in <ORG> in the State of Madras were apportioned in the following manner: Non-Brahmin (Hindus)-6, Backward Hindus-2, Brahmin-2, Harijan-2, Anglo Indians and Indian Christians-1, Muslims-1.
Precedent	Even after the advent of the Constitution, the G.O.
Precedent	was being acted upon which was challenged by Smt.
Precedent	<NAME> as violative of the fundamental rights guranteed to her by Articles 15(1) and 29(2) of the Constitution of India.
Precedent	A Full Bench of <ORG> declared the said G.O.
Precedent	as void and un-enforceable with the advent of the Constitution.
Precedent	The State of Madras brought the matter in appeal to this Court.
Precedent	A Special Bench of Seven Judges heard the matt er and came to the unanimous conclusion that the allocation of seats in the manner aforesaid is violative of Articles 15(1) and 29(2) inasmuch as the refusal to admit the respondent (writ petitioner) notwithstanding her higher marks, was based only on the ground of caste.
Precedent	The <ORG> of Madras sought to sustain the G.O.
Precedent	with reference to Article 46 of the Constitution.
Precedent	Indeed the argument was that Article 46 over-rides Article 29(2).
Precedent	This argument was rejected.
Precedent	The Court pointed out that while in the case of employment under the <ORG>, Clause (4) of Article 16 provides for reservations in favour of backward class of citizens, no such provision was made in Article 15.
Precedent	29.
Precedent	In the matter of appointment to public services too, a similar communal G.O.
Precedent	was in force in the <ORG> of Madras since prior to the Constitution.
Precedent	In December, 1949, <ORG> invited applications for 83 posts of District Munsifs, specifying at the same time that the selection of the candidates would be made from the  various castes, religions and communities as specified in the communal G.C.
Precedent	The 83 vacancies were distributed in the following manner: Harijans-19, Muslims-5, Christians-6, Backward Hindus-10, Non-Brahmin (Hindus)-32 and Brahmins-11.
Precedent	The petitioner <NAME> (it was a petition under Article 32 of the Constitution) applied for and appeared at the interview and the admitted position was that if the provisions of the communal G.O.
Precedent	were to be disregarded, he would have been selected.
Precedent	Because of the <ORG>., he was not selected (he belonged to Brahmin community).
Precedent	Whereupon he approached this Court.
Precedent	<NAME> speaking for the Special Bench referred to Article 16 and in particular to Clause (4) thereof and observed: "Reservation of posts in favour of any backward class of citizens cannot, therefore, be regarded as unconstitutional".
Precedent	He proceeded to hold:  <ORG> itself makes an express reservation of seats for Harijans & Backward Hindus.
Precedent	The other categories, namely, Muslims, Christians, Non-Brahmin Hin dus & Brahmins must be taken to have been treated as other than Harijans & Backward Hindus.
Precedent	Our attention was drawn to a schedule of Backward Classes set out in Schedule III to Part I of the Madras Provincial & Subordinate Service Rules.
Precedent	It was, therefore, argued that Backward Hindus would mean Hindus of any of the communities mentioned in that Schedule.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is, in the circumstances, impossible to say that classes of people other than Harijans & Backward Hindus can be called Backward Classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	As regards the posts reserved for Harijans & Backward Hindus it may be said that the petitioner who does not belong to those two classes is regarded as ineligible for those reserved posts not on the ground of religion, race, caste etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	but because of the necessity for making a provision for reservation of such posts in favour of a backward class of citizens, but the ineligibility of the petitioner for any of the posts reserved for communities other than Harijans and Backward Hindus cannot but be regarded as founded on the  ground only of his being a Brahmin.
RatioOfTheDecision	For instance, the petitioner may be far better qualified than a Muslim or a Christian or a Non-Brahmin candidate & if all the posts reserved for those communities were open to him he would be eligible for appointment, as is conceded by the learned Advocate General of Madras, but, nevertheless, he cannot expect to get any of those posts reserved for those different categories only because he happens to be a Brahmin.
RatioOfTheDecision	His ineligibility for any of the posts reserved for the other communities, although he may have far better qualifications than those possessed by members falling within those categories, is brought about only because he is a Brahmin & does not belong to any of those categories.
RatioOfTheDecision	This ineligibility created by <ORG> does not appear to us to be sanctioned by Clause (4) of Article 16 and it is an infringement of the fundamental right guaranteed to the petnr.
RatioOfTheDecision	as an individual citizen under Article 16(1)  & (2).
Precedent	<ORG>, in our opinion, is repugna nt to the provisions of Article 16 & is as such void and illegal.
Precedent	30.
Precedent	<NAME>, the learned Counsel appearing for the Respondent-State of Bihar placed strong reliance on the above passage.
Precedent	He placed before us an extract of the Schedule of the backward classes appended to the Madras Provincial and Subordinate Service Rules, 1942.
Precedent	He pointed out that Clause (3)(a) in Rule 2 defined the expression backward classes to mean "the communities mentioned in Schedule III to this part", and that Schedule III is exclusively based upon caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Schedule describes the communities mentioned therein under the heading 'Race, Tribe or Caste'.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is pointed out that when the said Schedule was substituted in 1947, the basis of classification still remained the caste, though the heading "Races, Tribes and Castes" was removed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Mr. <NAME> points out that <ORG> took note of the fact that Schedule III was nothing but a collection of certain 'communities', notified as backward classes and yet upheld the re servation in their favour.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to him, the decision in Venkataraman clearly supports the identification of backward classes on the basis of caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Communal G.O.
RatioOfTheDecision	was struck down, he submits, only in so far as it apportioned the remaining vacancies between sections other than Harijans and backward classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is rather curious, says the counsel, that the decision in Venkataraman has not attracted the importance it deserves all these years; All the subsequent decisions of this <ORG> refer to Champakam.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hardly any decision refers to Venkataraman notwithstanding the fact that Venkataraman was a decision rendered with reference to Article 16.
RatioOfTheDecision	31.
RatioOfTheDecision	Soon after the said two decisions were rendered the <ORG> intervened and in exercise of its constituent power, amended Article 15 by inserting Clause (4), which reads:  Nothing in this article or in Clause (2) of Article 29 shall prevent the <ORG> from making any special provision for the advancement of any socially and educationally backward classes of ci tizens or for the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is worthy of notice that the <ORG>, which enacted the first Amendment to the Constitution, was in fact the very same <ORG> which had framed the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The speech of Dr. <NAME> on the occasion is again instructive.
Precedent	He said:-  Then with regard to Article 16 , Clause (4), my submission is this that it is really impossible to make any reservation which would not result in excluding somebody who has a caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	I think it has to be borne in mind and it is one of the fundamental principles which I believe is stated in <NAME>'s edition on the very first page that there is no Hindu who has not a caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every Hindu has a caste-he is either a Brahmin or a <NAME> or a <NAME> or a Kumbhar or a carpenter.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no Hindu-that is the fundamental proposition-who has not a caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	Consequently, if you make a reservation in favour of what are called backward classes which are nothing else but a collection of certain castes, those who  are excluded are persons who belong to certain castes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, in the circumstances of this country, it is impossible to avoid reservation without excluding some people who have got a caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	After the enactment of the First Amendment the first case that came up before this Court is <CITATION_599701> .
Precedent	(In the year 1961, this Court decided the General Manager, <ORG> v. <NAME>, but that related to reservations in favour of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes in the matter of promotion in the <ORG>.
Precedent	<NAME> will be referred to at an appropriate stage later.)
Precedent	In the State of Karnataka, reservations were in force since a few decades prior to the advent of the Constitution and were being continued even thereafter.
Precedent	On July 26, 1958 the State of Mysore issued an order under Article 15(4) of the Constitution declaring all the communities excepting the Brahmin community as socially and educationally backward and reserving a total of 75 per cent seats in Educational Institutions in favour  of <ORG> and SCs/STs.
Precedent	Such orders were being issued every year, with minor variation in the percentage of reservations.
Precedent	On 13th of July, 1972, a similar order was issued wherein 68 per cent of the seats in all <ORG> in the State were reserved in the favour of the <ORG>, SCs and STs.
Precedent	<ORG> were again divided into two categories-backward classes and more backward classes.
Precedent	The validity of this order was questioned under Article 32 of the Constitution.
Precedent	While striking down the said order this Court enunciated the following principles:-  (1) Clause (4) of Article 15 is a proviso or an exception to Clause (1) of Article 15 and to Clause (2) of Article 29;   (2) For the purpose of Article 15(4 ), backwardness must be both social and educational.
Precedent	Though caste in relation to Hindus may be a relevant factor to consider, in determining the social backwardness of a class of citizens, it cannot be made the sole and dominant test.
Precedent	Christians, Jains and Muslims do not be lieve in caste system; the test of caste cannot be applied to them.
Precedent	Inasmuch as identification of all backward classes under the impugned order has been made solely on the basis of caste, it is bad.
Precedent	(3) The reservation made under Clause (4) of Article 15 should be reasonable.
Precedent	It should not be such as to defeat or nullify the main Rule of equality contained in Clause (1).
Precedent	While it is not possible to predicate the exact permissible percentage of reservations, it can be stated in a general and broad way that they should be less than 50 per cent.
Precedent	(4) A provision under Article 15(4) need not be in the form of legislation; it can be made by an executive order.
Precedent	(5) The further categorisation of backward classes into backward and more backward is not warranted by Article 15(4).
Precedent	It must be remembered that Balaji was a decision rendered under and with reference to Article 15 though it contains certain observations with respect to Article 16 as well.
Precedent	33.
Precedent	Soon after the decision in Balaji this Court was confronted  with a case arising under Article 16 -  <CITATION_1466728> .
Precedent	This was also a petition under Article 32 of the Constitution.
Precedent	It related to the validity of the 'carry-forward' rule obtaining in <ORG>.
Precedent	The reservation in favour of Scheduled Castes was twelve and half per cent while the reservation in favour of Scheduled Tribes was five per cent.
Precedent	The 'carry-forward' rule considered in the said decision was in the following terms: "If a sufficient number of candidates considered suitable by the recruiting authorities, are not available from the communities for whom reservations are made in a particular year, the unfilled vacancies should be treated as unreserved and filled by the best available candidates.
Precedent	The number of reserved vacancies, thus, treated as unreserved will be added as an additional quota to the number that would be reserved in the following year in the normal course; and to the extent to which approved candidates are not available in that year against this additional quotas, a  corresponding addition should be made to the number of reserved vacancies in the second following year."
Precedent	Because sufficient number of <ORG> candidates were not available during the earlier years the unfilled vacancies meant for them were carried forward as contemplated by the said rule and filled up in the third year - that is in the year 1961.
Precedent	Out of 45 appointments made, 29 went to Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes.
Precedent	In other words, the extent of reservation in the third year came to 65 per cent.
Precedent	The rule was declared unconstitutional by <ORG>, with <NAME> dissenting.
Precedent	The majority held that the carry forward rule which resulted in more than 50 per cent of the vacancies being reserved in a particular year, is bad.
Precedent	The principle enunciated in Balaji regarding 50 percent was followed.
Precedent	<NAME> in his dissenting opinion, however, upheld the said rule.
Precedent	The learned Judge observed: "The expression, "nothing in this article" is a legislative device to express its intention in a most  emphatic way that the power conferred thereunder is not limited in any way by the main provision but falls outside it.
Precedent	It has not really carved out an exception, but has preserved a power untrammelled by the other provisions of the Article."
Precedent	The learned Judge opined that once a class is a backward class, the question whether it is adequately represented or not is left to the subjective satisfaction of the <ORG> and is not a matter for this <ORG> to prescribe.
Precedent	We must, at this stage, clarify that a 'carry-forward' rule may be in a form different than the one considered in Devadasan.
Precedent	The Rule may provide that the vacancies reserved for Scheduled Castes or Scheduled Tribes shall not be filled up by general (open competition) candidates in case of non-availability of <ORG> candidates and that such vacancies shall be carried forward.
Precedent	34.
Precedent	In the year 1964 another case from Mysore arose, again under Article 15 -  <CITATION_203735> .
Precedent	<ORG> had by an order defined backward classes on the basis of oc cupation and income, unrelated to caste.
Precedent	Thirty per cent of seats in professional and technical institutions were reserved for them in addition to eighteen per cent in favour of SCs and STs.
Precedent	One of the arguments urged was that the identification done without taking the caste into consideration is impermissible.
Precedent	The majority speaking through <NAME>, held the identification or classification of backward classes on the basis of occupation-cum-income, without reference to caste, is not bad and does not offend Article 15(4).
Precedent	35.
Precedent	During the years 1968 to 1971, this Court had to consider the validity of identification of backward classes made by Madras and Andhra Pradesh Governments.
Precedent	<CITATION_1342109> related to specification of socially and educationally backward classes with reference to castes.
Precedent	The question was whether such an identification infringes Article 15.
