Precedent	<NAME>, <ORG>., speaking for <ORG> dealt with the contention in the following words:  The contention is that the list of social ly and educationally backward classes for whom reservation is made under Rule 5 nothing but a list of certain castes.
Precedent	Therefrore, reservation in favour of certain castes based only on caste considerations violates Article 15(1 ), which prohibits discrimination on the ground of caste only.
Precedent	Now if the reservation in question had been based only on caste and had not taken into account the social and educational backwardness of the caste in question, it would be violative of Article 15(1).
Precedent	But it must not be forgotten that a caste is also a class of citizens and if the caste as a whole is socially and educationally backward reservation can be made in favour of such a caste on the ground that it is a socially and educationally backward class of citizens within the meaning of Article 15(4)....
Precedent	It is true that in the present cases the list of socially and educationally backward classes has been specified by caste.
Precedent	But that does not necessarily mean that caste was the sole consideration and that person belonging to t hese castes are also not a class of socially and educationally backward citizens.... As it was found that members of these castes as a whole were educationally and socially backward, the list which had been coming on from as far back as 1906 was finally adopted for purposes of Article 15(4) In view however of the explanation given by the State of Madras, which has not been controverted by and rejoinder, it must be accepted that though the list shows certain castes, the members of those castes are really classes of educationally and socially backward citizens.
Precedent	No attempt was made on behalf of the petitioners/appellant to show that any caste mentioned in this list was not educationally and socially backward.
Precedent	In this state of the pleadings, we must come to the conclusion that though the list is prepared caste-wise, the castes included therein are as a whole educationally and socially backward and therefore the list is not violative of Article 15.
Precedent	The challenge to Rule 5 must therefore fail.
Precedent	36.
Precedent	The shift in app roach and emphasis is obvious.
Precedent	The Court now held that a caste is a class of citizens and that if a caste as a whole is socially and educationally backward, reservation can be made in favour of such a caste on the ground that it is a socially and educationally backward class of citizens within the meaning of Article 15(4).
Precedent	More over the burden of proving that the specification/identification was bad, was placed upon the petitioners.
Precedent	In case of failure to discharge that burden, the identification made by the <ORG> was upheld.
Precedent	The identification made on the basis of caste was upheld inasmuch as the petitioner failed to prove that any caste mentioned in the list was not socially and educationally backward.
Precedent	37.
Precedent	<ORG> took a similar view in Triloki Nath [1969] 1 S.C.R.
Precedent	103.
Precedent	<NAME> was expressly referred to and followed in <NAME> v. <ORG> of Tamil Nadu, a decision rendered by a Bench of three Judges (<NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.).
Precedent	This was a Petition under Article 32 of  the Constitution and one arising under Article 15.
Precedent	The argument was that identification of SEBCs having been done on the basis of caste alone is bad.
Precedent	Repelling the argument, <NAME>, J. held:-  There is no gainsaying the fact that there are numerous castes in this country which are socially and educationally backward.
Precedent	To ignore their existence is to ignore the facts of life.
Precedent	Hence, we are unable to uphold the contention that impugned reservation is not in accordance with Article 15(4).
Precedent	38.
Precedent	Again, in <CITATION_492135> , a case arising from Andhra Pradesh, <ORG> (<NAME> and <NAME>,JJ.)
Precedent	adopted the same approach and upheld the identification made by <ORG> on the basis of caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	Answering the criticism that <ORG> appointed by the State Government did not do a scientific and thorough job, the <ORG> observed:  In our opinion, the <ORG> has taken considerable pains to collect as much relevant material as possible to judge the social and educational  backwardness of the persons concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	When, for instance, it had called for information regarding the student population in classes X and XI from nearly 2224 institutions, if only 50% of the institutions sent replies, it is not the fault of the <ORG> for they could not get more particulars.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the commission has only to go on doing the work of collecting particulars and materials, it will be a never ending matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	In spite of best efforts that any commission may make in collecting materials and datas, its conclusions cannot be always scientifically accurate in such matters.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the proper approach, in our opinion should be to see whether the relevant data and materials referred to in the report of the <ORG> justify its conclusions.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our opinon, there was sufficient material to enable the <ORG> to be satisfied that the persons included in the list are really socially and educationally bakcward.
RatioOfTheDecision	No doubt there are few instances where the educational average is slightly above the <ORG> a verage, but that circumstances by itself is not enough to strike down the entire list.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even assuming there are few categories which are little above the <ORG> average, in literacy, that is a matter for the <ORG> to take note of and review the position of such categories of persons and take a suitable decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	We respectfully agree with these observations.
Precedent	Answering the main criticism that the list of SEBCs was wholly based upon caste, the <ORG> observed:-  To conclude, though prima facie the list of Backward Classes which is under attack before us may be considered to be on the basis of caste, a closer examination will clearly show that it is only a description of the group following the particular occupations or professions, exhaustively referred to by the <ORG>.
Precedent	Even on the assumption that the list is based exclusively on caste, it is clear from the materials before the <ORG> and the reasons given by it in its report that the entire caste is socially and educationally backward and therefore the ir inclusion in the list of Backward Classes is warranted by Article 15(4).
Precedent	The groups mentioned therein have been included in the list of Backward classes as they satisfy the various tests, which have been laid down by this Court for ascertaining the social and educational backwardness of a class.
Precedent	39.
Precedent	In certain cases including <CITATION_579569> and <CITATION_1734464> , it was held that poverty alone cannot be the basis for determining or identifying the social and educational backwardness.
Argument	It was emphasised that Article 15(4) - or for that matter Article 16(4) - is not an instance of poverty alleviation programme.
Argument	They were directed mainly towards removal of social and educational bachwardness, it was pointed out.
Argument	In <NAME>, a decision under Article 15(4 ), <NAME>,C.J.
Precedent	speaking for <ORG> of three Judges opined:  Broadly stated, neither caste nor race nor religion can be made the basis of classification for the purposes of determining social and educational backwardness within the meaning o f Article 15(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	When Article 15(1) forbids discrimination on grounds only of religion, race, caste, caste cannot be made one of the criteria for determining social and educational backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	If caste or religion is recognised as a criterion of social and educational backwardness Article 15(4) will stultify Article 15(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that Article 15(1) forbids discrimination only on the ground of religion, race, caste but when a classification taken recourse to caste as one of the criteria in determining socially and educationally backward classes the expression "classes" in that case violates the rule of expressio unions est exclusio alterius.
RatioOfTheDecision	The socially and educationally backward classes of citizens are groups other than groups based on caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	This statement was made without referring to the dicta in Rajendran, a decision of a larger <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Though <NAME> was referred to, we must point out with respect that <NAME> does not support the above statement.
Precedent	<NAME> indeed said that "though castes in relation t o Hindus may be a relevant factor to consider in determining the social backwardness of groups or classes of citizens, it cannot be made the sole or the dominant test in that behalf."
Precedent	40.
Precedent	<NAME> marks the beginning of a new thinking on Article 16 , though the seed of this thought is to be found in the dissenting opinion of <NAME> in Devadasan.
Precedent	<ORG> had, by amending <ORG> and Subordinate Service Rules empowered the <ORG> to exempt, by order, for a specified period, any member or members belonging to Scheduled Castes or Scheduled Tribes and already in service, from passing the test which an employee had to pass as a precondition for promotion to next higher post.
Precedent	Exercising the said power, the <ORG> of Kerala issued a notification granting "temporary exemption to members already in service belonging to any of the Scheduled Castes or Scheduled Tribes from passing all tests (unified, special or departmental test) for a period of two years".
Precedent	On the basis of the said exempti on, a large number of employees belonging to Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes, who had been stagnating in their respective posts for want of passing the departmental tests, were promoted.
Precedent	They were now required to pass the tests within the period of exemption.
Precedent	Out of 51 vacancies which arose in the category of <ORG> in the year 1972, 34 were filled up by members of Scheduled Castes leaving only 17 for others.
Precedent	This was questioned by <NAME>, a member belonging to non-reserved category.
Precedent	His grievance was: but for the said concession/exemption given to members of Scheduled Castes/Scheduled Tribes he would have been promoted to one of those posts in view of his passing the relevant tests.
Precedent	He contended that Article 16(4) permits only reservations in favour of backward classes but not such an exemption.
Precedent	This argument was accepted by <ORG>.
Precedent	It also upheld the further contention that inasmuch as more than 50% vacancies in the year had gone to the members of Scheduled Castes as a re sult of the said exemption, it is bed for violating the 50% rule in Balaji.
Precedent	<ORG> carried the matter in appeal to this Court which was allowed by a majority of 5:2.
Precedent	All the Seven Judges wrote separate opinions.
Precedent	The head-note to the decision in <ORG> succinctly sets out the principles enunciated in each of the judgments.
Precedent	We do not wish to burden this judgment by reproducing them here.
Precedent	We would rest content with delineating the broad features emerging from these opinions.
Precedent	<NAME>, CJ.
Precedent	held that Article 16(1 ), being a facet of Article 14 , permits reasonable classification.
Precedent	Article 16(4) clarifies and explains that classification on the basis of backwardness.
Precedent	Classification of Scheduled Castes does not fall within the mischief of Article 16(2) since Scheduled Castes historically oppressed and backward, are not castes.
Precedent	The concession granted to them is permissible under and legitimate for the purposes of Article 16(1).
Precedent	The rule giving preference to an un-represented or under-represented  backward community does not contravene Articles 14, 16(1) or 16(2).
Precedent	Any doubt on this score is removed by Article 16(4).
Precedent	He opined further that for determining whether a reservation is excessive or not one must have to look to the total number of posts in a given unit or department, as the case may be.
Precedent	<NAME>, J. agreed that Article 16(4) is not an exception to Article 16(1 ), that.
Precedent	Article 16(1) permits reasonable classification and that Scheduled Castes are not 'castes' within the meaning of Article 16(2).
Precedent	He espoused the theory of 'proportional equality' evolved in certain American decisions.
Argument	He does not refer to the decisions in Balaji or Devadasan in his opinion nor does he express any opinion the extent of permissible reservation.
Argument	<NAME>, J. adopted a different reasoning.
Argument	According to him, the rule and the orders issued thereunder was "a kind of reservation" falling under Article 16(4) itself.
Argument	<NAME> was also of the opinion that Article 16(1) being a facet of Article 16 permits reasonable classif ication, that Article 16(4) is not an exception but an emphatic statement of what is inherent in Article 16(1) and further that Scheduled Castes are not 'castes' within the meaning of Article 16(2) but a collection of castes, races and groups.
Argument	Article 16(4) is one made of reconciling the claims of backward people and the opportunity for free competition the forward sections are ordinarily entitled to, held the learned Judge.
Precedent	He approved the dissenting opinion of <NAME> in Devadasan.
Precedent	<NAME> too adopted a similar approach.
Precedent	The learned Judge pointed out "if we read Article 16(4) as an exception to Article 16(1) then the inescapable conclusion would be that Article 16(1) does not permit any classification at all because an express provision has been made for this in Clause (4).
Precedent	This, however, is contrary to the basic concept of equality contained in Article 14 which implicitly permits classification in any form provided certain conditions are fulfilled.
Precedent	Furthermore, if no classification can be made u nder Article 16(1) except reservation contained in Clause (4) then the mandate contained in Article 335 would be defeated."
Precedent	He held that the Rule and the orders impugned are referable to and sustainable under Article 16.
Precedent	The learned Judge went further and held that the rule of 50% evolved in <NAME> is a mere rule of caution and was not meant to be exhaustive of all categories.
Precedent	He expressed the opinion that the extent of reservation depends upon the proportion of the backward classes to the total population and their representation in public services.
Precedent	He expressed a doubt as to the correctness of the majority view in Devadasan.
Precedent	Among the minority Khanna, J. preferred the view taken in <NAME> and other cases to the effect that Article 16(4) is an exception to Article 16(1).
Precedent	He opined that no preference can be provided in favour of backward classes outside Clause (4).
Precedent	<NAME>, J. concurred with this view.
Precedent	41.
Precedent	The last decision of this <ORG> on this subject is in <CITATION_1759197>  Suppl.
Precedent	1 S.C.R.
Precedent	352.
Argument	<ORG> ve Judges constituting the <ORG> wrote separate opinions, each treading a path of his own.
Argument	<NAME>, C.J.
Argument	opined that the present reservations should continue for a further period of 15 years making a total of 50 years from the date of commencement of the Constitution.
Argument	He added that the means test must be applied to ensure that the benefit of reservations actually reaches the deserving sections.
Argument	<NAME>, J. was of the opinion that the only basis upon which backward classes should be identified is the economic one and that a time has come to discard all other bases.
Argument	<NAME> was of the; view that identification of backward classes on the basis of caste cannot be taken exception to for the reason that in the Indian context caste is a class.
Argument	Caste, the learned Judge said, is the primary index of social backwardness, so that social backwardness is often readily identificable with reference to a person's caste.
Argument	It it is found in the case of a given caste that a few members have progressed far enough so  as to compare favourably with the forward classes in social, economic and educational fields, an upper income ceiling can perhaps be prescribed to ensure that the benefit of reservation reaches the really deserving.
Argument	He opined that identification of SEBCs in the Indian milieu is a difficult and complex exercise, which does not admit of any rigid or universal tests.
Argument	It is not a matter for the courts.
Argument	The "backward class of citizens", he held, are the very same SEBCs referred to in Article 15(4).
Argument	The learned Judge condemned the argument that reservations are likely to lead to deterioration in efficiency or that they are anti-merit.
Argument	He disagreed with the view that for being identified as SEBCs, the relevant groups should be comparable to SCs/STs in social and educational backwardness.
RulingByLowerCourt	The learned Judge agreed with the opinion of <NAME>, J. in Thomas that the rule of 50% in Balaji is a rule of caution and not an inflexible rule.
RulingByLowerCourt	At any rate, he said, it is not for the court to lay down any such hard and fast r ule.
RulingByLowerCourt	<NAME> was of the opinion that the predominant and only factor for making special provision under Article 15(4) or 16(4) should be poverty and that caste should be used only for the purpose of identification of groups comparable to Scheduled Castes/Scheduled Tribes.
RulingByLowerCourt	The reservation should continue only till such time as the backward classes attain a state of enlightenment.
RulingByLowerCourt	<NAME>, J. agreed with <NAME> that identification of backward classes can be made on the basis of caste.
RulingByLowerCourt	He cited the <ORG> and <ORG> debates in support of this view.
RulingByLowerCourt	According to the learned Judge, equality of opportunity revolves around two dominant principles viz., (i) the traditional value of equality of opportunity and (ii) the newly appreciated - though not newly conceived idea of equality of results.
RulingByLowerCourt	He too did not agree with the argument of 'merit'.
Fact	Application of the principle of individual merit, un-mitigated by other consideration, may quite often lead to inhuman results, he poi nted out.
Fact	He supported the imposition of the 'means' test but disagreed with the view that the extent of reservations can exceed 50%.
Fact	Periodic review of this list of SEBCs and extention of other facilities to them is stressed.
Precedent	Decisions of <ORG>  42.
Precedent	At this stage, it would be interesting to notice the development of law on the subject in the U.S.A.
Precedent	The problem of blacks (Negroes) - holds a parallel to the problem of Scheduled Castes, Scheduled Tribes and Backward Classes in India, with this difference that in U.S.A. the problem is just about 200 years' old and far less complex.
Precedent	Blacks were held not entitled to be treated as citizens.
Precedent	They were the lawful property of their masters [Dred Scott v. Sanford [1857] 15 L.E.
Precedent	691].
Precedent	In spite of the Thirteenth Amendment abolishing slavery and the Fourteenth Amendment guaranteeing equality, it persisted in South and Mid-West for several decades.
Precedent	All challenges to slavery and apartheid failed in courts.
Precedent	World War II and its aftermath, however, brought about  a radical change in this situation, the culmination of which was the celebrated decisions in <CITATION_480016>  98 L.E.
Precedent	591 and <NAME> v. <NAME>  98 L.E.
Precedent	583 over-ruling the 'separate but equal' doctrine evolved in <NAME> v. <NAME>  41 L.E.
Precedent	256.
Precedent	In quick succession followed several decisions which effectively out-lowed all discrimination against blacks in all walks of life.
Precedent	But the ground-realities remained.
Precedent	Socially, educationally and economically, blacks remained a backward community.
Precedent	Centuries of discrimination, deprivation and degradation had left their mark.
Precedent	They were still unable to compete with their white counterparts.
Precedent	Similar was the case of other minorities like Indians and Hispanics.
Precedent	It was not a mere case of economics.
Precedent	It was really a case of 'persisting effects of past-descrimination'.
Precedent	The <ORG>, the <ORG> Universities and other organs of the <ORG> took note of these lingering effects and the consequent disadvantage suffered by them.
Precedent	They set out to initiate measures to ameliorate them.
Precedent	That was the command of the Fourteenth Amendment.
Precedent	Not unnaturally, these measures were challenged in Courts-with varying results.
Precedent	The four decisions examined hereinafter, rendered during the period 1974-1990 mirror the conflict and disclose the judicial thinking in that country.
Precedent	43.
Precedent	The first decision is in <NAME> v. <NAME> [1974] 40 L.Ed.
Precedent	2nd.
Precedent	164.
Precedent	<ORG> - a school operated by the <ORG> - evolved, in December 1973, an admissions policy whereunder certain percentage of seats in <ORG> were reserved for minority racial groups.
Precedent	Para 6 of the programme stated, "because certain ethnic groups in our society have historically been limited in their access to the legal profession and because the resulting under-representation can affect the quality of legal services available to members of such groups, as well as limit their opportunity for full participation in the governance of our communities, the faculty recognises a special obligation in its admissions policy  to contribute to the solution of the problem."
Precedent	(emphasis added) Procedure for admission for the minority students was different and of a lesser standard than the one adopted for all others.
Precedent	<NAME>, a non-minority student was denied admission while granting it to minority applicants with lower evaluation.
Precedent	He commenced an action challenging the validity of the programme.
Precedent	According to him, the special admissions programme was violative of the Equal Protection Clause in the Fourteenth Amendment.
Precedent	<ORG> granted the requested relief including admission to the plaintiff.
Precedent	On Appeal, <ORG> of Washington reversed <ORG> Judgment.
Precedent	It upheld the constitutionality of the Admissions Policy.
Precedent	The matter was brought by <NAME> to <ORG> by way of certiorari.
Precedent	The Judgment of <ORG> was stayed pending the decision.
Precedent	By the time the matter reached the stage of final hearing, <NAME> had arrived in the final quarter of the last term.
Precedent	In view of this circu mstance, five Members of the <ORG> held that the Constitutional question raised has become 'moot' (academic) and, therefore, it is unnecessary to go into the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	Four of the Judges <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ., however, did not agree with that view.
RatioOfTheDecision	Of them, only <NAME>, J. recorded his reasons for upholding the Special Admissions' Programme.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Judge was of the opinion that the Equal Protection Clause did not require that law schools employ an admissions formula based solely upon testing results and under-graduate grades nor does it prohibit Law Schools from evaluating an applicant's prior achievements in the light of the barriers that he had to overcome.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would be appropriate to quote certain observations of the learned Judge to the above affect which inter alia emphasise the importance of looking to the promise and potential of a candidate rather than to mere scores obtained in the relevant tests.
RatioOfTheDecision	He said:  the Equal Protection Clause did not enact a requirement that Law Schools  employ as the sole criterion for admissions a formula based upon the LSAT (Law School Admission Test) and under-graduate grades, nor does it prohibit law schools from evaluating an applicant's prior achievements in light of the barriers that he had to overcome.
RatioOfTheDecision	A black applicant who pulled himself out of the ghetto into a junior college may thereby demonstrate a level of motivation, perseverance and ability that would lead a fair-minded admissions committee to conclude that he shows more promise for law study than the son of a rich alumnus who achieved better grades at <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	That applicant would not be offered admission because he is black, but because as an individual he has shown he has the potential, while the <ORG> man may have taken less advantage of the vastly superior opportunities offered to him.
RatioOfTheDecision	Because of the weight of the prior handicaps, the black applicant may not realize his full potential in the first year of law school, or even in the full three years, but in the long pull of a legal care er, his achievements may far outstrip those of his classmates whose earlier records appeared superior by conventional criteria.
Precedent	The learned Judge while agreeing that any programme employing racial classification to favour certain minority groups would be subject to strict scrutiny under Equal Protection Clause, yet concluded that the material placed before the <ORG> did not establish that <NAME> was invidiously discriminated against because of his race.
Precedent	Accordingly, he opined that the matter should be remanded for fresh trial to consider whether the plaintiff has been individually discriminated against because of his race.
Precedent	44.
Precedent	The next case is in <ORG> v. <NAME> [1978] 57 L.Ed.
Precedent	2nd 750.
Precedent	<ORG> of <ORG> had been following two admissions programmes, one in respect of the 84 seats (general) and the other, a special admissions programme under which only disadvantaged members of certain minority races were considered for the rema ining 16 seats - the total seats available being 100 a year.
Precedent	For these 16 seats, none except the members of the minority races were considered and evaluated.
Precedent	The respondent, <NAME>, a white, could not obtain admission for two consecutive years, in view of his evaluation scores, while admission was given to members of minority races who had obtained lesser scores than him.
Precedent	He questioned the validity of special admissions programme on the ground that it violated the equal protection clause in the Fourteenth Amendment to the Constitution and also Title VI of the Civil Rights Act.
Precedent	1964.
Precedent	<ORG> upheld the plea on the ground that the programme excluded members of non-minority races from the 16 reserved seats only on the basis of race and thus operated as a racial quota.
Precedent	It, however, refused to direct the plaintiff to be admitted inasmuch as he failed to establish that he would have been admitted but for the existence of the special admissions programme.
Precedent	The matter was carried in direct appeal to <ORG> rt of California, which not only affirmed <ORG> Judgment in so far as it held the special admission programme to be invalid but also granted admission to the plaintiff-respondent into <ORG>.
Precedent	It was of the view that the <ORG> had failed to prove that in the absence of special admissions programme the respondent would not have been admitted.
Precedent	The matter was then carried to <ORG>, where three distinct view-points emerged.
Precedent	<NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	were of the opinion that the special admissions programme was a valid one and is not violative of the Federal or State Constitutions or of Title VI of the Civil Rights Act, 1964.
Precedent	They were of the opinion that the purpose of overcoming substantial, chronic minority under-representation in the medical profession is sufficiently important to justify the <ORG>'s remedial use of race.
Precedent	Since the Judgment of the Supreme Court of California prohibited the use of race as a factor in <ORG> admissio ns, they reversed that Judgment.
Precedent	Chief Justice <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	took the other view.
Precedent	They affirmed the judgment of <ORG>.
Precedent	They based their judgment mainly on Title VI of Civile Rights Act, 1964, which provided that "no person in the United States shall, on the ground of race, colour or national origin, be excluded from participation in, be denied the benefits of or be subjected to discrimination under any programme or activity receiving Federal Financial assistance."
Argument	They opined that <NAME> was the victim of, what may be called, reverse discrimination and that his exclusion from consideration in respect of the 16 seats being solely based on race, is impermissible.
Argument	<NAME>, J. took the third view in his separate opinion, partly agreeing and partly disagreeing with the other view-points.
Argument	He based his decision on Fourteenth Amendment alone.
Argument	He did not take into consideration the 1964 Act.
Argument	The learned Judge held that though racial and ethnic classifications o f any kind are inherently suspect and call for the most exacting judicial scrutiny, the goal of achieving a racially balanced student body is sufficiently compelling to justify consideration of race in admissions decisions under certain circumstances.
Argument	He was of the opinion that while preference can be provided in favour of minority races in the matter of admission, setting up of quotas (which have the effect of foreclosing consideration of all others in respect thereof) is not necessary for achieving the said compelling goal.
Argument	He was of the opinion that impugned programme is bad since it set apart a quota for minority races.
Argument	He sustained the admission granted to <NAME> on the ground that the <ORG> failed to establish that even without the quota, he would not have been admitted.
Precedent	45.
Precedent	It would be useful to notice the three points of view in a little more detail.
Precedent	<NAME>, J.
Precedent	(with whom <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	agreed) observed that though the U.S. Constitution was founded on the principle that "all m en are created equal", the truth is that it is not so in fact.
RatioOfTheDecision	Racial discrimination still persists in the society.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such a situation the claim that the law must be "colour-blind" is more an aspiration rather than a description of reality.
RatioOfTheDecision	The context and the reasons for which Title VI of the Civil Rights Act, 1964 was enacted leads to the conclusion that the prohibition contained in Title VI was intended to be consistent with the commands of the Constitution and no more.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, "any claim that the use of racial criteria is barred by the plain language of the statute must fail in light of the remedial purpose of Title VI and its legislative history."
RatioOfTheDecision	On the contrary, said the learned Judge, prior decisions of the court strongly suggest that Title VI does not prohibit the remedial use of race where such action is constitutionally permissible.
Precedent	Dealing with the equal protection clause in the Fourteenth Amendment, the learned Judge observed:  The assertion of human equality is closely associated with the p roposition that differences in colour or creed, birth or status, are neither significant nor relevant to the way in which person should be treated.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nonetheless, the position that such factors must be "constitutionally an irrelevance" summed up by the shorthand phrase "our Constitution is colour-blind" has never been adopted by this <ORG> as the proper meaning of the Equal Protection clause.
RatioOfTheDecision	We conclude, therefore, that racial classifications are not per se invalid under the Fourtheenth Amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	Accordingly, we turn to the problem of articulating what our role should be in reviewing state action that expressly classifies by race.
RatioOfTheDecision	(emphasis added) After examining a large number of decided cases, the learned Judge held:  The conclusion that state educational institutions may constitutionally adopt admissions programs designed to avoid exclusion of historically disadvantaged minorities, even when such programs explicitly take race into account, finds direct support in our cases construing congressional legisla tion designed to overcome the present effects of past discrimination.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, held the learned Judge, failure to take race into account to remedy unequal access to <ORG> programs caused by their own or by past societal discrimination would not be consistent with the mandate of the Fourteenth Amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The special admissions programme whereunder whites are excluded from the 16 reserved seats is not bad for the reason that "its purpose is to overcome the effects of segregation by bringing races together."
Precedent	The learned Judge then pointed out the relevance of race and the lesser impact of economic disadvantage, with reference to certain facts and figures, and concluded:  While race is positively correlated with differences in GPA and <ORG> scores, economic disadvantage is not.
Precedent	Thus, it appears that economically disadvantaged whites do not score less well than economically advantaged whites while economically advantaged blacks score less well than do disadvantaged whites.
Precedent	46.
Argument	<NAME>,CJ., with whom <NAME> <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Argument	agreed opined that since in respect of 16 seats reserved for racial minorities, whites are totally excluded only on the basis of their race, it is a clear case of discrimination on the basis of race and, therefore, violative of the Fourteenth Amendment to the Constitution as well as Title VI of the Civil Rights Act, 1964.
Argument	47.
Argument	<NAME>, J. took different line agreeing in part with both the points of view.
Argument	His approach is this:  (1) It is not necessary to consider the impact or the scope of Title VI of the Civil Rights Act inasmuch as the said question was not raised or considered in the courts below.
RatioOfTheDecision	The matter had to be examined only with reference to the Fourteenth Amendment;  (2) Any distinction based on race is inherently suspect in the light of the equal protection clause and calls for more exacting judicial examination.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is for the <ORG> in such a case to establish that the distinction was precisely tailored to serve a compelling governmental interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	(3) Since the spe cial admissions program of the <ORG> totally excluded some individual (non-minorities) from enjoying the <ORG> provided benefit of admission to the medical school solely because of their race, the classification must be regarded as suspect and it will be sustained only if it is supported by substantial state purpose or interest and only where it is established that the classification is necessary to the accomplishment of such purpose or for safeguarding such interest.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> has failed to discharge this burden, though the <ORG> interest in removing "identified discrimination"  and attainment of a "diverse student body" were certainly compelling interests.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, the <ORG> has failed to establish that for attaining the said abjectives, creation of quotas was necessary.
RatioOfTheDecision	(4) While preferences can be provided in favour of disadvantaged sections, reservation of seats which had the effect of excluding members of a race or races from those seats altogether, is not permissible.
RatioOfTheDecision	For this re ason too, the special admissions program of the <ORG> must be held to violate the Fourteenth Amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the course of his opinion, the learned Judge observed:  A facial intent to discriminate, however, is evident in petitioner's preference program and not denied in this case.
RatioOfTheDecision	No such facial infirmity exists in an admissions program where race or ethnic background is simply one element - to be weighed fairly against other elements - in the selection process....
RatioOfTheDecision	In summary, it is evident that the <ORG> special admissions program involves the use of an explicit racial classification never before countenanced by this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It tells applicants who are not Negro, Asian, or Chicano that they are totally excluded from a specific percentage of the seats in an entering class.
RatioOfTheDecision	No matter how strong their qualifications, quantitative and extracurricular including their own potential for contribution to educational diversity, they are never afforded the chance to compete with applicants from the preferred groups  for the special admissions seats.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time, the preferred applicants have the opportunity to compete for every seat in the class.
Precedent	In this manner, the learned Judge agreed with <NAME>, <NAME> that race-conscious admissions programmes are permissible under the Fourteenth Amendment, but qualified the meaning of the race-conscious programmes.
Precedent	At the same time, he agreed with the learned Chief Justice that the special admissions programme of <NAME> was unconstitutional.
Precedent	He commended the <ORG> admissions programme which provided for certain preferences in favour of racially disadvantaged sections, without reserving any seats as such for them.
Precedent	48.
Precedent	We may next notice the decision in <NAME> v. <NAME> [1980] 65 Lawyers Ed.
Precedent	2nd 90.
Fact	The Public Works Employment Act, 1977 contained a provision to the effect that atleast 10% of federal funds granted for local public works projects must be used by the <ORG> or the local grantee to procure services or supplies from businesses owned by minority group  members, defined as United State citizens "who are negroes, spanish-speaking, Orientals, Indians, Eskimos and Aleuts".
Fact	Regulations were framed under the Act and guidelines issued requiring the grantees and private contractors to seek out all available qualified bona fide minority business enterprises (MBEs), to the extent feasible, for fulfilling the 10% MBE requirement.
Fact	The guidelines provided that contracts shall be awarded to bona fide MBEs, even though they are not the lowest bidders if their bids reflect merely attempts to cover costs inflated by the present effects of prior disadvantage and discrimination.
Fact	This requirement could, however, be waived in individual cases if the grantee established the infeasibility of the requirement.
Fact	Several associations of construction contractors and Sub-contractors filed a suit in <ORG> for a declaration that the said provision of the Public Works Employment Act and the regulations made thereunder are void and enforceable being violative of the equ al protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and equal protection component of the due process clause of the Fifth Amendment.
Fact	The challenge failed in <ORG> as well as in <ORG>.
Fact	The matter was then carried to <ORG>.
Fact	By a majority of 6:3 (<NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Fact	dissenting) <ORG> repelled the challenge.
Fact	Chief Justice <NAME> speaking for himself.
Precedent	<NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Argument	stated the object of the impugned provision in the following words:  The device of a 10% MBE participation requirement, subject to administrative waiver, was thought to be required to assure minority business participation, otherwise it was thought that repetition of the prior experience could be expected, with participation by minority business accounting for an inordinately small percentage of government contracting.
Argument	The learned Chief Justice then proceeded to examine" the question whether as a means to accomplish these plainly constitutional objectives, <ORG> ca n use racial and ethnic criteria in this limited way as a condition attached to a federal grant."
Argument	Indeed, he posed the same question in this form: "Whether the limited use of racial and ethnic criteria is a constitutionally permissible means for achieving the congressional objectives", and proceeded to answer the same - after referring exhaustively to the earlier decisions of the court relating to school admissions - in the following words:  We held that "just as the race of students must be considered in determining whether a constitutional violation has occurred, so also must race be considered in formulating a remedy."
Argument	(emphasis added) ...
Argument	In dealing with this facial challenge to the statute, doubts must be resolved in support of the congressional judgment that this limited program is a necessary step to effectuate the constitutional mandate for equality of economic opportunity.
Argument	49.
Argument	<NAME>, J. speaking for himself, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	in his concurring opinion, pointed out the approach to be adop ted in judging the validity of the race-conscious programmes and concluded with these resounding words:  In my separate opinion in <NAME>, I recounted the ingenious and pervasive forms of discrimination against the Negro" long condoned under the Constitution and concluded that "the position of the Negro today in America is the tragic but inevitable consequence of centuries of unequal treatment" I there stated:  It is because of a legacy of unequal treatment that we now must permit the institutions of this society to give consideration to race in making decisions about who will hold the positions of influence, affluence, and prestige in America.
Precedent	For far too long, the doors to those positions have been shut to Negroes.
Precedent	If we are ever to become a fully integrated society, one in which the color of a person's skin will not determine the opportunities available to him or her, we must be willing to take steps to open those doOrs.
Precedent	50.
Fact	We may now examine the decision in <ORG> v. <ORG> ons <ORG>, rendered on June 27, 1990 (Copies of the decision have been made available to us by <NAME>, counsel for <ORG>).
Fact	Under the Communications Act, 1934, <ORG> was vested with the exclusive authority to grant licences to persons wishing to construct and operate <ORG> in United States.
Fact	The grant of licences was to be based on 'public convenience, interest or necessity'.
Fact	The commission found that over the last two decades relatively fewer members of minority groups have held broadcasting licences, indeed less than one percent.
Fact	Even as late as in 1986, they owned just 2.1%.
Fact	The <ORG> proposed to remedy this under-representation and accordingly evolved a policy whereunder minorities were to be granted certain preferences in the matter of grant of these licences.
Fact	The policy had two prominent features.
Fact	The first was to provide for a preference in the matter of evaluation of applicants and the second was, what may b e called, 'distress sale policy'.
Fact	The second feature meant that where the qualifications of a licencee to hold a broadcast licence comes into question he was entitled to transfer the said licence to save the disqualification provided such transfer is made in favour of a member of a minority.
Fact	The said two features were questioned by <ORG>, which matter was ultimately brought to <ORG>.
Fact	The decision of the majority (<NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.)
Fact	rendered by <NAME>, J. is note-worthy for the shift of approach from the earlier decisions.
Fact	It is now held that a classification based on race (benign race conscious measures) is constitutionally permissible even if it is not designed to compensate victims of past governmental or societal discrimination so long as it serves important governmental objectives and is substantially related to achievement of those objectives.
Precedent	In other words, it is held that it is not necessary that the court apply a strict standard of scru tiny to evaluate racial classification to ascertain whether it is necessary for achieving the relevant objective and further whether it is narrowly tailored to achieve a compelling state interest.
Precedent	<NAME>, J. relied upon the opinion of Chief Justice <NAME> in Fullilove for this liberal approach.
Precedent	It would be appropriate to quote certain observations from his opinion:  We hold that benign race-conscious measures mandated by <ORG> - even if those manures are not "remedial" in the sense of being designed to compensate victims of past governmental or societal discrimination - are constitutionally permissible to the extent that they serve important governmental objectives within the power of <ORG> and are substantially related to achievement of those objectives.
Precedent	<ORG> and the <ORG> have selected the minority ownership policies primarily to promote programming diversity, and they urge that such diversity is an important governmental objective that can serve as a constitutional basis for the preference policie s. We agree....  Against this background, we conclude that the interest in enhancing broadcast diversity is, at the very least an important governmental objective and is therefore a sufficient basis for the <ORG>'s minority ownership policies...we must pay close attention to the expertise of the <ORG> and the fact finding of the <ORG> when analyzing the nexus between minority ownership and programming diversity.
Precedent	With respect to this "complex" empirical question, ibid., we are required to give "great weight to the decisions of <ORG> and the experience of the <ORG>.
Precedent	51.
Precedent	On the other hand, the minority (<NAME>, <NAME> speaking for herself, <NAME>, C.<NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, J<NAME>) protested against the abandonment of what they thought was a well established standard of scrutiny in such cases in the following words:  Strict scrutiny" requires that, to be upheld, racial classifications must be determined to be necessary and narrowly tailored to achieve a compelling state interest.
Precedent	The court aba ndons this traditional safeguard against discrimination for a lower standard of review, and in practice applies a standard like that applicable to routine legislation.
Precedent	This <ORG>'s precedents in no way justify the <ORG>'s marked departure from our traditional treatment or race classifications and its conclusion that different equal protection principles apply to these federal actions.
Precedent	52.
Precedent	We have examined the decisions of U.S. Supreme <ORG> at some length only with a view to notice how another democracy is grappling with a problem similiar in certain respects to the problem facing this country.
RatioOfTheDecision	The minorities (including blacks) in United States are just about 16 to 18% of the total population, whereas the backward classes (including the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes) in this country - by whichever yardstick they are measured - do certainly constitute a majority of the population.
RatioOfTheDecision	The minorites there comprise 5 to 7 groups - Blacks, spanish-speaking people, Indians, Purto Ricano, Aleuts and so on - wh ereas the castes and communities comprising backward classes in this country run into thousands.
RatioOfTheDecision	Untouchability - and 'unapproachability', as it was being practised in Kerala - is something which no other country in the world had the misfortune to have - nor the blessed caste system.
RatioOfTheDecision	There have been equally old civilisations on earth like ours, if not older, but none had evolved these pernicious practices, much less did they stamp them with scriptural sanction.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now coming to Constitutional provisions, Section 1 of the Fourteenth Amendment (insofar as it guarantees equal protection of the laws) corresponds to Article 14 but they do not have provisions corresponding to Article 16(4) or 15(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	Title VI of the Civil Rights Act enacted in 1964 roughly corresponds to Clause (2) of Articles 15 and 16.
RatioOfTheDecision	53.
RatioOfTheDecision	At this stage, we wish to clarify one particular aspect.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 16(1) is a facet of Article 14.
RatioOfTheDecision	Just as Article 14 permits reasonable classification, so does Article 16(1).
RatioOfTheDecision	A classification may involve reservatio n of seats or vacancies, as the case may be.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, under Clause (1) of Article 16 , apointments and/or posts can be reserved in favour of a class.
RatioOfTheDecision	But an argument is now being advanced - evidently inspired by the opinion of <NAME>, J. in <NAME> that Article 16(1) permits only preferences but not reservations.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reasoning in support of the said argument is the same as was put forward by <NAME>, J.
RatioOfTheDecision	This argument, in our opinion, disregards the fact that that is not the unanimous view of the court in <NAME>.
Precedent	Four Judges including <NAME>, J. took the view that such a reservation was not barred by the Fourteenth Amendment while the other four (including <NAME>, C.J.)
Precedent	took the view that the Fourteenth Amendment and Title VI of the Civil Right Acts, 1964 bars all race-conscious progammes.
Precedent	At the same time, there are a series of decisions relating to school desegregation - from <NAME> to <ORG> v. <NAME> (28 L.Ed.
Precedent	2nd 586) - where the court has been consistently taking the view that if rac e be the basis of discrimination, race can equally form the basis of remedial action.
RatioOfTheDecision	The shift in approach indicated by <ORG> is equally significant.
RatioOfTheDecision	The 'lingering effects' (of past discrimination) theory as well as the standard of strictest scrutiny of race-conscious programmes have both been abandoned.
RatioOfTheDecision	Suffice it to note that no single uniform pattern of thought can be discerned from these decisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Ideas appear to be still in the process of evolution.
RatioOfTheDecision	PART - III (QUESTIONS 1 AND 2) We may now proceed to deal with the questions aforementioned.
RatioOfTheDecision	Question.
RatioOfTheDecision	1(a): Whether the 'provision' in Article 16(4) must necessarily be made by <ORG>?
Argument	54.
Argument	<NAME>, learned Counsel for the petitioner in Writ Petition No.
Argument	930 of 1990 submits that the "provision" contemplated by Clause (4) of Article 16 can be made only by and should necessarily be made by the legislative wing of the <ORG> and not by the executive or any other authority.
Argument	He disputes the correctness of the ho lding in <NAME> negativing an identical contention.
Argument	He submits that since the provision made under Article 16(4) affects the fundamental rights of other citizens, such a provision can be made only by <ORG>.
Argument	He submits that if the power of making the "provision" is given to the executive, it well give room for any amount of abuse.
Precedent	According to the learned Counsel, the political executive, owing to the degeneration of the electoral process, normally acts out of political and electoral compulsions, for which reason it may not act fairly and indepenently.
Precedent	If, on the other hand, the provision is to be made by the legislative wing of the <ORG>, it will not only provide an opportunity for debate and discussion in the <ORG> where several shades of opinion are represented but a balanced and unbiased decision free from the allurements of electoral gains is more likely to emerge from such a deliberating body.
Precedent	Sri <NAME> cites the example of Tamil Nadu where, according to him, before every g eneral election a few communities are added to the list of backward classes, only with a view to winning them over to the ruling party.
Precedent	We are not concerned with the aspect of what is ideal or desirable but with what is the proper meaning to be ascribed to the expression 'provision' in Article 16(4) having regard to the context.
Precedent	The use of the expression 'provision' in Clause (4) of Article 16 appears to us to be not without design.
Precedent	According to the definition of 'State' in Article 12 , it includes not merely the government and <ORG> of India and <ORG> of each of the States but all local authorities and other authorities within the territory of India or under the control of <ORG> which means that such a measure of reservation can be provided not only in the matter of services under the Central and State Governments but also in the services of local and other authorities referred to in Article 12.
Precedent	The expression 'Local Authority' is defined in <CITATION_174566148> of  the General Clauses Act.
Precedent	It takes in all municipalites, <ORG> and other similar bodies.
Precedent	The expression 'other authorities' has received extensive attention from the court.
Precedent	It includes all statutory authorities and other agencies and instrumentalities of <ORG>.
Precedent	Now, would it be reasonable, possible or practicable to say that the <ORG> or the <ORG> of the State should provide for reservation of posts/appointments in the services of all such bodies besides providing for in respect of services under <ORG>?
Precedent	This aspect would become clearer if we notice the definition of "Law" in Article 13(3)(a).
Precedent	It reads:  13(3) In this article, unless the context otherwise requires,-  (a) "Law" includes any Ordinance, order, bye-law, rule, regulation, notification, custom or usage having in the territory of India the force of law;...
Precedent	The words "order", "bye-law", "rule" and "regulation" in this definition are significant.
Precedent	Reading the definition of "State"  in Article 12 and of "Law" in Article 13(3)(a ), it becomes clear that a measure of the nature contemplated by Article 16(4) can be provided not only by <ORG> but also by the executive in respect of <ORG> services and by the local bodies and "other authorities" contemplated by Article 12 , in respect of their respective services.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some of the local bodies and and some of the statutory corporations like <ORG> may have thier own legislative wings.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such a situation, it would be unreasonable and inappropriate to insist that reservation in all these services should be provided by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The situation and circumstances of each of these bodies may vary.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rule regarding reservation has to be framed to suit the particular situations.
RatioOfTheDecision	All this cannot reasonably be done by <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even textually speaking, the contention cannot be accepted.
RatioOfTheDecision	The very use of the word "provision" in Article 16(4) is significant.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whereas Clauses (3) and (5) of Articl e 16 - and Clauses (2) to (6) of Article 19 - use the word "Law", Article 16(4) uses the word "provision".
RatioOfTheDecision	Regulation of service conditions by orders and Rules made by the <ORG> was a well known feature at the time of the framing of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Probably for this reason, a deliberate departure has been made in the case of Clause (4).
RatioOfTheDecision	Accordingly, we hold, agreeing with <NAME>, that the "provision" contemplated by Article 16(4) can also be made by the executive wing of the <ORG> or of the <ORG>, as the case may be, as has been done in the present case.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> has been followed recently in <CITATION_1395006> .
RatioOfTheDecision	With respect to the argument of abuse of power by the political executive, we may say that there is adequate safeguard against misuse by the political executive of the power under Article 16(4) in the provision itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Any determination of backwardness is not a subjective exercise nor a matter of subjective satisfaction.
RatioOfTheDecision	As held herein - as also by earlier judgments - the exercise is an objective  one.
RatioOfTheDecision	Certain objective social and other criteria has to be satisfied before any group or class of citizens could be treated as backward.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the executive includes, for collateral reasons, groups or classes not satisfying the relevant criteria, it would be a clear case of fraud on power.
Fact	Question 1(b): Whether an executive order making a 'provision' under Article 16(4) is enforceable forthwith?
Precedent	55.
Argument	A question is raised whether an executive order made in terms of Article 16(4) is effective and enforceable by itself or whether it is necessary that the said "provision" is enacted into a law made by the appropriate <ORG> under Article 309 or is incorporated into and issued as a Rule by the President/Governor under the proviso to Article 309 for it to become enforceable?
Argument	Mr. <NAME> submits that Article 16(4) is merely declaratory in nature, that it is an enabling provision and that it is not a source of power by itself.
Argument	He submits that unless made into a law by the appropriate <ORG> or issued  as a rule in terms of the proviso to Article 309 , the "provision" so made by the <ORG> does not become enforceable.
Argument	At the same time, he submits that the impugned Memorandums must be deemed to be and must be treated as Rules made and issued under the proviso to Article 309 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	We find it difficult to agree with Sri <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once we hold that a provision under Article 16(4) can be made by the executive, it must necessarily follow that such a provision is effective the moment it is made.
Precedent	<ORG> of this Court in <NAME> (1981 S.C. 561), (<NAME>, C.J., speaking for the <ORG>) has observed:   Article 235 does not confer upon <ORG> the power to make rules relating to conditions of service of judicial officers attached to district courts and the courts subordinate thereto.
Precedent	Whenever it was intended to confer on any authority the power to make any special provisions or rules, including rules relating to conditions of service, the Constitution has stated so  in express terms.
Precedent	See, for example Articles 15(4), 16(4), 77(3), 87(2), 118, 145(1), 146(1) and (2), 148(5), 166(3), 176(2), 187(3), 208, 225, 227(2) and (3), 229(1) and (2), 234, 237 and 283(1) and (2).
RatioOfTheDecision	Be that as it may, there is yet another reason, why we cannot agree that the impugned Memorandums are not effective and enforceable the moment they are issued.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is well settled by the decisions of this Court that the appropriate government is empowered to prescribe the conditions of service of its employees by an executive order in the absence of the rules made under the proviso to Article 309.
Precedent	It is further held by this Court that even where Rules under the proviso to Article 309 are made, the government can issue orders/instructions with respect to matters upon which the Rules are silent.
Precedent	[see <CITATION_1320680> ].
Precedent	This view has been reiterated in a recent decision of this Court in <CITATION_1395006> wherein it is held:  <ORG> is not right in stating that there cannot be an administrative order  directing reservation for Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes as it would alter the statutory rules in force.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rules do not provide for any reservation.
RatioOfTheDecision	In fact it is silent on the subject of reservation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> could direct the reservation by executive orders.
RatioOfTheDecision	The administrative orders cannot be issued in contravention of the statutory rules but it could be issued to supplement the statutory rules [See the observations in <CITATION_142307411> .
RatioOfTheDecision	In fact similar circulars were issued by <ORG> introducing reservations for Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes in <ORG> both for selection and non-selection categories of posts.
RatioOfTheDecision	They were issued to implement the policy of <ORG> and they have been upheld by this Court in <CITATION_1111529> .
RatioOfTheDecision	It would, therefore, follow that until a law is made or rules are issued under Article 309 with respect to reservation in favour of backward classes, it would always be open to the Executive (<ORG>) to provide for re servation of appointments/posts in favour of Backward Classes by an executive order.
RatioOfTheDecision	We cannot also agree with Sri <NAME> that the impugned Memorandums should be treated as Rules made under the proviso to Article 309.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is nothing in them suggesting even distantly that they were issued under the proviso to Article 309.
RatioOfTheDecision	They were never intended to be so, nor is that the stand of <ORG> before us.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are executive orders issued under Article 73 of the Constitution read with Clause (4) of Article 16.
RatioOfTheDecision	The mere omission of a recital "in the name and by order of the President of India" does not affect the validity or enforceability of the orders, as held by this <ORG> repeatedly.
RatioOfTheDecision	Question 2(a).
Precedent	Whether Clause (4) of Article 16 is an exception to Clause (1)?
Precedent	56.
Precedent	In Balaji it was held - "there is no doubt that Article 15(4) has to be read as a proviso or an exception to Articles 15(1) and 29(2)".
Precedent	It was observed that Article 15(4) was inserted by the First Amendment in the light of the decis ion in Champakam, with a view to remove the defect pointed out by this <ORG> namely, the absence of a provision in Article 15 corresponding to Clause (4) of Article 16.
Precedent	Following <NAME> it was held by <ORG> (by majority) in Devadasan - "further this <ORG> has already held that Clause (4) of Article 16 is by way of a proviso or an exception to Clause (1)".
Precedent	<NAME>, J., however, opined in his dissenting opinion that Article 16(4) is not an exception to Article 16(1) but that it is only an emphatic way of stating the principle inherent in the main provision itself.
Precedent	Be that as it may, since the decision in Devadasan, it was assumed by this <ORG> that Article 16(4) is an exception to Article 16(1).
Precedent	This view, however, received a severe set-back from the majority decision in <CITATION_1130169> .
Precedent	Though the minority (<NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.)
Precedent	stuck to the view that Article 16(4) is an exception, the majority (<NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.)
Precedent	held that Article 16(4) is  not an exception to Article 16(1) but that it was merely an emphatic way of stating a principle implicit in Article 16(1).
Precedent	(Beg.
Precedent	J. took a slightly different view which it is not necessary to mention here).
Precedent	The said four learned Judges - whose views have been referred to in para 41 - held that Article 16(1) being a facet of the doctrine of equality enshrined in Article 14 permits reasonable classification just as Article 14 does.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our respectful opinion, the view taken by the majority in <NAME> is the correct one.
RatioOfTheDecision	We too believe that Article 16(1) does permit reasonable classification for ensuring attainment of the equality of opportunity assured by it.
RatioOfTheDecision	For assuring equality of opportunity, it may well be necessary in certain situations to treat unequally situated persons unequally.
RatioOfTheDecision	Not doing so, would perpetuate and accentuate inequality.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 16(4) is an instance of such classification, put in to place the matter beyond controversy.
RatioOfTheDecision	The "backward class of citizens" are classified as a separate category  deserving a special treatment in the nature of reservation of appointments/posts in the services of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Accordingly, we hold that Clause (4) of Article 16 is not exception to Clause (1) of Article 16.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is an instance of classification implicit in and permitted by Clause (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	The speech of Dr. <NAME> during the debate on draft Article 10(3) [ corresponding to Article 16(4)] in <ORG> - referred to in para 28 - shows that a substantial number of members of <ORG> insisted upon a "provision (being) made for the entry of certain communities which have so far been outside the administration", and that draft Clause (3) was put in recognition and acceptance of the said demand.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a provision which must be read along with and in harmony with Clause (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, even without Clause (4), it would have been permissible for the <ORG> to have evolved such a classification and made a provision for reservation of appointments/posts in their favour.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (4) merely puts th e matter beyond any doubt in specific terms.
RatioOfTheDecision	Regarding the view expressed in Balaji and Devadasan, it must be remembered that at that time it was not yet recognised by this <ORG> that Article 16(1) being a facet of Article 14 does implicitly permit classification.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once this feature was recognised the theory of Clause (4) being an exception to Clause (1) became untenable.
RatioOfTheDecision	It had to be accepted that Clause (4) is an instance of classification inherent in Clause (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	Now, just as Article 16(1) is a facet or an elaboration of the principle underlying Article 14 , Clause (2) of Article 16 is also an elaboration of a facet of Clause (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	If Clause (4) is an exception to Clause (1) then it is equally an exception to Clause (2).
RatioOfTheDecision	Question then arises, in what respect is Clause (4) an exception to Clause (2), if 'class' does not means 'caste'.
RatioOfTheDecision	Neither Clause (1) nor Clause (2) speak of class.
RatioOfTheDecision	Does the contention mean that Clause (1) does not permit classification and therefore Clause (4) is an exception to it.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus, f rom any point of view, the contention of the petitioners has no merit.
Precedent	Question 2(b): Whether Article 16(4) is exhaustive of the concept of reservations in favour of backward classes?
Precedent	57.
Precedent	The question than arises whether Clause (4) of Article 16 is exhaustive of the topic of reservations in favour of backward classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Before we answer this question it is well to examine the meaning and content of the expression "reservation".
RatioOfTheDecision	Its meaning has to be ascertained having regard to the context in which it occurs.
RatioOfTheDecision	The relevant words are "any provision for the reservation of appointments or posts."
RatioOfTheDecision	The question is whether the said words contemplate only one form of provision namely reservation simplicitor, or do they take in other forms of special provisions like preferences, concessions and exemptions.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our opinion, reservation is the highest form of special provision, while preference, concession and exemption are lesser forms.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitutional scheme and context of Article 16(4) induces us to take the view t hat larger concept of reservations takes within its sweep all supplemental and ancilliary provisions as also lesser types of special provisions like exemptions, concessions and relaxations, consistent no doubt with the requirement of maintenance of efficiency of administration - the admonition of Article 335.
RatioOfTheDecision	The several concessions, exemptions and other measures issued by <ORG> and noticed in <ORG> are instances of supplementay, incidental and ancilliary provisions made with a view to make the main provision of reservation effective i.e., to ensure that the members of the reserved class fully avail of the provision for reservation in their favour.
RatioOfTheDecision	The other type of measure is the one in <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was no provision for reservation in favour of Scheduled Castes/Scheduled Tribes in the matter of promotion to the category of <ORG> Clerks.
RatioOfTheDecision	Certain tests were required to be passed before a Lower Division Clerk could be promoted as <ORG> Clerk.
RatioOfTheDecision	A large number of  Lower Division Clerks belonging to S.C./S.T.
RatioOfTheDecision	were not able to pass those tests, with the result they were stagnating in the category of L.D.Cs.
RatioOfTheDecision	Rule 13AA was accordingly made empowering the government to grant exemption to members of S.C./S.T.
RatioOfTheDecision	from passing those tests and the Government did exempt them, not absolutely, but only for a limited period.
RatioOfTheDecision	This provision for exemption was a lesser form of special treatment than reservation.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no reason why such a special provision should not be held to be included within the larger concept of reservation.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is in this context that the words "any provision for the reservation of appointments and posts" assume significance.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "any" and the associated words must be given their due meaning.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are not a mere surplusage.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that in <NAME> it was assumed by the majority that Clause (4) permits only one form of provision namely reservation of appointments/posts and that if any concessions or exemptions are to be extended to backward classes it can  be done only under Clause (1) of Article 16.
RulingByLowerCourt	In fact the argument of the writ petitioners (who succeeded before <ORG>) was that the only type of provision that the <ORG> can make in favour of the backward classes is reservation of appointments/posts provided by Clause (4) and that the said clause does not contemplate or permit granting of any exemptions or concessions to the backward classes.
RulingByLowerCourt	This argument was accepted by <ORG>.
RulingByLowerCourt	This <ORG>, however, by a majority (<NAME>,<NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME> and <NAME>, <NAME>.)
RatioOfTheDecision	reversed the view taken by <ORG>, holding that such exemptions/concessions can be extended under Clause (1) of Article 16.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME>, J. who joined the majority in exemption provided by impugned notification was indeed a kind of reservation and was warranted by and relatable to Clause (4) of Article 16 itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	This was because - according to the learned Judge - Clause (4) was exhaustive of the provisions that can be made in favour of the backward classes in the matt er of employment.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are inclined to agree with the view taken by <NAME>, J. for the reasons given hereinabove.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our opinion, therefore, where the <ORG> finds it necessary - for the purpose of giving full effect to the provision of reservation to provide certain exemptions, concessions or preferences to members of backward classes, it can extend the same under Clause (4) itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, all supplemental and ancilliary provisions to ensure full availment of provisions for reservation can be provided as part of concept of reservation itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, in a given situation, the <ORG> may think that in the case of a particular backward class it is not necessary to provide reservation of appointments/posts and that it would be sufficient if a certain preference or a concession is provided in their favour.
RatioOfTheDecision	This can be done under Clause (4) itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this sense, Clause (4) of Article 16 is exhaustive of the special provisions that can be made in favour of "the backward class of citizens".
RatioOfTheDecision	Backward Classes h aving been classified by the Constitution itself as a class deserving special treatment and the Constitution having itself specified the nature of special treatment, it should be presumed that no further classification or special treatment is permissible in their favour apart from or outside of Clause (4) of Article 16.
Fact	Question 2(c): Whether Article 16(4) is exhaustive of the very concept of reservations?
Precedent	58.
Precedent	The aspect next to be considered is whether Clause (4) is exhaustive of the very concept of reservations?
Precedent	In other words, the question is whether any reservations can be provided outside Clause (4) i.e., under Clause (1) of Article 16.
Precedent	There are two views on this aspect.
RatioOfTheDecision	On a fuller consideration of the matter, we are of the opinion that Clause (4) is not, and cannot be held to be, exhaustive of the concept of reservations; it is exhaustive of reservations in favour of backward classes alone.
RatioOfTheDecision	Merely bacause, one form of classification is stated as a specific clause, it does not follow that the very c oncept and power of classification implicit in Clause (1) is exhausted thereby.
RatioOfTheDecision	To say so would not be correct in principle.
RatioOfTheDecision	But, at the same time, one thing is clear.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is in very exceptional situations, and not for all and sundry reasons - that any further reservations, of whatever kind, should be provided under Clause (1).
RatioOfTheDecision	In such cases, the <ORG> has to satisfy, if called upon, that making such a provision was necessary (in public interest) to redress a specific situation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The very presence of Clause (4) should act as a damper upon the propensity to create further classes deserving special treatment.
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason for saying so is very simply.
RatioOfTheDecision	If reservations are made both under Clause (4) as well as under Clause (1), the vacancies available for free competition as well as reserved categories would be correspondingly whittled down and that is not a reasonable thing to do.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whether Clause (1) of Article 16 does not permit any reservations?
RatioOfTheDecision	59.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the reasons given in the preceding paragraphs we must reject  the argument that Clause (1) of Article 16 permits only extending of preferences, concessions and exemptions, but does not permit reservation of appointments/posts.
RatioOfTheDecision	As pointed out in para (54) the argument that no reservations can be made under Article 16(1) is really inspired by the opinion of <NAME>, J. in <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	But in the very same paragraph we had pointed out that it is not the unanimous opinion of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In principle, we see no basis for acceding to the said contention.
RatioOfTheDecision	What kind of special provision should be made in favour of a particular class is a matter for the <ORG> to decide, having regard to the facts and circumstances of a given situation - subject, of course, to the observations in the preceding paragraph.
Precedent	PART - IV (QUESTIONS 3, 4 AND 5) Question 3(a): Meaning of the expression "Backward Class of citizens" in Article 16(4).
Precedent	60.
Precedent	What does the expression "Backward Class of Citizens" in Article 16(4) signify and how should they be identified?
Precedent	This has been the single most difficult quest ion tormenting this nation.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression is not defined in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	What does it mean then?
RatioOfTheDecision	The arguments before us mainly revolved round this question.
RatioOfTheDecision	Several shades of opinion have been presented to us ranging from one extreme to the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, it may be difficult to set out in full the reasoning presented before us orally and in several written propositions submitted by various counsel.
RatioOfTheDecision	We can mention only the substance of and the broad features emerging from those submissions.
RatioOfTheDecision	At one end of the spectrum stands <NAME> (supported by several other counsel) whose submissions may briefly be summarised in the following words: a secular, unified and caste-less society is a basic feature of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Caste is a prohibited ground of distinction under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It ought be erased altogether from <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It can never be the basis for determining backward classes referred to in Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	The Report of the Mandal Commission, which is the basis of the impugn ed Memorandums, has treated the expression "backward classes" as synonymous with backward castes and has proceed to identify backward classes solely and exclusively on the basis of caste, ignoring all other considerations including poverty.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has indeed invented castes for Non-Hindus where none exists.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> has divided the nation into two sections, backward and forward, placing 52% of the population in the former section.
RatioOfTheDecision	Acceptance of <ORG> would spell disaster to the unity and integrity of the nation.
RatioOfTheDecision	If half of the posts are reserved for backward classes, it would seriously jeopardise the efficiency of the administration, educational system, and all other services resulting in backwardness of the entire nation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Merit will disappear by deifying backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	Article 16(4) is broader than Article 15(4).
Precedent	The expression "backward class of citizens" in Article 16(4) is not limited to "socially and educationally backward classes" in Article 15(4).
Precedent	The impugned Memorandums, based on the said report must n ecessarily fall to the ground along with the Report.
Precedent	In fact the main thrust of Sri <NAME>'s argument has been against the Mandal Commission Report.
Precedent	61.
Precedent	<NAME> appearing for the petitioner in Writ Petition No.
RatioOfTheDecision	930 of 1990 adopted a slightly different approach while reiterating that the expression "backward classes of citizens" in Article 16(4) cannot be construed as backward castes.
RatioOfTheDecision	According to him, backwardness may be social and educational and may also be economic.
RatioOfTheDecision	The authority appointed to identify backward classes must first settle the criteria or the indicators for determining backward classes and then it must apply the said criteria to each and every group in the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the course of such identification, it may well happen that certain castes answer and satisfy the criteria of backwardness and may as a whole qualify for being termed as a backward class.
Precedent	But it is not permissible to start with castes to determine whether a caste is a backward class.
Precedent	He relied upon the provision in  Clause (2) of Article 38 and Article 46 to say that the objective is to minimize the inequalities in income not only among individuals but also among groups of persons and to help the weaker sections of the society.
Precedent	The economic criterion is an important one and must be applied in determining backward classes and also for excluding those sections or identified groups who may for the sake of convenience be referred to as the 'creamy layer'.
Precedent	Since castes do not exist among Muslims, Christians and Sikhs, caste can never be the basis of identification.
Precedent	The learned Counsel too pointed out the alleged basic errors in the approach adopted by and conclusions arrived at by <ORG>.
Precedent	62.
Precedent	Smt.
Precedent	<NAME> also took the stand that caste can never be the basis for identification.
Precedent	According to her, survey to identify backward classes should be from individual to individual; it cannot be caste-wise.
Argument	To the same effect are the submissions of <NAME> appearing for <ORG>.
