Argument	Accor ding to him, the only basis for identifying backward classes should be occupation-cum-means as was done in the State of Karnataka at a particular stage which aspect is dealt with and approved by this Court in <CITATION_203735> .
Argument	A secular socialist society, he submitted, can never countenance identification of backward classes on the basis of caste which would only perpetuate and accentuate caste differences and generate antagonism and antipathy between castes.
Argument	63.
Argument	At the other end of the spectrum stands <NAME>, counsel appearing for the State of Bihar supported by several other counsel.
Argument	According to him, backward castes in Article 16(4) meant and means only the members of Shudra casts which is located between the three upper castes (Brahmins, Kshatriyas and Vaishyas) and the out-castes (Panchamas) referred to as Scheduled Castes.
Argument	According to him, Article 16(4) was conceived only for these "middle castes" i.e., castes categorised as shudras in the caste system and for none else.
RatioOfTheDecision	These backward c astes have suffered centuries of discrimination and disadvantage, leading to their backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	The expression "backward classes" does not refer to any current characteristic of a backward caste save and except paucity or inadequacies of representation in the apparatus of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Poverty is not a necessary criterion of backwardness; in is in fact irrelevant.
RatioOfTheDecision	The provision for reservation is really a programme of historical compensation.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is neither a measure of economic reform nor a poverty alleviation programme.
Argument	The learned Counsel further submitted that it is for the <ORG> to determine who are the backward classes; it is not a matter for the court.
Argument	The decision of the <ORG> is not judicially reviewable.
Argument	Even if reviewable, the scope of judicial review is extremely limited - to the only question whether the exercise of power is a fraud on the Constitution.
Argument	The learned Counsel referred to certain American decisions to show that even in that country several programmes of affirmative action and  compensatory discrimination have been evolved and upheld by courts.
Argument	64.
Argument	Dr. <NAME>, learned Counsel appearing for <ORG> (an association of Ezhavas in Kerala) submitted that Article 16(4) and 15(4) occupy different fields and serve different purposes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Whereas Article 15(4) contemplates positive action programmes, Article 16(4) enables the <ORG> to undertake schemes of positive discrimination.
RatioOfTheDecision	For this reason, the class of intended beneficiaries under both the clauses is different.
RatioOfTheDecision	The social and educational backwardness which is the basis of identifying backwardness under Article 15(4) is only partly true in the case of 'backward class of citizens' in Article 16(4).
Precedent	The expression "any backward class of citizens" occurring in Article 16(4) must be understood in the light of the purpose of the said clause namely, empowerment of those groups and classes which have been kept out of the administration - classes which have suffered historic disabilities arising from discriminat ion or disadvantage or both and who must now be provided entry into the administrative apparatus.
Precedent	In the light of the fact that the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes were also intended to be beneficiaries of Article 16(4) there is no reason why caste cannot be an exclusive criteria for determining beneficiaries under Article 16(4).
Precedent	Counsel emphasised the fact that Article 16(4) speaks of group protection and not individual protection.
Precedent	<NAME> appearing for <ORG>, an Intervenor, submitted that caste plus poverty plus location plus residence should be the basis of identification and not mere caste.
Precedent	According to the learned Counsel, a national concensus is essential to introduce reservations for 'orther backward classes' under Article 16(4) and that efforts must be made to achieve such a concensus.
Precedent	65.
Argument	Sri <NAME> appearing for the State of Tamil Nadu supported the <ORG> in its entirety.
Argument	According to him, backward classes must be identified only on t he basis of caste and that no economic criteria should be adopted for the said purpose.
Argument	He submitted that economic criteria may be employed as one of the indicators for identification of backward classes but once a backward class is identified as such, there is no question of excluding any one from that class on the basis of income or means or on any other economic criterion.
Argument	He referred to the history of reservations in the province of Madras prior to independence and now it has been working there successfully and peacefully over the last several decades.
Argument	<NAME> appearing for the State of Kerala supported the identification of backward classes solely and exclusively on the basis of caste.
Argument	He submitted that the caste system is scientifically organised and practiced in Kerala and, therefore, furnishes a perfectly scientific basis for identification of backward classes.
Precedent	He submitted that besides the vice of untouchability, another greater vice of 'unapproachability' was also being practiced in that Stat e.  <NAME>, M.P., President of <ORG> and President of <ORG> submitted that caste should be the sole criteria for determining backwardness.
Precedent	He referred to centuries of injustice meted out by upper castes to shudras and panchamas and submitted that these castes must now be given a share in the governance of the country which alone will assure their dignity besides instilling in them a sense of confidence and a spirit of competition.
Precedent	66.
Precedent	<NAME>, learned Counsel appearing for <ORG> urged the following submissions:  (1) The reservation provided for by Clause (4) of Article 16 is not in favour of backward citizens, but in favour of backward class of citizens.
Precedent	What is to be identified is backward class of citizens and not citizens who can be classified as backward.
Precedent	The homogeneous groups based on religion, race, caste, place of birth etc.
Precedent	can form a class of citizens and if that clas s is backward there can be a reservation in favour of that class of citizens.
Precedent	(2) Caste is a relevant consideration.
Precedent	It can even be the dominant consideration.
RulingByLowerCourt	Indeed, most of the lists prepared by the <ORG>s are prepared with reference to and on the basis of castes.
RulingByLowerCourt	They have been upheld by this Court.
RulingByLowerCourt	(3) Article 16(2) prohibits discrimination only on any or all of the grounds mentioned therein.
RulingByLowerCourt	A provision for protective discrimination on any of the said grounds coupled with other relevant grounds would not fall within the prohibition of Clause (2).
RulingByLowerCourt	In other words, if reservation is made in favour of backward class of citizens the bar contained in Clause (2) is not attracted, even if the backward classes are identified with reference to castes.
RulingByLowerCourt	The reason is that the reservation is not being made in favour of castes simplicitor but on the ground that they are backward castes/classes which are not adequately represented in the services of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	(4) The criteria of backwardness evolved by <ORG> ission is perfectly proper and unobjectionable.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has made an extensive investigation and has prepared a list of backward classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even if there are instances of under-inclusion or over-inclusion, such errors do not vitiate the entire exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	Moreover, whether a particular caste or class is backward or not and whether it is adequately represented in the services of the <ORG> or not are questions of fact and are within the domain of the executive decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	67.
RatioOfTheDecision	In paragraphs 33 to 42, we have noticed how this <ORG> has been grappling with the problem over the years.
Precedent	In <NAME>'s case, <ORG> of this <ORG> noticed the list of backward classes mentioned in Schedule III to the Madras Provincial and Subordinate Service Rules, 1942, as also the fact that backward classes were enumerated on the basis of caste/race.
Precedent	It found no objection thereto though in Champakam, rendered by the same <ORG> and on the same day it found such a classification bad under Article 15 on the ground that Article 15 did  not contain a clause corresponding to Clause (4) of Article 16.
Precedent	In <NAME>'s case this <ORG> observed that in respect of the vacancies reserved for backward classes of Hindus, the petitioner (a Brahmin) cannot have any claim inasmuch as "those reserved posts (were reserved) not on the ground of religion, race, caste etc.
Precedent	but because of the necessity for making a provision for reservation of such post in favour of a backward class of citizens."
Precedent	The writ petition was allowed on the ground that the allocation of vacancies to and among communities other than Harijans and backward classes of Hindus cannot be sustained in view of Clauses (1) and (2) of Article 16.
Precedent	68.
Precedent	Though <NAME> was not a case arising under Article 16(4 ), what it said about Article 15(4) came to be accepted as equally good and valid for the purpose of Article 16(4).
Precedent	The formulations enunciated with respect to Article 15(4) were, without question, applied and adopted in cases arising under Article 16(4).
Precedent	It is, therefore, necessary to no tice precisely the formulations in Balaji relevant in this behalf.
Precedent	<NAME>,J.
Precedent	speaking for <ORG> found, on an examination of the Nagangowda <ORG> Report, "that the <ORG> virtually equated the class with the castes."
Precedent	The learned Judge then examined the scheme of Article 15 , the meaning of the expression 'class', the importance of caste in the Hindu social structure and observed, while dealing with social backwardness:  Therefore, in dealing with the question as to whether any class of citizens is socially backward or not, it may not be irrelevant to consider the caste of the said group of citizens....
RatioOfTheDecision	Though the caste of the group of citizens may be relevant, its importance should not be exaggerated.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the classification of backward classes of citizens was based solely on the caste of the citizen, it may not always be logical and may perhaps contain the vice of perpetuating the caste themselves.
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned Judge further proceeded to hold:  Besides, if the caste of the group  of citizens was made the sole basis for determining the social backwardness of the said group, the test would inevitably break down in relation to many sections of Indian society which do not recognise castes in the conventional sense known to Hindu society.
RatioOfTheDecision	How is one going to decide whether Muslims, Christians or Jains or even Linguists are socially backward or not?
RatioOfTheDecision	The test of castes would be inapplicable to those groups, but that would hardly justify the exclusion of these groups in to to from the operation of Article 15(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not unlikely that in some States some Muslims or Christians or Jains forming groups may be socially backward.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is why we think that though castes in relation to Hindus may be a relevant factor to consider in determining the social backwardness of groups or class of citizens, it cannot be made the sole or the dominant test in that behalf.
Precedent	Social backwardness is in the ultimate analysis the result of poverty to a very large extent....
Precedent	It is true that social backwardness which  results form poverty is likely to be aggravated by considerations of caste to which the poor citizens may belong, but that only shows the relevance of both caste and poverty in determining the backwardness of citizens.
Precedent	The learned Judge stressed the part played by the occupation, conventional beliefs and place of habitation in determining the social backwardness.
Precedent	Inasmuch as the identification of backward classes of <ORG> was based almost solely on the basis of caste, it was held to be bad.
Precedent	The criticism of the Respondents' counsel against the Judgment runs thus: While it recognises the relevance and significance of the caste and the integral connection between caste, poverty and social backwardness, it yet refuses to accept caste as the sole basis of identifying socially backward classes, partly for the reason that castes do not exist among non-Hindus.
Precedent	The Judgment does not examine whether caste can or cannot form the starting- point of process of identification of socially backward classes.
Precedent	Nor does it consider the aspect - how does the non-existence of castes among non-Hindus (assuming that the said premise is factually true) makes it irrelevant in the case of Hindus, who constitute the bulk of the country's population.
Precedent	There is no rule of law that a test of basis adopted must be uniformly applicable to the entire population in the country as such.
Precedent	Before proceeding further it may be noticed that <NAME> was dealing with Article 15(4 ), which clause contains the qualifying words "socially and educationally" preceding the expression "backward classes".
Precedent	Accordingly, it was held that the backwardness contemplated by Article 15(4) is both social and educational.
Precedent	Though, Clause (4) of Article 16 did not contain any such qualifying words, yet they came to be read into it.
Precedent	In <NAME>, <NAME>, J. speaking for <ORG>, took it as "well-settled that the expression 'backward classes' in Article 16(4) means the same thing as the expression 'any socially and educationally backwa rd class of citizens' in Article 15(4)".
Precedent	More of this later.
Precedent	69.
Precedent	In Minor P.Rajendran, the caste vis-a-vis class debate took a sharp turn.
Precedent	The ratio in this case marks a definite and clear shift in emphasis.
Precedent	(We have dealt with it at some length in para 36).
Precedent	Suffice it to mention here that in this decision, it was held that "a caste is also a class of citizens and if the caste as a whole is socially and educationally backward reservation can be made in favour of such a caste on the ground that it is a socially and educationally backward class of citizens within the meaning of Article 15(4)....
Precedent	It is true that in the present case the list of socially and educationally backward classes has been specified by caste.
Precedent	But that does not necessarily mean that caste was a sole consideration and that persons belonging to these castes are also not a class of socially and educationally backward citizens."
Precedent	This principle was reiterated in <ORG> Balram and <ORG>.
Precedent	We have referred to these decisions at so me length in paras 38 and 39.
Precedent	In <NAME>, <NAME>,J.
Precedent	concluded, "a caste has always been recognised as a class."
Precedent	70.
Precedent	This issue was gone into in some detail in Vasant Kumar, where all the five Judges constituting <ORG> expressed different opinions.
Precedent	<NAME>,CJ.
Precedent	did not express himself on this aspect but other four learned Judges did.
Precedent	<NAME>, J. recognised that "in the early stages of the functioning of the Constitution, it was accepted without dissent or dialogue that caste furnishes a working criterion for identifying socially and educationally backward class of citizens for the purpose of Article 15(4)."
Precedent	He also recognised that "there has been some vacillation on the part of the judiciary on the question whether the caste should be the basis for recognising the backwardness."
Precedent	After examining the significance of caste in the Indian social structure, the learned Judge observed:  Social hierarchy and economic position exhibit an indisputable mutuality.
Precedent	The lower the caste, the poorer i ts member.
Precedent	The poorer the members of a caste, the lower the caste.
Precedent	Caste and economic situation, reflecting each other as they do are the Deus ex-Machina of the social status occupied and the economic power wielded by an individual or class in rural society.
Precedent	Social status and economic power are so woven and fused into the caste system in Indian rural society that one may without hesitation, say that if poverty be the cause, caste is the primary index of social backwardness, so that social backwardness is often readily identifiable with reference to a person's caste.
Precedent	The learned Judge also recognised that caste system has even penetrated other religions to whom the practice of caste should be anathema.
Precedent	He observed:  So sadly and oppressively deep-rooted is caste in our country that it has cut across even the barriers of religion.
Precedent	The caste system has penetrated other religious and dissentient Hindu sects to whom the practice of caste should be anathema and today we find that practitioners of other religious f aiths and Hindu dissentients are some times as rigid adherents to the system of caste as the conservative Hindus.
Precedent	We find Christians Harijans, Christian Madars, Christian Reddys, Christian Kammas, Mujbi Sikhs, etc.
Precedent	etc.
Precedent	In Andhra Pradesh there is a community known as Pinjars or Dudekulas (known in the North as 'Rui Pinjane Wala'): (professional cottonbeaters) who are really Muslims but are treated in rural society, for all practical purposes, as a Hindu caste.
Precedent	Several other instances may be given.
Precedent	Having thus noticed the pernicious effects of the caste system, the learned Judge opined that the only remedy in such a situation is to devise a method for determining social and educational backward classes without reference to caste.
Precedent	He stressed the significance of economic criterion and of poverty and concluded that a time has come when the economic criterion alone should be the basis for identifying the backward classes.
Precedent	Such an identification has the merit of advancing the secular character of the nation and w ill tend towards nullifying caste influence, said the learned Judge.
Precedent	71.
Precedent	<NAME>J.
Precedent	dealt with the question at quite some length.
Precedent	The learned Judge quoted <NAME>, according to whom the three dimensions of social inequality are class, status and power - and stressed the importance of poverty in this matter.
Precedent	Learned Judge opined that caste system is closely entwined with economic power.
Precedent	In the words of the learned Judge: Social status and economic power are so woven and fused into the caste system in Indian rural society that one may without hesitation, say that if poverty be the cause, caste is the primary index of social backwardness, so that social backwardness is often readily identifiable with reference to a person's caste.
Precedent	The learned Judge too recognised the percolation of caste system into other religions and concluded his opinion in the following words:  Poverty, caste, occupation and habitation are the principal factors which contribute to brand a class as socially backward....
Precedent	But mere p overty it seems is not enough to invite the Constitutional branding, because of the vast majority of the people of our country are poverty-struck but some among them are socially and educationally forward and others backward....
Precedent	True, a few members of those caste or social groups may have progressed far enough and forged ahead so as to compare favourably with the leading forward classes economically, socially and educationally.
Precedent	In such cases, perhaps an upper income ceiling would secure the benefit of reservation to such of those members of the class who really deserve it.... Class poverty, not individual poverty, is therefore the primary test.... Once the relevant conditions are taken into consideration and the backwardness of a class of people is determined, it will not be for the court to interfere in the matter.
Precedent	But, lest there be any misunderstanding, judicial review will not stand excluded.
Precedent	72.
Precedent	A.P.Sen,J.
Precedent	dealt with this question in a short opinion.
Precedent	According to him:  ....The predominant and only facto r for making special provisions under Article 15(4) or for reservation of posts and appointments under Article 16(4) should be poverty, and caste or a sub-caste or a group should be used only for purposes of identification of persons comparable' to Scheduled Castes or Scheduled Tribes, till such members of backward classes attain a state of enlightenment and there is eradication of poverty amongst them and they become equal partners in a new social order in our national life.
Precedent	73.
Precedent	"<NAME> too dealt with this aspect at some length.
Precedent	After examining the origins of the caste and the ugly practices associated with it, the learned Judge opined:  An examination of the question in the background of the Indian social conditions shows that the expression 'backward classes' used in the Constitution referred only to those who were born in particular castes, or who belonged to particular races or tribes or religious minorities which were backward.
Precedent	The learned Judge then referred to the debates in the Consti <ORG> on draft Article 10 and other allied articles, including the speech of Dr. <NAME> and observed thus:  The whole tenor of discussion in the Consti<ORG> pointed to making reservation for a minority of the population including Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes which were socially backward.
Precedent	During the discussion, the Constitution (First Amendment) Bill by which Article 15(4) was introduced, Dr. <NAME> referred to Article 16(4) and said that backward classes are 'nothing else but a collection of certain castes.
Precedent	This statement leads to a reasonable inference that this was the meaning which the Consti<ORG> assigned to classes' at any rate so far as Hindus were concerned.
Precedent	The learned Judge also supported the imposition of a means test as was done by <ORG> in <CITATION_1939903> .
Precedent	.
Precedent	The above opinions emphasise the integral connection between caste, occupation, poverty and social backwardness.
Precedent	They recognise that in the Indian context, lower castes are and ough t to be treated as backward classes.
Precedent	<NAME> and <NAME> (opinions of <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.)
Precedent	constitute important milestones on the road to recognition of relevance and significance of caste in the context of Article 16(4) and Article 15(4).
Precedent	74.
Precedent	At this stage, it would be fruitful to examine, how he words "caste" and "class" were understood in <ORG>.
Precedent	We shall first refer to various Rules in force in several parts of India, where these expressions were used and notice how were these expressions defined and understood.
Precedent	In the Madras Provincial and Subordinate Service Rules, 1942, framed by the Governor of Madras under Section 241(2)(b) read with 255 and 275 of the Government of India Act.
Precedent	1935, the expression "backward classes" was defined in Clause 3(A) of Rule 2.
Precedent	(The provinces of Madras at that time covered not only the present State of Tamil Nadu but also a major portion of the present State of Andhra Pradesh and parts of present States of Kerala and Karnataka.)
Precedent	Th e definition read as follows:  3(A).
Precedent	"Backward classes" means the communities mentioned in <ORG> III of this part.
Precedent	<ORG> III bore the heading "backward classes".
Precedent	It was a collection of castes and tribes under the sub-heading "race, tribe or caste."
Precedent	The backward classes in the <ORG> not only included the backward castes and tribes in Hindu religion but also certain sections of Muslims in the nature of castes.
Precedent	For example, item (23) in <ORG> III referred to 'Dudekula' who, as is well known, is a socially disadvantaged section of Muslims - in effect, a caste - pursuing the occupation of ginning and cleaning of cotton and preparing pillows and mattresses.
Precedent	In this connection, reference may be had to Chapter III - 'History of the Backward Classes Movement in Tamil Nadu' - of the Report of the Tamil Nadu Second Backward Classes Commission (1985), which inter alia refers to formation of '<ORG>, an association representing the various backward Hindu communities' in 1 934 and its demand for separate representation for them in services.
Precedent	The former <ORG> of Mysore was one of the earliest <ORG>s, where certain provisions were made in favour of Backward Classes.
Precedent	The opinion of <NAME>, (at pages 442-443) traces briefly the history of reservations in the <ORG> of Mysore from 1918-21 upto the re-organisation of <ORG>.
Precedent	The learned Judge points out how the expression 'backward classes' and 'backward communities' were used interchangeably.
Precedent	All the castes/communities' except Brahmins in the <ORG> were notified as backward communities/castes.
Precedent	As far back as 1921, preferential recruitment was provided in favour of "backward communities", in Government services.
Precedent	In Bombay province, <ORG>, <ORG> Resolution No.
Precedent	2610 dated 5.2.1925 defined "Backward Classes" as all except Brahmins, Prabhus, Marwaris, Parsis, Banyas and Christians.
Precedent	Certain reservations in Government service were provided for these classes.
Precedent	In 1930, the <ORG> C ommittee noticed the over-lapping meanings attached to the expressions "depressed classes" and "backward classes" and recommended that "Depressed Classes" should be used in the sense of untouchables, a usage which "will coincide with existing common practice."
Precedent	They proposed that the wider group should be called "Backward Classes", which should be subdivided into Depressed Classes (i.e., untouchables); Aboriginals and Hill Tribes; Other Backward Classes (including wandering tribes).
Precedent	They opined that the groups then currently called Backward Classes should be renamed "intermediate classes".
Precedent	In addition to 36 Depressed classes (approximate 1921 population 1.475 millions) and 24 Aboriginal and Hill Tribes (approximate 1921 population 1.323 millions), they listed 95 Other Backward Classes (approximate 1921 population 1.041 millions)".
Precedent	75.
Precedent	In the former princely State of Travancore, the expression used was "Communities", as would be evident from the Proceedings of the Government of His Highness the Maharaja of Tra vancore, contained in Order R. Dis.
Precedent	N. 893/general dated Trivandrum, 25th June, 1935.
Precedent	It refers to earlier orders on the subject as well.
Precedent	What is significant is that the expression "communities" was used as taking in Muslims and certain sections of Christians as well; it was not understood as confined to castes in Hindu social system alone.
Precedent	The operative portion of the order reads as follows:  ....Accordingly, Government have decided that all communities whose population is approximately 2 per cent of the total population of the <ORG> or about one lakh, be recognised as separate communities for the purpose of recruitment to the public service.
Precedent	The only exception from the above rule will be the Brahmin community who, though forming only 1.8 per cent of the total population, will be dealt with as a separate community.

Precedent	In the then <ORG>, the term "Backward Classes" was understood as covering both the untouchable classes as well other "Hindu Backward" classes.
Precedent	<NAME> says:  The <ORG> Hindu Backward Classes <ORG> (founded in 1929) submitted a memorandum which suggested that the term "Depressed" carried a connotation "of untouchability, in the sense of causing pollution by touch as in the case of Madras and Bombay" and that many communities were reluctant to identify themselves as depressed.
Precedent	The <ORG> suggested the term "'Hindu' Backward'" as a more suitable nomenclature.
Precedent	The list of 115 castes submitted included all candidates from the untouchable category as well as a stratum above.
Precedent	"All of the listed communities belong to non-Dwijas or degenerate or Sudra classes of the Hindus."
Precedent	They were described as low socially, educationally and ec onomically and were said to number over 60% of the population.
Precedent	The expression "depressed and other backward classes" occurs in the Objectives Resolution of the Constituent Assembly moved by <NAME> on December 13, 1946.
Precedent	76.
Precedent	We may also refer to a speech delivered by Dr. <NAME> on May 9, 1916 at the <ORG> university of New York, U.S.A. on the subject "castes in India: their mechanism, genesis and development" (the speech was published in Indian Antiquary-May 1917-Vol.XLI), which shows that as early as 1916, "class" and "caste" were used inter-changeably.
Precedent	In the course of the speech, he said:  ....society is always composed of classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be an exaggeration to assert the theory of class-conflict, but the existence of definite classes in a society is a fact.
RatioOfTheDecision	Their basis may differ.
RatioOfTheDecision	They may be economic or intellectual or social, but an individual in a society is always a member of a class.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is a universal fact and early Hindu society could not have been an exception to this rule, and, as  a matter of fact, we know it was not.
RatioOfTheDecision	If we bear this generalization in mind, our study of the genesis of caste would be very much facilitated, for we have only to determine what was the class that first made itself into a caste, for class and caste, so to say, are next door neighbours, and it is only a span that separates the two.
RatioOfTheDecision	A Caste is an Enclosed Class.
RatioOfTheDecision	A little later he stated:  We shall be well advised to recall at the outset that the Hindu society, in common with other societies, was composed of classes and the earliest known are the (1) Brahmins or the priestly class; (2) the Kshatriya, or the military class; (3) the Vaishya, or the merchant class and (4) the Shudra or the artisan and menial class.
RatioOfTheDecision	Particular attention has to be paid to the fact that this was essentially a class system, in which individuals, when qualified, could change their class, and therefore classes did change their personnel.
Precedent	At some time in the history of the Hindus, the priestly class socially detached itself from the res t of the body of people and through a closed-door policy became a caste by itself.
Precedent	The other classes being subject to the law of social division of labour underwent differentiation, some into large, others into very minute groups.
Precedent	77.
Precedent	In <ORG>, the following statement occurs under the heading "Slavery, Serfdom and Forced labour" under the sub-heading "servitude in Ancient India and China."
Precedent	- "castes in India."
RatioOfTheDecision	More abundant than slavery were serfdom.
RatioOfTheDecision	Within the rigid classification of social classes in ancient India, the Sudra caste was obliged to serve the Ksatriya, or warrior caste, the Brahmins, or priests, and the Vaisyas, or farmers, cattle raisers and merchants.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is an unbreakable barrier, however, separating these castes from the inferior Sudra caste, the descendants of the primitive indigenous people who lived in serfdom.
Precedent	In those times it was not a person's economic wealth that gave him his social rank but rather his social and racial level; and thus one of the Manu 's laws says" Although able, a Sudra must not acquire excess riches, since when a Sudra acquires a fortune, he vexes the Brahmans with his insolence."
Precedent	The barrier separating the servile castes took on extreme cruelty in some laws:  The legal condition of the Sudra left him only death as a means of improving his condition.
Precedent	In Legal Thesaurus (Regular Edition) the following meanings are given to the word "class":  Assortment, bracket, branch, brand, breed, caste, category, classification, classes, denomination, designation, division...; gradation, grade, group, grouping hierarchy.... sect, social rank, social status....
Precedent	The following meanings are given to the word "caste" in Webster's English Dictionary:  (1) a race, stock, or breed of men or animals (2): one of the hereditary classes into which the society of India is divided in accordance with a system fundamental to Hinduism, reaching back into distant antiquity, and dictating to every orthodox Hindu the rules and restrictions of all social intercourse and  of which each has a name of its own and special customs that restrict that occupation of its members and their intercourse with the members of the other classes (3)(a): a division or class of society comprised of persons within a separate and exclusive order based variously upon differences of wealth, inherited rank or privilege, profession, occupation... (b) the position conferred by caste standing.
Precedent	(4) a system of social stratification more rigid than a class and characterized by hereditary status, endogamy and social barriers rigidly sanctioned by custom law or religion.
Precedent	All the above material does go to show that in pre-Independence India, the expressions 'class' and 'caste' were used interchangeably and that caste was understood as an enclosed class.
Precedent	78.
Precedent	We may now turn to <ORG> debates with a view to ascertain the original intent underlying the use of words "backward class of citizens".
RatioOfTheDecision	At the outset we must clarify that we are not taking these debates or even the speeches of Dr. <NAME> kar as conclusive on the meaning of the expression "backward classes."
RatioOfTheDecision	We are referring to these debates as furnishing the context in which and the objective to achieve which this phrase was put in Clause (4).
RatioOfTheDecision	We are aware that what is said during these debates is not conclusive or binding upon the court because several members may have expressed several views, all of which may not be reflected in the provision finally enacted.
RatioOfTheDecision	The speech of Dr. <NAME> on this aspect, however, stands on a different footing.
RatioOfTheDecision	He was not only the Chairman of <ORG> which inserted the expression "backward" in draft Article 10(3) [ it was not there in the original draft Article 10(3 )], he was virtually piloting the draft Article.
RatioOfTheDecision	In his speech, he explains the reason behind draft Clause (3) as also the reason for which <ORG> added the expression "backward" in the clause.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this situation, we fail to understand how can anyone ignore his speech while trying to ascertain the meaning of the said ex pression.
RatioOfTheDecision	That the debates in <ORG> can be relied upon as an aid to interpretation of a constitutional provision is borne out by a series of decisions of this <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	See Madhu Limaye A.I.R.
RatioOfTheDecision	1969 S.C. 1014 at 1018; <ORG> (<NAME>, CJ.
RatioOfTheDecision	); opinion of <NAME>, CJ., in <CITATION_582704> and the several opinions in <ORG> where the relevance of these debates is pointed out, emphasising at the same time, the extent to which and the purpose for which they can be referred to).
RatioOfTheDecision	Since the expression "backward" or "backward class of citizens" is not defined in the Act, reference to such debates is permissible to ascertain, at any rate, the context, background and objective behind them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Particularly, where the <ORG> wants to ascertain the 'original intent' such reference may be unavoidable.
Precedent	79.
Precedent	According to Dr. <NAME> (his speech is referred in para 28 and need not be reproduced here), <ORG> was of the opinion that such a qualifying expression was nece ssary to indicate that the classes of citizens for whom reservations were to be made are those "communities which have not had so far representation in the <ORG>."
RatioOfTheDecision	It was also of the opinion that without such a qualifying expression (like 'backward') the "exemption made in favour of reservation will ultimately eat up the rule altogether".
RatioOfTheDecision	This was also the opinion of Sri <NAME>, who too was a member of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In his speech (referred to in para 27) he explains why the said qualifying expression "backward" was inserted by <ORG> in draft Article 10(3).
RatioOfTheDecision	His speech, in so far as it is relevant on this aspect, has been quoted in extenso in para 28 and need not be repeated here.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our opinion too, the words "class of citizens - not adequately represented in the services under the <ORG>" would have been a vague and uncertain description.
RatioOfTheDecision	By adding the word "backward" and by the speeches of Dr. <NAME> and Sri <NAME>, it was made clear that the "class of citizens...not adeq uately represented in the services under the <ORG>" meant only those classes of citizens who were not so represented on account of their social backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reference can also be made in this context to the speech of Dr. <NAME> in the <ORG> at the time the First Amendment to the Constitution was being enacted.
Precedent	It must be remembered that the <ORG> which enacted the First Amendment was the very same <ORG> which framed the Constitution and Dr. <NAME> as the Minister of Law was piloting the Bill.
Precedent	He said that backward classes "are nothing else but a collection of certain castes".
Precedent	(the relevant portion of his speech is referred to in papa 32) and that it was for those backward classes that Article 15(4) was being enacted.
Precedent	80.
Precedent	Pausing here, we may be permitted to make a few observations.
Precedent	The speeches of Dr. <NAME> may have to be understood in the context of the then obtaining ground realities viz., (a) Hindus constituted 84% of the total population of India.
Precedent	And among Hindus, caste  discrimination was unfortunately an unpleasant reality; (b) caste system had percolated even the Non-Hindu religions - no doubt to varying extents.
Precedent	Particularly among Christians in Southern India, who were converts from Hinduism, it was being practised with as much rabidity as it was among Hindus.
Precedent	(This aspect has been stressed by <ORG> (Chapter 12 paras 11 to 16) and has also been judicially recognised.
Precedent	(See, for instance, the opinions of <NAME> and <NAME>, JJ.
Precedent	in <NAME>).
Precedent	<ORG> refers to existence of castes among Muslims and Christians at pages 618 and 61.9.
Precedent	Among Muslims, it is pointed out, a distinction is made between 'Ashrats' (supposed to be descendants ascendants of Arab immigrants) and <ORG> (native converts).
RatioOfTheDecision	Both are divided into subgroups.
RatioOfTheDecision	Particularly, the <ORG>, who are converts from Hinduism, it is pointed out, practice caste system (including endogamy)" in a manner close to that of their Hindu counter-parts."
RatioOfTheDecision	All this  could not have been unknown to Dr. <NAME>, the keen social scientist that he was.
RatioOfTheDecision	(c) It is significant to notice that throughout his speech in <ORG>, Dr. <NAME> was using the word "communities" (and not 'castes') which expression includes not only the castes among the Hindus but several other groups.
RatioOfTheDecision	For example, Muslims as a whole were treated as a backward community in the princely State of Travancore besides several sections/denominations among the Christians.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "community" is clearly wider than "caste" - and "backward communities" meant not only the castes - wherever they may be found - but also other groups, classes and sections among the populace.
RatioOfTheDecision	81.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, there are very good reasons why the Constitution could not have used the expression "castes" or "caste" in Article 16(4) and why the word "class" was the natural choice in the context.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution was meant for the entire country and for all time to come.
RatioOfTheDecision	Non-Hindu religions like <ORG>, <ORG> and Sikh d id not recognise caste as such though, as pointed out hereinabove, castes did exist even among these religions to a varying degree.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further, a Constitution is supposed to be a permanent document expected to last several centuries.
RatioOfTheDecision	It must surely have been envisaged that in future many classes may spring-up answering the test of backwardness, requiring the protection of Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	It, therefore, follows that from the use of the word "class" in Article 16(4 ), it cannot be concluded either that "class" is antithetical to "caste" or that a caste cannot be a class or that a caste as such can never be taken as a backward class of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	The word "class" in Article 16(4 ), in our opinion, is used in the sense of social class - and not in the sense it is understood in Marxist jargon.
RatioOfTheDecision	In <ORG>, <ORG>, <NAME> and <NAME>, this reality was recognised and given effect to, notwithstanding the fact that they had to respect and operate within the rather qualified formulation of <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the sa ke of completeness, we may refer to a few passages from <NAME> to show what does the concept of 'caste' signify?
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME>, J. defines and describes "caste" in the following terms:  What then is a caste?
RatioOfTheDecision	Though caste has been discussed by scholars and jurists, no precise definition of the expression has emerged.
RatioOfTheDecision	A caste is a horizontal segmental division of society spread over a district or a region or the whole State and also sometimes outside it.
RatioOfTheDecision	Homo Hierarchicus is expected to be the central and substantive element of the caste/system which differentiate it from other social systems.
RatioOfTheDecision	The concept of purity and impurity conceptualises the caste system....
RatioOfTheDecision	There are four essential features of the caste system which maintained its homo hierarchicus character: (1) hierarchy (2) commensality: (3) restrictions on marriage; and (4) hereditary occupation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Most of the caste are endogamous groups.
RatioOfTheDecision	Intermarriage between two groups is impermissible.
RatioOfTheDecision	But 'Pratilom' marriages are not wholly known.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME>, <NAME> also defined "caste" in practically the same terms.
Precedent	He said:  A caste is an association of families which practice the custom of endogamy i.e.
Precedent	which permits marriages amongst the members belonging to such families only.
Precedent	Caste rules prohibit its members from marrying outside their caste.... A caste is based on various factOrs.
RatioOfTheDecision	Sometimes it may be a class, a race or a racial unit.
RatioOfTheDecision	A caste has nothing to do with wealth.
RatioOfTheDecision	The caste of a person is governed by his birth, in a family.
RatioOfTheDecision	Certain ideas of ceremonial purity are peculiar to each caste....
RatioOfTheDecision	Even the choice of occupation of members of castes was predetermined in many cases, and the members of particular caste were prohibited from engaging themselves in other types of callings, profession or occupations.
RatioOfTheDecision	Certain occupations were considered to be degrading or impure.
RatioOfTheDecision	82.
RatioOfTheDecision	The above material makes it amply clear that a caste is nothing but a social class - a socially homogeneous class.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also an occupational grouping, with this difference that its members hip is hereditary.
RatioOfTheDecision	One is born into it.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its membership is involuntary.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even if one ceases to follow that occupation, still he remains and continues a member of that group.
RatioOfTheDecision	To repeat, it is a socially and occupationally homogenous class.
RatioOfTheDecision	Endogamy is its main charateristic.
RatioOfTheDecision	Its social status and standing depends upon the nature of the occupation followed by it.
RatioOfTheDecision	Lowlier the occupation, lowlier the social standing of the class in the graded hierarchy.
RatioOfTheDecision	In rural India, occupation-caste nexus is true even today.
RatioOfTheDecision	A few members may have gone to cities or even abroad but when they return - they do, barring a few exceptions they go into the same fold again.
RatioOfTheDecision	It doesn't matter if he has earned money.
RatioOfTheDecision	He may not follow that particular occupation.
RatioOfTheDecision	Still, the label remains.
RatioOfTheDecision	His identity is not changed.
RatioOfTheDecision	For the purposes of marriage, death and all other social functions, it is his social class - the caste - that is relevant.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a matter of common knowledge that an overwhelming majority of doctors, engineers and other highly  qualified people who go abroad for higher studies or employment, return to India and marry a girl from their own caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even those who are settled abroad come to India in search of brides and bridegrooms for their sons and daughters from among their own caste or community.
RatioOfTheDecision	As observed by Dr. <NAME>, a caste is an enclosed class and it was mainly these classes <ORG> had in mind though not exclusively - while enacting Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	Urbanisation has to some extent broken this caste- occupation nexus but not wholly.
RatioOfTheDecision	If one sees around himself, even in towns and cities, a barber by caste continues to do the same job - may be, in a shop (hair dressing saloon).
RatioOfTheDecision	A washerman ordinarily carries on the same job though he may have a laundry of his own.
RatioOfTheDecision	May be some others too carry on the profession of barber or washerman but that does not detract from the fact that in the case of an over-whelming majority, the caste-occupation nexus subsists.
RatioOfTheDecision	In a rural context, of course, a member of barber caste  carrying on the occupation of a washerman or vice versa would indeed be a rarity - it is simply not done.
RatioOfTheDecision	There, one is supposed to follow his caste occupation, ordained for him by his birth.
RatioOfTheDecision	There may be exceptions here and there, but we are concerned with generality of the scene and not with exceptions or aberrations.
RatioOfTheDecision	Lowly occupation results not only in low social position but also in poverty; it generates poverty.
RatioOfTheDecision	'Caste-occupation-poverty' cycle is thus an ever present reality.
RatioOfTheDecision	In rural India, it is strikingly apparent; in urban centers, there may be some dilution.
RatioOfTheDecision	But since rural India and rural population is still the overwhelmingly predominant fact of life in india, the reality remains.
RatioOfTheDecision	All the decisions since Balaji speak of this 'caste-occupation-poverty' nexus.
RatioOfTheDecision	The language and emphasis may very but the theme remains the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is the stark reality notwithstanding all our protestations and abhorrence and all attempts at weeding out this phenomenon.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are not saying it ought to be encouraged.
Precedent	I t should not be.
Precedent	It must be eradicated.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is the ideal - the goal.
RatioOfTheDecision	But any programme towards betterment of these sections-classes of society and any programme designed to eradicate this evil must recognise this ground reality and attune its programme accordingly.
RatioOfTheDecision	Merely burying our heads in the sand - <NAME>-like - wouldn't help.
RatioOfTheDecision	One cannot fight his enemy without recognizing him.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> has said repeatedly, if race be the basis of discrimination - past and present - race must also form the basis of redressal programmes though in our constitutional scheme, it is not necessary to go that far.
RatioOfTheDecision	Without a doubt, an extensive restructuring of socio-economic system is the answer.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is indeed the goal, as would be evident from the preamble and Part IV (Directive Principles).
RatioOfTheDecision	But we are concerned here with a limited aspect of equality emphasised in Article 16(4) - equality of opportunity in public employment and a special provision in favour of backward class of citizens to enable them to achiev e it.
Precedent	(b).
Precedent	Identification of "backward class of citizens".
Precedent	83.
Precedent	Now, we may turn to the identification of "backward class of citizens".
RatioOfTheDecision	How do you go about it?
RatioOfTheDecision	Where do you begin?
RatioOfTheDecision	Is the method to very from <ORG> to <ORG>, region to region and from rural to urban?
RatioOfTheDecision	What do you do in the case of religions where caste system is not prevailing?
RatioOfTheDecision	What about other classes, groups and communities which do not wear the label of caste?
RatioOfTheDecision	Are the people living adjacent to cease-fire line (in Jammu and Kashmir) or hilly or inaccessible regions to be surveyed and identified as backward classes for the purpose of Article 16(4)?
RatioOfTheDecision	And so on and so forth are the many questions asked of us.
RatioOfTheDecision	We shall answer them.
RatioOfTheDecision	But our answers will necessarily deal with generalities of the situation and not with problems or issues of a peripheral nature which are peculiar to a particular <ORG>, district or region.
RatioOfTheDecision	Each and every situation cannot be visualised and answered.
RatioOfTheDecision	That must be left to the appropriate authorities appointed to identify.
RatioOfTheDecision	We can lay down only general guidelines.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the outset, we may state that for the purpose of this discussion, we keep aside the Scheduled Tribes and Scheduled Castes (since they are admittedly included within the backward classes), except to remark that backward classes contemplated by Article 16(4) do comprise some castes - for it cannot be denied that Scheduled Castes include quite a few castes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Coming back to the question of identification, the fact remains that one has to begin somewhere - with some group, class or section.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no set or recognised method.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no law or other statutory instrument prescribing the methodology.
RatioOfTheDecision	The ultimate idea is to survey the entire populace.
RatioOfTheDecision	If so, one can well begin with castes, which represent explicit identifiable social classes/groupings, more particularly when Article 16(4) seeks to ameliorate social backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is unconstitutional with it, more so when caste, occupation, poverty and social backwardness are so closely inter-twined in our society?
RatioOfTheDecision	[Individual survey is out of question, since Article 16(4) speaks of class protection and not individual protection].
RatioOfTheDecision	This does not mean that one can wind up the process of identification with the castes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Besides castes (whether found among Hindus or others) there may be other communities, groups, classes and denominations which may qualify as backward class of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	For example, in a particular State, Muslim community as a whole may be found socially backward.
RatioOfTheDecision	(As a matter of fact, they are so treated in the State of Karnataka as well as in the State of Kerala by their respective State Governments).
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, certain sections and denominations among Christians in Kerala who were included among backward communities notified in the former princely State of Travancore as far back as in 1935 may also be surveyed and soon and so forth.
RatioOfTheDecision	Any authority entrusted with the task of identifying backward classes may well start with the castes.
RatioOfTheDecision	It can take caste 'A', apply the criteria of backwardness evolved by it t o that caste and determine whether it qualifies as a backward class or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	If it does qualify, what emerges is a backward class, for the purposes of Clause (4) of Article 16.
RatioOfTheDecision	The concept of 'caste' in this behalf is not confined to castes among Hindus.
RatioOfTheDecision	It extends to castes, wherever they obtain as a fact, irrespective of religious sanction for such practice.
RatioOfTheDecision	Having exhausted the castes or simultaneously with it, the authority may take up for consideration other occupational groups, communities and classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	For example, it may take up the Muslim community (After excluding those sections, castes and groups, if any, who have already been considered) and find out whether it can be characterised as a backward class in that State or region, as the case may be.
RatioOfTheDecision	The approach may differ from State to State since the conditions in each State may differ.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nay, even within a State, conditions may differ from region to region.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, Christians may also be considered.
RatioOfTheDecision	If in a given place, like Kerala, there are severa l denominations, sections or divisions, each of these groups may separately be considered.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this manner, all the classes among the populace will be covered and that is the central idea.
RatioOfTheDecision	The effort should be to consider all the available groups, sections and classes of society in whichever order one proceeds.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since caste represents an existing, identifiable, social group spread over an overwhelming majority of the country's population, we say one may well begin with castes, if one so chooses, and then go to other groups, sections and classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	We may say, at this stage, that we broadly commend the approach and methodology adopted by <ORG> in this respect.
RatioOfTheDecision	We do not mean to suggest - we may reiterate - that the procedure indicated hereinabove is the only procedure or method/approach to be adopted.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, there is no such thing as a standard or model procedure/approach.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is for the authority (appointed to identify) to adopt such approach and procedure as it thinks appropriate,  and so long as the approach adopted by it is fair and adequate, the court has no say in the matter.
RatioOfTheDecision	The only object of the discussion in the preceding para is to emphasise that if a Commission/Authority begins its process of identification with castes (among Hindus) and occupational groupings among others, it cannot by that reason alone be said to be constitutionally or legally bad.
RatioOfTheDecision	We must also say that there is no rule of law that a test to be applied for identifying backward classes should be only one and/or uniform.
RatioOfTheDecision	In a vast country like India, it is simply not practicable.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the real object is to discover and locate backwardness, and if such backwardness is found in a caste, it can be treated as backward; if it is found in any other group, section or class, they too can be treated as backward.
RatioOfTheDecision	83A.
RatioOfTheDecision	The only basis for saying that caste should be excluded from consideration altogether while identifying the Backward Class of Citizens for the purpose of Article 16(4) is Clause (2) of Article 16.
RatioOfTheDecision	This arg ument, however, overlooks and ignores the true purport of Clause (2).
RatioOfTheDecision	It prohibits discrimination on any or all of the grounds mentioned therein.
RatioOfTheDecision	The significance of the word "any" cannot be minimised.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reservation is not being made under Clause (4) in favour of a 'caste' but a 'backward class'.
RatioOfTheDecision	Once a caste satisfies the criteria of backwardness, it becomes a backward class for the purposes of Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	Even that is not enough.
RatioOfTheDecision	It must be further found that that backward class is not adequately represented in the services of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such a situation, the bar of Clause (2) of Article 16 has no application whatsoever.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, the argument based upon secular nature of the Constitution is too vague to be accepted.
RatioOfTheDecision	It has been repeatedly held by <ORG> in School desegregation cases that if race be the basis of discrimination, race can equally form the basis of redressal.
RatioOfTheDecision	In any event, in the present context, it is not necessary to go to that extent.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is sufficient to say that the cl assification is not on the basis of the caste but on the ground that that caste is found to be a backward class not adequately represented in the services of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Born Heathen, by baptism, it becomes a Christian - to use a similie.
RatioOfTheDecision	Baptism here means passing the test of backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	84.
RatioOfTheDecision	Another contention urged is that only that group or section of people, who are suffering the lingering effects of past discrimination, can alone be designated as a backward class and not others.
RatioOfTheDecision	This argument, inspired by certain American decisions, cannot be accepted for more than one reason.
RatioOfTheDecision	Firstly, when the caste discrimination is still prevalent, more particularly in rural India (which comprises the bulk of the total population), the theory of lingering effects has no relevance.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where the discrimination has ended, does that aspect become relevant and not when the discrimination itself is continuing.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, as we have noticed hereinabove, the said theory has practically been given up by <ORG> in  <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this case, it is held sufficient for introducing and implementing a race-conscious programme that such programme serves important <ORG> objectives.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, according to this test, it is no longer necessary to prove that such programme is designed to compensate victims of past societal or governmental discrimination.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thirdly, the basic premise of the theory of lingering effects is not accepted by all the learned Judges of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	If one sees the opinion of <NAME>, <NAME> and of <NAME>, <NAME> <NAME> and <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would become evident.
RatioOfTheDecision	They also say that discriminatory practices against blacks and other minorities have not come to an end but are still persisting.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this country too, none can deny - in the face of the material collected by the various <ORG> including <ORG> - that discrimination persists even today in India.
RatioOfTheDecision	The representation of the socially backward classes in the Government apparatus is quite inadequate and that convers ely the upper classes have a disproportionately large representation therein.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is the lingering effect, if one wants to see it.
Precedent	Whether the backwardness in Article 16(4) should be both social and educational?
Precedent	85.
Precedent	The other aspect to be considered is whether the backwardness contemplated in Article 16(4) is social backwardness or educational backwardness or whether it is both social and educational backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	Since the decision in Balaji, it has been assumed that the backward class of citizens contemplated by Article 16(4) is the same as the socially and educationally backward classes, Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes mentioned in Article 15(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	Though Article 15(4) came into existence later in 1951 and Article 16(4) does not contain the qualifying words 'socially and educationally' preceding the words "backward class of citizens" the same meaning came to be attached to them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, it was stated in <NAME> (<NAME>,J.
RatioOfTheDecision	speaking for <ORG>) that:   Article 15(4) spea ks about socially and educationally backward classes of citizens."
RatioOfTheDecision	However, it is now settled that the expression "backward class of citizens" in Article 16(4) means the same thing as the expression "any socially and educationally backward class of citizens" in Article 15(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	In order to qualify for being called a 'backward class citizens' he must be a member of a socially and educationally backward class.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is social and educational backwardness of a class which is material for the purposes of both Article 15(4) and 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that no decision earlier to it specifically said so, yet such an impression gained currency and it is that impression which finds expression in the above observation.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our respectful opinion, however, the said assumption has no basis.
RatioOfTheDecision	Clause (4) of Article 16 does not contain the qualifying words "socially and educationally" as does Clause (4) of Article 15.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be remembered that Article 340 (which has remained unamended) does employ the expression 'socially and educati onally backward classes' and yet that expression does not find place in Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	The reason is obvious: "backward class of citizens" in Article 16(4) takes in Scheduled Tribes, Scheduled Castes and all other backward classes of citizens including the socially and educationally backward classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus, certain classes which may not qualify for Article 15(4) may qualify for Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	They may not qualify for Article 15(4) but they may qualify as backward class of citizens for the purposes of Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	It is equally relevant to notice that Article 340 does not expressly refer to services or to reservations in services under the <ORG>, though it may be that the <ORG> appointed thereunder may recommend reservation in appointments/posts in the services of the <ORG> as one of the steps for removing the difficulties under which <ORG> are labouring and for improving their conditions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus, S.E.B.Cs, referred to in Article 340 is only one of the categories for whom Article 16(4) was enacted; Article  16(4) applies to a much larger class than the one contemplated by Article 340.
RatioOfTheDecision	It would, thus, be not correct to say that backward class of citizens' in Article 16(4) are the same as the socially and educationally backward classes in Article 15(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	Saying so would mean and imply reading a limitation into a beneficial provision like Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	Moreover, when speaking of reservation in appointments/posts in the <ORG> services - which may mean, at any level whatsoever - insisting upon educational backwardness may not be quite appropriate.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further, if one keeps in mind the context in which Article 16(4) was enacted it would be clear that the accent was upon social backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	It goes without saying that in Indian context, social backwardness leads to educational backwardness and both of them together lead to poverty which in turn breeds and perpetuates the social and educational backwardness.
RatioOfTheDecision	They feed upon each other constituting a vicious circle.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is a well known fact that till independence the admini strative apparatus was manned almost exclusively by members of the 'upper' castes.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Shudras, the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes and other similar backward social groups among Muslims and Christians had practically no entry into the administrative apparatus.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was this imbalance which was sought to be redressed by providing for reservations in favour of such backward classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this sense Dr. <NAME> may be right when he says that the object of Article 16(4) was "empowerment" of the backward classes.
Precedent	The idea was to enable them to share the state power.
Precedent	We are, accordingly, of the opinion that the backwardness contemplated by Article 16(4) is mainly social backwardness.
Precedent	It would not be correct to say that the backwardness under Article 16(4) should be both social and educational.
Precedent	The Scheduled Tribes and the Scheduled Castes are without a doubt backward for the purposes of the clause; no one has suggested that they should satisfy the test of social and educational backwardness.
Precedent	It is neces sary to state at this stage that <ORG> appointed under Article 340 was concerned only with the socially and educationally backward classes contemplated by the said Article.
Precedent	Even so, it is evident that social backwardness has been given precedence over others by <ORG> - 12 out of 22 total points.
Precedent	Social backwardness - it may be reiterated - leads to educational and economic backwardness.
Precedent	No objection can be, nor is taken, to the validity and relevancy of the criteria adopted by <ORG>.
Precedent	For a proper a appreciation of the criteria adopted by <ORG> and the difficulties in the way of evolving the criteria of backwardness, one must read closely Chapters III and XI of Volume I along with Appendixes 12 and 21 in Volume II.
Precedent	Appendix XII is the Report of <ORG> while Appendix 21 is the 'Final List of Tables' adopted in the course of socio-educational survey.
Precedent	In particular, one may read paras 11.18 to 11.22 in Chapter  XI, which are quoted hereunder for ready reference:  11.18.
Precedent	<ORG> constituted <ORG> (Appendix-20, Volume II) to help the <ORG> prepare 'Indicators of Backwardness' for analysing data contained in computerised tables.
Precedent	After a series of meetings and a lot of testing of proposed indicators against the tabulated data, the number of tables actually required for the <ORG>'s work was reduced to 31 (Appendix-21 Volume II).
Precedent	The formulation and refinement of indicators involved testing and validation checks at every stage.
Precedent	11.19.
Precedent	In this connection, it may be useful to point out that in social sciences no mathematical formulae or precise bench-marks are available for determining various social traits.
Precedent	A survey of the above type has to read warily on unfamiliar ground and evolve its own norms and bench-marks.
Precedent	This exercise was full of hidden pitfalls and two simple examples are given below to illustrate this point.
Precedent	11.20.
Precedent	In <NAME>'s case <ORG> held that if a pa rticular community is to be treated as educationally backward, the divergence between its educational level and that of the <ORG> average should not be marginal but substantial.
Precedent	The <ORG> considered 50% divergence to be satisfactory.
Precedent	Now, 80% of the population of Bihar (1971 Census) is illiterate.
Precedent	To beat this percentage figure by a margin of 50% will mean that 120% members of a caste/class should be illiterates.
Precedent	In fact it will be seen that in this case even 25% divergence will stretch us to the maximum saturation point of 100%.
Precedent	11.21.
Precedent	In the Indian situation where vast majority of the people are illiterate, poor or backward, one has to be very careful in setting deviations from the norms as, in our conditions, norms themselves are very low.
Precedent	For example, Per Capita Consumer Expenditure for 1977-78 at current prices was Rs.
Fact	991 per annum.
Fact	For the same period, the poverty line for urban areas was at Rs.
Fact	900 per annum and for rural areas at Rs.
Fact	780.
Fact	It will be seen that this poverty line is quite close to the  Per Capita Consumer Expenditure of an average Indian.
Fact	Now following the dictum of <NAME> case, if 50% deviation from this average <ORG> was to be accepted to identify 'economically backward' classes, their income level will have to be 50% below the <ORG> i.e.
Fact	less than Rs.
Fact	495.5 per year.
Fact	This figure is so much below the poverty line both in urban and rural areas that most of the people may die of starvation before they qualify for such a distinction.
Fact	11.22.
Fact	In view of the above, 'Indicators for Backwardness' were tested against various cut-off points.
Fact	For doing so, about a dozen castes well-known for their social and educational backwardness were selected from amongst the castes covered by our survey in a particular State.
Fact	These were treated as 'Control' and validation checks were carried out by testing them against 'Indicators' at various cut-off points.
Fact	For instance, one of the 'Indicators' for social backwardness is the rate of student dropouts in the  age group 5-15 years as compared to the <ORG> average.
Fact	As a result of the above tests, it was seen that in educationally backward castes this rate is at least 25 per cent above the <ORG> average.
Fact	Further, it was also noticed that this deviation of 25% from the <ORG> average in the case of most of the 'Indicators' gave satisfactory results.
Fact	In view of this, wherever an 'Indicator' was based on deviation from the <ORG> average, it was fixed at 25%, because a deviation of 50% was seen to give wholly unsatisfactory results and, at times, to create anomalous situations.
Fact	It is after these paragraphs that the <ORG> sets out the indicators (criteria) evolved by it, set out in Paras 11.23 and 11.24 of the <ORG>.
Fact	102.
Fact	The S.E.B.Cs.
Fact	referred to by the impugned Memorandums are undoubtedly 'backward class of citizens' within the meaning of Article 16(4).
Argument	(d) 'Means' test and 'creamy layer':  86.
Argument	'Means test' in this discussion signifies imposition of an income limit, for the purpose of excluding persons (from the b ackward class) whose income is above the said limit.
Argument	This submission is very often referred to as "the creamy layer" argument.
Argument	Petitioners submit that some members of the designated backward classes are highly advanced socially as well as economically and educationally.
Argument	It is submitted that they constitute the forward section of that particular backward class - as forward as any other forward class member - and that they are lapping up all the benefits of reservations meant for that class, without allowing the benefits to reach the truly backward members of that class.
Argument	These persons are by no means backward and with them a class cannot be treated as backward.
Argument	It is pointed out that since <NAME>, almost every decision has accepted the validity of this submission.
Argument	On the other hand, the learned Counsel for the State of Bihar, Tamil Nadu, Kerala and other counsel for respondents strongly oppose any such distinction.
Argument	It is submitted that once a class is identified as a backward class after applying the relevan t criteria including the economic one, it is not permissible to apply the economic criteria once again and sub-divide a backward class into two sub-categories.
Argument	Counsel for the State of Tamil Nadu submitted further that at one stage (in July 1979) the State of Tamil Nadu did indeed prescribe such an income limit but had to delete it in view of the practical difficulties encountered and also in view of the representation received.
Argument	In this behalf, the learned Counsel invited our attention to Chapter 7-H (pages 60 to 62) of the <ORG> (Tamil Nadu Second Backward Classes Commission) Report.
Argument	According to the respondents the argument of 'creamy layer' is but a mere ruse, a trick, to deprive the backward classes of the benefit of reservations.
Argument	It is submitted that no member of backward class has come forward with this plea and that it ill becomes the members of forward classes to raise this point.
Precedent	Strong reliance is placed upon the observations of <NAME>, J. in <NAME>, to the following  effect:  ... One must, however, enter a caveat to the criticism that the benefits of reservation are often snatched away by the top creamy layer of backward class or caste.
Precedent	That a few of the seats and posts reserved for backward classes are snatched away by the more fortunate among them is not to say that reservation is not necessary.
Precedent	This is bound to happen in a competitive society such as ours.
Precedent	Are not the unreserved seats and posts snatched away, in the same way, by the top creamy layers amongst them on the same principle of merit on which the non reserved seats are taken away by the top layers of society.
Precedent	How can it be bad if reserved seats and posts are snatched away by the creamy layer of backward classes, if such snatching away of unreserved posts by the top creamy layer of society itself is not bad?
RatioOfTheDecision	In our opinion, it is not a question of permissibility or desirability of such test but one of proper and more appropriate identification of a class - a backward class.
RatioOfTheDecision	The very concept of a class denotes  a number of persons having certain common traits which distinguish them from the others.
RatioOfTheDecision	In a backward class under Clause (4) of Article 16 , if the connecting link is the social backwardness, it should broadly be the same in a given class.
RatioOfTheDecision	If some of the members are far too advanced socially (which in the context, necessarily means economically and, may also mean educationally) the connecting thread between them and the remaining class snaps.
RatioOfTheDecision	They would be misfits in the class.
RatioOfTheDecision	After excluding them alone, would the class be a compact class.
RatioOfTheDecision	In fact, such exclusion benefits the truly backward.
RatioOfTheDecision	Difficulty, however, really lies in drawing the line - how and where to draw the line?
RatioOfTheDecision	For, while drawing the line, it should be ensured that it does not result in taking away with one hand what is given by the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	The basis of exclusion should not merely be economic, unless, of course, the economic advancement is so high that it necessarily means social advancement.
RatioOfTheDecision	Let us illustrate the point.
RatioOfTheDecision	A member of backward  class, say a member of carpenter caste, goes to Middle East and works there as a carpenter.
RatioOfTheDecision	If you take his annual income in rupees, it would be fairly high from the Indian standard.
RatioOfTheDecision	Is he to be excluded from the Backward Class?
RatioOfTheDecision	Are his children in India to be deprived of the benefit of Article 16(4)?
RatioOfTheDecision	Situation may, however, be different, if he rises so high economically as to become - say a factory owner himself.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such a situation, his social status also rises.
RatioOfTheDecision	He himself would be in a position to provide employment to others.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such a case, his income is merely a measure of his social status.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even otherwise there are several practical difficulties too in imposing an income ceiling.
RatioOfTheDecision	For example, annual income of Rs.
RatioOfTheDecision	36,000 may not count for much in a city like Bombay, Delhi or Calcutta whereas it may be a handsome income in rural India anywhere.
RatioOfTheDecision	The line to be drawn must be a realistic one.
RatioOfTheDecision	Another question would be, should such a line be uniform for the entire country or a given <ORG> or should it diffe r from rural to urban areas and so on.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further, income from agriculture may be difficult to assess and, therefore, in the case of agriculturists, the line may have to be drawn with reference to the extent of holding.
RatioOfTheDecision	While the income of a person can be taken as a measure of his social advancement, the limit to be prescribed should not be such as to result in taking away with one hand what is given with the other.
RatioOfTheDecision	The income limit must be such as to mean and signify social advancement.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time, it must be recognised that there are certain positions, the occupants of which can be treated as socially advanced without any further enquiry.
Argument	For example, if a member of a designated backward class becomes a member of <ORG> or <ORG> or any other <ORG>, his status in society (social status) rises; he is no longer socially disadvantaged.
Argument	His children get full opportunity to realise their potential.
Argument	They are in no way handicapped in the race of life.
Argument	His salary is also such that he is above want.
Argument	I t is but logical that in such a situation, his children are not given the benefit of reservation.
Precedent	For by giving them the benefit of reservation, other disadvantaged members of that backward class may be deprived of that benefit.
Precedent	It is then argued for the Respondents that 'one swallow doesn't make the summer', and that merely because a few members of a caste or class become socially advanced, the class/caste as such does not cease to be backward.
Precedent	It is pointed out that Clause (4) or Article 16 aims at group backwardness and not individual backwardness.
Precedent	While we agree that Clause (4) aims at group backwardness, we feel that exclusion of such socially advanced members will make the 'class' a truly backward class and would more appropriately serve the purpose and object of Clause (4).
Precedent	(This discussion is confined to Other Backward Classes only and has no relevance in the case of Scheduled Tribes and Scheduled Castes).
Precedent	Keeping in mind all these considerations, we direct <ORG> to specify the basi s of exclusion - whether on the basis of income, extent of holding or otherwise - of 'creamy layer'.
Precedent	This shall be done as early as possible, but not exceeding four months.
Precedent	On such specification persons falling within the net of exclusionary rule shall cease to be the members of the Other Backward Classes (covered by the expression 'backward class of citizens') for the purpose of Article 16(4).
Precedent	The impugned Office Memorandums dated 13th August, 1990 and 25th September, 1991 shall be implemented subject only to such specification and exclusion of socially advanced persons from the backward classes contemplated by the said O.M.
Precedent	In other words, after the expiry of four months from today, the implementation of the said O.M.
Precedent	shall be subject to the exclusion of the 'creamy layer' in accordance with the criteria to be specified by <ORG> and not otherwise.
Precedent	(c) Whether a class should be situated similarly to the Scheduled Caste/Scheduled Tribe for being qualified as a Backward Class?
Precedent	87.
Precedent	In Balaji  it was held "that the backward classes for whose improvement special provision is contemplated by Article 15(4) are in the matter of their backwardness comparable to Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes."
Precedent	The correctness of this observation is questioned by the counsel for the respondents.
