Precedent	In passing, it may be stated that this <ORG> while reversing the judgment of the Punjab and Haryana High <ORG> in favour of the appellant <CITATION_1289642> .
Precedent	upheld the reservation which was made not by a legislation but by an executive order.
Precedent	See also <CITATION_809499> .
Precedent	309.
Precedent	Agreeing with the reasonings of <NAME>, I hold that the provision or reservation in the "Services under the State" under Article 16(4) can be made by an executive order.
Precedent	310.
Precedent	Whether the power conferred under Article 16(4) is coupled with duty?
Precedent	311.
Precedent	Mr. <NAME> put forth an argument that the enabling power conferred under Article 16(4) is intended for the benefit of the 'backward classes o f citizens' who in the opinion of the <ORG> are not adequately represented in the <ORG> under the <ORG> and that the power is one coupled with a duty and, therefore, has to be exercised by the state for the benefit of those for whom it is intended.
Precedent	Reference was made to H.W.R.
Precedent	Wade Administrative Law v. Edn.
Precedent	Pages 228 and 229.
Precedent	<ORG> Vol.
Precedent	V paras page 34 para 27 and page 35 para 29.
Precedent	He adds that the duty caused on the <ORG> is to be exercised in keeping with the directive principles laid down under Article 46 to promote with special care the educational and economic interests of the weaker sections of the people and, in particular, of the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes and to protect them from social injustice and all other forms of exploitation.
Precedent	In this connection, attention was drawn to a few decisions of this <ORG>, namely, (1) <CITATION_269585> .
Precedent	SCR 536; (2) <CITATION_1887849> 964; (3) <CITATION_1194605> .
Precedent	<NAME> ; and (4) <NAME> (supra).
Precedent	311.
Precedent	Whether forma tion of opinion by <ORG> is subjective?
Precedent	312.
Precedent	The expression "in the opinion of the <ORG>" would mean the formation of opinion by the <ORG> which is purely a subjective process.
Precedent	It cannot be challenged in a Court on the grounds of propriety, reasonableness and sufficiency though such an opinion is required to be formed on the subjective satisfaction of the <ORG> whether the identified 'backward class of citizens' are adequately represented or not in the <ORG> under 'the <ORG>.
Precedent	But for drawing such requisite satisfaction, the existence of circumstances relevant to the formation of opinion is a sine quo non.
Precedent	If the opinion suffers from the vice of non-application of mind or formulation of collateral grounds or beyond the scope of Statute, or irrelevant and extraneous material then that opinion is challengeable.
Precedent	See (1) <CITATION_1321505>  SCR 519; (2) <CITATION_1565642>  Bom.
Precedent	147; (3) <CITATION_1475436> .
Precedent	SCR 308; (4) <CITATION_1748256> .
Precedent	Suppl.
Precedent	SCR 311 and (5) <CITATION_443837> .
Precedent	.
Precedent	313.
Precedent	In the prese nt case, nothing is shown that the opinion of the <ORG> as regards the inadequacy of representation in the <ORG> is vitiated on any of the grounds mentioned above.
Precedent	314.
Precedent	Whether the policy of <ORG> can be subjected to judicial review:  315.
Precedent	The action of the <ORG> in making provision for the reservation of appointments or posts in favour of any 'backward class of citizens' is a matter of policy of the <ORG>.
Precedent	What is best for the 'backward class' and in what manner the policy should be formulated and implemented bearing in mind the object to be achieved by such reservation is a matter for decision exclusively within the province of the <ORG> and such matters do not ordinarily attract the power of judicial review or judicial interference except on the grounds which are well settled by a catena of decisions of this Court.
Precedent	Reference may be made to (1) <CITATION_245074> ; (2) <CITATION_682224> .
Precedent	3 SCC 233; (3) D.C.M.
Precedent	v. S. Paramjit Singh ; (4) <CITATION_197599> .
Precedent	1988 Suppl.
Precedent	SCC 176; ( 5) <CITATION_1474119> ; (6) <CITATION_1270021>  4 SCC; (7) <CITATION_178640246> .
Precedent	2 SCC 459; (8) <CITATION_827604> ; (9) <CITATION_1730815> and Ors.
Precedent	; (10) <CITATION_1601447> and Ors.
Precedent	: and (11) <CITATION_968709> .
Precedent	2 SCR 375.
Precedent	316.
Precedent	To what extent can the reservation be made?
Precedent	317.
Precedent	The next baffling question relates to the permissible extent of reservation in appointments.
Precedent	318.
Precedent	It was for the first time that this <ORG> in Balaji has indicated broadly that the reservation should be less than 50% and the question how much less than 50% would depend on the relevant prevailing circumstances in each case.
Precedent	Though in Balaji, the issue in dispute related only to the reservation prescribed for admissions in the medical college from the educationally and socially backward classes, scheduled caste and scheduled tribes as being violative of Article 15(4 ), this <ORG> after expressing its view that it should be less than 50% observed further that "the provisions of Article 15(4) are similar to those of Article 16(4)....
Precedent	Therefore, what is true in regard to Article 15(4) is equally true in regard to Article 16(4)... reservation made under Article 16(4) beyond the permissible and legitimate limits would be liable to be challenged as a fraud on the Constitution."
Precedent	This decision has gone further holding that the reservation of 68% seats made in that case was offending Article 15(4) of the Constitution.
Precedent	To say in other words, <NAME> has fixed that the maximum limit of reservation all put together should not exceed 50% and if it exceeds, it is nothing but a fraud on the Constitution.
Precedent	Even at the threshold, I may emphatically state that I am unable to agree with the proposition fixing the reservation for SEBCs at 50% as the maximum limit.
Precedent	319.
Argument	Mr. <NAME> strongly articulated that the observation in <NAME> that reservation under Article 16(4) should not be beyond 50% is only an obiter dicta since that question did not at all arise for consideration in that case.
Argument	Therefore, according to him, this observation is not a law declare d by <ORG> within the meaning of Article 141 of the Constitution.
Argument	He continued to state that unfortunately some of the subsequent decision have mistakenly held as if the question of permissible limit has been settled in Balaji while, in fact, the view expressed in it was an obiter dicta.
Argument	According to him, the policy of reservation is in the nature of affirmative action, firstly to eliminate the past inhuman discrimination and secondly to ameliorate the sufferings and reverse the genetic damage so that the people belonging to 'backward class' can be uplifted.
Precedent	When it is the main objective of Clause (4) of Article 16 any limitation on reservation would defeat the very purpose of this Article falling under Fundamental Rights and, therefore, reservation if the circumstances so warrant can go even upto 100%.
Precedent	320.
Precedent	This view of Mr. <NAME> has been fully supported by Mr. <NAME> appearing on behalf of the State of Tamil Nadu who pointedly referred to the speech of the Chief Minister of Tamil  Nadu made in the Chief Ministers' Conference held on 10th April 1992 and produced a copy of the printed speech of the Chief Minister, issued by <ORG> as an annexure to the written submission.
Precedent	It is seen from the said annexure that the Chief Minister has categorically emphasised the stand of <ORG> stating that the total reservation for backward classes, scheduled castes and scheduled tribes is 69%; that it is but fair and proper that socially and educationally backward classes (alone) as a whole should be given at least 50% reservation for employment opportunities in <ORG> services and its undertakings as well as for admission in educational institutions run by the <ORG>.
Precedent	It has also been pointed out that in consonance with this avowed policy, <ORG> passed unanimously a resolution on 30.9.1991 urging <ORG> to adopt a policy of 50% reservation for the Backward Classes instead of 27% and to ap ply this reservation not only for employment opportunities in all <ORG> departments and <ORG>, but also for admission in all Educational Institutions run by the <ORG>.
Precedent	321.
Precedent	Mr. <NAME> appearing in W.P.
Precedent	No.
Precedent	1094/91 submits that the limits to the reservation in Article 16(4) cannot be fixed on percentage but it must be with the ulterior objective of achieving adequate representation for 'backward classes'.
RatioOfTheDecision	322.
RatioOfTheDecision	I see much force in the above submissions and hold that any reservation in excess of 50% for 'backward classes' will not be violative of Articles 14 and/or 16 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	But at the same time, I am of the view that such reservations made either under Article 16(4) or under Article 16(1) and (4) cannot be extended to the totality of 100%.
Precedent	In fact, my learned brother, <NAME>, J in his separate judgment has also expressed a similar view that "there is no legal infirmity in keeping the reservations under Clause (4) alone or under Clause (4)  and Clause (1) of Article 16 together exceeding 50 per cent" though for other reasons the learned Judge has concluded that ordinarily the reservations kept under Article 16(1) and 16(4) together should not exceed 50% of the appointments in a cadre or service in any particular year, but for extraordinary reasons this percentage may be exceeded.
Precedent	My learned brother, <NAME>, J in his separate judgment has expressed his view that in given circumstances, some relaxation in the strict rule of reservation may become imperative and added that in doing so extreme caution is to be exercised and a special case made out.
Precedent	323.
Precedent	As to what extent the proportion of reservation will be so excessive as to render it bad must depend upon adequacy of representation in a given case.
Precedent	Therefore, the decisions fixing the percentage of reservation only upto the maximum of 50% are unsustainable.
Precedent	The percentage of reservation at the maximum of 50% is neither based on scientific data nor on any established and agreed formula.
Precedent	In fact, Article 16(4) itself does not limit the power of the <ORG> in making the reservation to any maximum pecentage; but it depends upon the quantum of adequate representation required in the <ORG>.
Precedent	In this context, it would be appropriate to recall some of the decisions of this <ORG>, not agreeing with <NAME> as regards the fixation of percentage of reservation.
Precedent	324.
Precedent	The question of percentage of reservation was examined in <NAME> wherein <NAME>, J not agreeing with <NAME> has observed thus:  ....
Precedent	Clause (4) of Article 16 does not fix any limit on the power of the <ORG> to make reservation.
Precedent	Since Clause (4) is a part of Article 16 of the Constitution it is manifest that the <ORG> cannot be allowed to indulge in excessive reservation so as to defeat the policy contained in Article 16(1).
Precedent	As to what would be a suitable reservation within permissible limits will depend upon the facts and circumstances of each case and no hard and fast rule can be laid down, nor can this matter be reduced to  a mathematical formula so as to be adhered to in all cases.
Precedent	Decided cases of this <ORG> have no doubt laid that the percentage of reservation should not exceed 50%.
Precedent	As I read the authorities, this is, however, a rule of caution and does not exhaust all categories.
Precedent	Suppose for instance a <ORG> has a large number of backward classes of citizens which constitute 80% of the population and the <ORG>, in order to give them proper representation, reserves 80% of the jobs for them, can it be said that the percentage of reservation is bad and violates the permissible limits of Clause (4) of Article 16?
Precedent	The answer must necessarily be in the negative.
Precedent	The dominant object of this provision is to take steps to make in adequate representation adequate.
Precedent	325.
Precedent	<NAME> in the same decision has agreed with the above view of <NAME>, J stating that "...the arithmetical limit of 50% in any one year set by some earlier rulings cannot perhaps be pressed too far."
Precedent	326.
Precedent	Though <NAME>, J did not specifically deal wit h this maximum limit of reservation, nevertheless the tenor of his judgment indicates that he did not favour 50% rule.
Precedent	327.
Precedent	<NAME>, J in <NAME> case [1981] 2 SCR 185 (supra) has expressed his view on the ceiling of reservation as follows:  ....
Precedent	There is no fixed ceiling to reservation or preferential treatment in favour of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes though generally reservation may not be far in excess of fifty percent.
Precedent	There is no rigidity about the fifty percent rule which is only a convenient guideline laid down by Judges.
Precedent	Every case must be decided with reference to the present practical results yielded by the application of the particular rule of preferential treatment and not with reference to hypothetical results which the application of the rule may yield in the future.
Precedent	Judged in the light of this discussion I am unable to find anything illegal or unconstitutional in any one of the impugned orders and circulars....  328.
Precedent	Again in <NAME>, <NAME>, J reiterate s his view taken in <NAME> in the following words:  We must repeat here, what we have said earlier, that there is no scientific statistical data or evidence of expert administrators who have made any study of the problem to support the opinion that reservation in excess of 50 per cent may impair efficiency.
Precedent	329.
Precedent	I fully share the above views of <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, JJ holding that no maximum percentage of reservation can be justifiably fixed under Articles 15(4) and/or 16(4) of the Constitution.
Precedent	330.
Precedent	It should not be out of place to recall the observation of <NAME>, J in <NAME> observing," The extent of reservation to be made is primarily a matter for the <ORG> to decided.
Precedent	By this we do not mean to say, that the decision of the <ORG> is not open to judicial review....
Precedent	The length of the leap to be provided depends upon the gap to be covered.
Precedent	(emphasis supplied)  331.
Precedent	Desai, J in <NAME> expressed his view that in dealing with the question of reservation in favour of Scheduled  Castes, Scheduled Tribes as well as other <ORG> 'Judiciary retained its traditional blindfold on its eyes and thereby ignored perceived realities."
Precedent	Whether the further arbitrary classification as poorer sections' from and out of the identified <ORG> is permissible under Article 16(4) after acceptance and approval of the list without reservation and whether such classification suffers from non-application of mind?
Precedent	332.
Precedent	The most important pivotal and crucial issue that I would now like to ponder over relates to the intent of para 2(i) of the OM dated 25th September 1991 whereunder it is declared that "Within the 27% of the vacancies in civil posts and services under <ORG> reserved for <ORG>, preference will be given to the candidates belonging to the poorer sections of the <ORG>.
Precedent	In case sufficient number of such candidates are not available, unfilled vacancies shall be filled by the other <ORG> candidates".
Precedent	(emphasis supplied)  333.
Precedent	To say in other words, the Government intends to prescrib e an income ceiling for determination of 'poorer sections' of the <ORG> who will be eligible to avail of the preference of reservation of appointments or posts in the <ORG> under the <ORG>.
Precedent	It is an admitted fact that the Government so far has not laid down any guideline or test for identifying and ascertaining the 'poorer sections' among the identified <ORG>.
Precedent	334.
Precedent	The <ORG> has specifically used the expression, 'poorer sections' but not 'weaker sections' as contemplated under Article 46 of the Constitution.
Precedent	Though the expressions 'poorer sections' and 'weaker sections' may connote in general 'the disadvantaged position of a section of the people' they do not convey one and the same meaning and they are not synonymous.
Precedent	When the <ORG> deliberately uses the expression 'pooer sections', it has become incumbent to examine what that expression means and whether there can be any sub-classification as 'poorer' and 'non-poorer' among the same category of potential backward class of citizens on the anvil of economic cri terion.
Precedent	335.
Precedent	The word 'poor' lexically means "having little or no money, goods or other means of support" (<ORG>) or "lacking financial or other means of subsistence" (<ORG>).
Precedent	336.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> uses the expression 'poorer' in its comparative term for the word 'poor'.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is common knowledge that the superlative term for the word 'poor' is 'poorerst'.
RatioOfTheDecision	The very usage of the word 'poorer' is in comparison with the positive word 'poor'.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, it, necessarily follows that the <ORG> firstly considers all the identified <ORG> in general as belonging to 'poor sections' from and out of which the 'poorer sections' are to be culled out by applying a test to be yet formulated by the <ORG> evidently on economic criterion or by application of poverty test based on the ceiling of income.
RatioOfTheDecision	After the segregation of 'poorer sections' of the <ORG>, the left out would be the 'poor sections'.
Precedent	By the use of the word 'poorer', the <ORG> is super-imposing a relative p overty test for identifying and determining a preferential class among the identified <ORG>.
Precedent	It is stated that the preference will be given first to the 'poorer sections' and only in case there are unfilled vacancies, those vacancies will be filled by the left out <ORG>, namely, those other than the poorer sections.
Precedent	In other words, it means that all the identified <ORG> do not belong to affluent sections but to poor and poorer sections, that the expression 'poorer sections' denotes only the economically weaker sections of <ORG> compared with the remaining same category of <ORG> and that those, other than the 'poorer sections' although socially and educationally backward are economically better off compared with the 'poorer sections'.
Precedent	The view that all the identified <ORG> are considered as 'poor' or 'poorer' is fortified by the fact that there is an inbuilt explanation in the amended OM itself to the effect that those who do not fall within the category of 'poorer sections' also will be entitled for the benef it of reservation but of course subject to the availability of unfilled vacancies.
Precedent	337.
Precedent	An argument was advanced that for identifying 'poorer sections', the.
Precedent	'means test' signifying an imposition of outer income limit should be applied and those who are above the cut off income limit should be excluded so that the better off sections of the <ORG> may be prevented from taking the benefit earmarked for the less fortunate brethren and the only genuine and truly members of 'poorer sections' of <ORG> may avail the benefit of reservation.
Precedent	In support of this argument, an attempt has been made to draw strength on two decisions of this <ORG> rendered in <NAME> and <NAME>.
Precedent	338.
Precedent	Chief Justice <NAME> in <NAME> seems to have been inclined to take the view that reservation of seats in educational institutions should not be allowed to be enjoyed by the rich people suffering from the same communal disabilities.
Precedent	339.
Precedent	<NAME>, J in <NAME> recognises this 'means test' saying that "an upper income cei ling would secure the benefit of reservation to such of those members of the class who really deserved it", with which view <NAME>, J (as the learned Chief Justice than was) has agreed.
Precedent	340.
Precedent	Thus the above argument based on 'means test' though seems to be plausible at the first sight is, in my opinion, not well founded and must be rejected on the ground that the identified category of SEBCs, having common characteristics or attributes - namely the potential social backwardness cannot be bisected or further classified by applying the economic or poverty test.
Precedent	341.
Precedent	A doubt has been created as to whether the word 'poorer' connotes economic status or social status or is to be understood in any other way.
Precedent	342.
Precedent	The word 'poorer' when examined in the context in which it is deployed both syntactically and etymologically, in my view, may not convey any other meaning except relative poverty or comparative economic status.
Precedent	If any other meaning is imported which the government evidently appears to have not cont emplated, virtually one will be rewriting the second OM.
Precedent	343.
Precedent	An order of <ORG> dated 1st October 1991 clearly spells out that that <ORG> was of the view that 'poorer sections' are to be identified by the economic criterion.
Precedent	The relevant portion of the above Order reads as follows:  The matters are adjourned to 31st October 1991 when learned Additional Solicitor General will tell us how and when Government would be able to give the list of the economic criteria referred to in the notification of 25th September 1992.
Precedent	(emphasis supplied)  344.
Precedent	The same view is reflected in a subsequent Order dated 4th December 1991 made by this nine-Judges <ORG>, the relevant part of which reads thus:  Learned Additional Solicitor General states that the Government definitely expects to be able to fix the economic criteria by January 28, 1992.... As far as the question of stay granted by us earlier is concerned, we see no reason to pass any order at this stage as the petitions are posted for hearing on January  28, 1992 and in view of the economic criterion not being yet determined and other relevant circumstances, no question of immediate implementation of the notification arises.
Precedent	(emphasis supplied)  345.
Precedent	The above Orders of this Court support my view that the <ORG> has to identify the 'poorer sections' only by the economic criteria or by the application of poverty test otherwise called 'means test'.
Precedent	It appears that this Court has all along been given to understand that 'poorer sections' will be tested by the <ORG> on economic criterion.
Precedent	346.
Precedent	The above view is further fortified by the very fact that the second OM providing 10% of the reservation 'for economically backward sections of the people not covered by any other scheme of the reservation' indicates that the <ORG> has taken only the economic criteria in making the classification of the various sections of the people (emphasis supplied).
Precedent	Therefore, I proceed on the basis that the second OM identifies the 'poorer sections' only on the basis of  economic status.
Precedent	347.
RatioOfTheDecision	When the 'means test' is analysed in depth so as to explore its merits and demerits, one would come to an inevitable conclusion that it is not a decisive test but on the other hand it will serve as a protective umbrella for many to get into this segregated section by adopting all kinds of illegal and unethical methods.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further, this test will be totally unworkable and impracticable in the determination of "getting somebody in and getting somebody out" from among the same identified <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	If this 'means test' argument is accepted and put into action by scanning the identified <ORG> by applying a super-imposition test, the very object and purpose of reservation, intended for the socially backward class would reach only a cul-de-sac and the identified <ORG> would be left in a maze.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my considered opinion, it will be a futile exercise for the courts to find out the reasons in support of the division between and among the group of <ORG> and make rule therefor, for multiple reasons, a few  of which which I am enumerating hereunder.
RatioOfTheDecision	(1) The division among the identified and ascertained SEBCs having common characteristics and attributes - the primary of which being the potential social backwardness, as 'poorer sections' and 'non-poorer sections' on the anvil of economic criterion or by application of a superimposition test of relative poverty is impermissible as being opposed to the scope and intent of Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	(2) If <ORG> puts its seal of approval to para 2(i) of the second OM whereunder a section of the people under the label of 'poorer sections' is carved out from among the SEBCs, it becomes a law declared by this <ORG> for the entire nation under Article 141 of the Constitution and is binding on all the <ORG>s within the territory of India and that the decision of this <ORG> on a constitutional question cannot be over-ridden except by the constitutionally recognised norms.
RatioOfTheDecision	When such is the legal position, the law so declared should be capable of being effectively implemen ted in its applicability to some rare or freakish cases.
RatioOfTheDecision	The law should not be susceptible of being abused or misused and leave scope for manipulation which can remain undetected.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the law so declared by this <ORG> is indecisive and leaves perceivable loopholes, by the aid of which one can defeat or circumvent or nullify that law by adopting an insidious, tricky, fraudulent and strategic device to suit one's purpose then that law will become otiose and remain as a dead letter.
RatioOfTheDecision	I would like to indicate the various reasons in support of my opinion that this process of elimination or exclusion of a section of people from and out of the same category of SEBCs cannot be sustained leave apart the authority of the Government to take any decision and formulate its policy in its discretion or opinion provided that the policy is not violative of any constitutional or legal provisions or that discretion or opinion is not vitiated by non-application of mind, arbitrariness, formulation of collateral grounds or consider ation of irrelevant and extraneous material etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	(a) If the annual gross income of a government servant derived from all his sources during a financial year is taken as a test for identifying to 'poorer sections', that test could be defeated by reducing the income below the ceiling limit by a Government servant voluntarily going on leave on loss of pay for few months during that financial year so that he could bring his annual income within the ceiling limit and claim the benefit of reservation meant fo r 'poorer sections'.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, a person owning extensive land also may lay a portion of his land fallow in any particular year or dispose of a portion of his land so as to bring his agricultural income below the ceiling limit so that he may fall within the category of 'poorer sections'.
RatioOfTheDecision	(b) The fluctuating fortunes or misfortunes also will play an important role in determining whether one gets within the area of 'poorer sections' or gets out of it.
RatioOfTheDecision	(c) Take a case wherein there are two brothers belonging t o the same family of 'backward class' of whom one is employed in <ORG> service and another is privately employed or has chosen some other profession.
RulingByPresentCourt	The annual income of the <ORG> employee if slightly exceeds the ceiling limit, his children will not fall within the category of 'poorer sections' whereas the other brother can deceitfully show his income within the ceiling limit so that his children can enjoy that benefit.
RulingByPresentCourt	(d) Among the pensioners also, the above anomaly will prevail as pointed out in <NAME>.
RulingByPresentCourt	(e) Any member of <ORG> who is in <ORG> job and is on the verge of his superannuation and whose income exceeds the ceiling limit, will go out of the purview of 'poorer sections' but in the next financial year, he may get into the 'poorer sections' if his total pensionary benefits fall within the ceiling limit.
RulingByPresentCourt	(f) A person who is within the definition of 'poorer sections' may suddenly go out of its purview by any intervening fortuitous circumstances such as getting a marital allian ce in a rich family or by obtaining any wind-fall wealth.
RulingByPresentCourt	(g) If poverty test is made applicable for identifying the 'poorer sections' then in a given case wherein a person is socially oppressed and educationally backward but economically slightly advanced in a particular year, he will be deprived of getting the preferential treatment.
RulingByPresentCourt	The above are only by way of illustrations, though this type can be multiplied, for the purpose of showing that a person can voluntarily reduce his income and thereby circumvent the declared law of this Court.
RulingByPresentCourt	In all the above illustrations, enumerated as (a) to (g), the chance of "getting into or getting out of the definition of 'poorer sections' will be like a see-saw depending upon the fluctuating fortunes or misfortunes.
RulingByPresentCourt	(3) The income-test for ascertaining poverty may severally suffer from the vice of corruption and also encourage patronage and nepotism.
RulingByPresentCourt	(4) When the Government has accepted and approved the lists of SEBCs, identified by the test of social backwardness,  educational backwardness and economic backwardness which lists are annexed to the <ORG>, there is no justification by dividing the <ORG> into two groups, thereby allowing one section to fully enjoy the benefits and another on a condition only if there are unfilled vacancies.
RulingByPresentCourt	(5) The elimination of a section of <ORG> by putting an arbitrary and unnecessary unjustified.
RulingByPresentCourt	This process of elimination or exclusion of a section of <ORG> will be tentamount to pushing those persons into the arena of open competition along with the forward class if there are no unfilled vacancies out of the total 27% meant for <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	This will cause an irretrievable injustice to all the non-poorer sections though they are also theoretically declared as <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	(6) The second OM providing a scanning test is neither feasible nor practicable.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be perceptible and effectual only if the entire identified backward class enjoys the benefit of reservation.
RatioOfTheDecision	(7) The proposed 'means test' is highly impressionistic test, the result of wh ich is likely to be influenced by many uncertain and imponderable facts.
Precedent	(8) It may theoretically sound well but in practice attempts may be made in a underhanded way to get round the problem.
Precedent	348.
Precedent	What I have indicated above is only the tip of the iceberg and more of it is likely to surface at the time when any scanning process and super-imposition test are put into practice.
Precedent	349.
Precedent	In this connection, I would like to mention the views of <ORG> as expressed by the Chief Minister of Tamil Nadu in the Chief Ministers' Conference held in New Delhi (already referred to) stating that the application of income limit on reservation will exclude those people whose income is above the 'cut-off limit and literally, it means that they will come under the open competition quota and if caste is not the sole criterion, income limit cannot also be the decisive and determining factor for social backwardness and that the exclusion of certain people from the benefits of reservation by the application of e conomic criterion will not bring the desired effect for the advancement and improvement of the backward classes who have suffered deprivation from the time immemorial.
Precedent	350.
Precedent	Reference also may, be made to Balaji wherein it has been ruled that backward classes cannot be further classified into backward and more backward and that such a sub-classification "does not appear to be justified under Article 15(4)".
Precedent	This view, in my opinion, can be equally applied even for sub-classification under Article 16(4).
Precedent	351.
Precedent	Arguing with the above view of Balaji, I hold that the further sub-classification as 'poorer sections' out of the ascertained <ORG> after accepting that group in which the common thread of social backwardness runs through as an identifiable unit within the meaning of the expression 'backward class', is violative of Article 16(4).
Precedent	352.
Precedent	Of course, in <NAME>, <NAME>, J. in his separate judgment has taken a slightly contrary view, holding that there can be classification for providing some  reservation to the more backward classes compared to little more advanced backward classes.
Precedent	This view is expressed only by the learned Judge (<NAME>, J.)
Precedent	on which view other Judges of that Bench have not expressed any opinion.
Precedent	However, it appears that the learned Judge has not said that the entire reservation should go only to the more backward classes but only some percentage of reservation should be provided and earmarked exclusively for the more backward classes.
Precedent	353.
Precedent	In the present case, the entire reservation of 27 per cent is given firstly to be enjoyed by the 'poorer sections' and only the unfilled vacancies, if any, can be availed of by others.
Precedent	As I have already held, the view expressed by <ORG> in Balaji is more acceptable to me.
Precedent	354.
Precedent	It may not be out of place to mention here that in Tamil Nadu, based on one of the recommendations of <ORG> constituted in 1969 - known as '<ORG> - the Government issued orders in G.O.
Precedent	Ms. No .
Precedent	1156, <ORG>, dated 2nd July 1979, superimposing the income ceiling of Rs.
Precedent	9,000 per annum as additional criterion for the backward classes to be eligible for reservation for admission in educational institutions and recruitment to public services.
Precedent	This order was challenged before <ORG> but <ORG> by 2:1 upheld the G.O.
Precedent	However, the order provoked a considerable volume of public criticism.
Precedent	After an All-party meet, the Government in G.O.
Precedent	Ms. No.
Precedent	72, <ORG> dated 1st February 1980 revoked their orders and the position as it stood prior to 2nd July 1979 was restored.
Precedent	Simultaneously, by another G.O.
Precedent	Ms. No.
Precedent	73, <ORG> dated 1st February 1980, the Government raised the percentage of reservation for backward classes from 31 per cent to 50 per cent commensurate with the population of the backward classes in the <ORG>.
Precedent	Both the GOs i.e.
Precedent	G.O.
Precedent	Ms. No.
Precedent	72 and 73 dated 1st February 1980 were challenged in <ORG> in Writ Petition Nos.
Precedent	4 995-4997 of 1980 along with W.P.
Precedent	No.
Precedent	402 of 1981.
Precedent	355.

Precedent	3069, 3292 and 3436/79 dated 20th August 1979 and (3) Paragraph 1.01 of Chapter I of the Report of <ORG> (popularly known as <ORG>).
Precedent	356.
Precedent	We have referred to the above facts for the purpose of showing that the fixation of ceiling limit on economic criterion was not successful and that for identifying the 'weaker sections', ceiling limit is not the proper test, once the backward class is identified and ascertained.
Precedent	357.
Precedent	Further, it is clear for the afore-mentioned reasons that the Executive while making the division of sub-classification has not properly applied its mind to various factors, indicated above which may ultimately defeat the very purpose of the division or sub-classification.
Precedent	In that view, para 2(i) not only becomes constitutionally invalid but also suffers from the vice of non-application of mind and arbitrariness.
Precedent	358.
Precedent	For the fermentation reason, I am of the  firm view that the division made in the amended OM dividing a section of the people as 'poorer sections' and leaving the remaining as 'non-poorer sections' on economic criterion from and same unit of identified and ascertained SEBCs, having common characteristics the primary of which is the social backwardness as listed in the report of the <ORG>, is not permissible and valid and such a division or sub-classification is liable to be struck down as being violative of Clause (4) of Article 16 of the Constitution.
Precedent	359.
Precedent	A further submission has been made stating that the benefits of reservation are often snatched away or eaten up by top creamy layer of socially advanced backward class who consequent upon their social development no longer suffer from the vice of social backwardness and who are in no way handicapped and who by their high professional qualifications occupy upper echelons in the public services and therefore, the children of those socially advanced section of the people, termed as 'creamy lay er' should be completely removed from the lists of 'Backward Classes' and they should not be allowed to compete with the children of socially under-privileged people and avail the quota of reservation.
Precedent	By way of illustration it is said that if a member of a designated backward class holds a high post by getting through the qualifying examinations of <ORG>, <ORG>, <ORG> or any other <ORG>, there can be no justification in extending the benefit of reservation to their children, because the social status is will advanced and they no longer suffer from the grip of poverty.
Precedent	360.
Precedent	On the same analogy, it has been urged that the children of other professionals such as Doctors, Engineers, Lawyers etc.
Precedent	etc.
Precedent	also should not be given the benefit of reservation, since in such cases, they are not socially handicapped.
Precedent	361.
RatioOfTheDecision	No doubt the above argument on the face of it appears to be attractive and reasonable.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the question is whether those individuals belonging to any particular caste, community or group which sa tisfies the test of backward class should be segregated, picked up and thrown over night out of the arena of backward class.
RatioOfTheDecision	One should not lose sight of the fact that the reservation of appointments or posts in favour of 'any backward class of citizens' in the <ORG> services have not yet been put in practice in spite of the impugned OMs.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is after 42 years since the advent of our Constitution, the Government is taking the first step to implement this scheme of reservation for OBCs under Article 16(4).
Precedent	In fact, some of the States have not even introduced policy of reservation in the matters of public employment in favour of OBCs.
Precedent	362.
Precedent	In opposition, it is said that only a very minimal percentage of BCs have stepped into <ORG> or any other public services by competing in the mainstream along with the candidates of advanced classes despite the fact that their legs are fettered by social backwardness and hence it would be very uncharitable to suddenly deprive their children of  the benefit of reservation under Article 16(4) merely on the ground that their parents have entered into <ORG> services especially when those children are otherwise entitled to the preferential treatment by falling within the definition of 'backward class'.
Precedent	It is further stressed that those children so long as they are wearing the diaper of social backwardness should be given sufficient time till the <ORG> realises on reviews that they are completely free from the shackles of social backwardness and have equated themselves to keep pace with the advanced classes.
Precedent	There are a few decisions of this Court which I have already referred to, holding the view that even if a few individuals in a particular caste, community or group are socially and educationaly above the general average, neither that caste nor that community or group can be held as not being socially backward.
Precedent	(<NAME>).
Precedent	363.

Precedent	365.
Precedent	Meeting an almost similar argument that the 'creamy layers' are snatching away the benefits of reservation,  <NAME>, J. observed in <NAME> to the following effect:  One must, however, enter a caveat to the criticism that the benefits of reservation are often snatched away by the top creamy layer of backward class or caste.
Precedent	That a few of the seats and posts reserved for backward classes are snatched away by the more fortunes among them is not to say that reservation is not necessary.
Precedent	This is bound to happen in a competitive society such as ours.
Precedent	Are not the unreserved seats and posts snatched away, in the same way, by the top creamy layers amongst them on the same principle of merit on which the non reserved seats are taken away by the top layers of society.
Precedent	How can it be bad if reserved seats and posts are snatched away by the creamy layer of backward classes, if such snatching away of unreserved posts by the top creamy layer of society itself is not bad?
Precedent	366.
Precedent	The above observation, in my view, is an apt reply to such a criticism with which I am in full agreement.
Precedent	To quote <NAME>, J.

Precedent	I am also reminded of an adage, "One swallow does not make the summer."
Precedent	367.
Precedent	Reverting to the case on hand, the O.M.
Precedent	does not speak of any 'creamy layer test'.
Precedent	It cannot be said by any stretch of imagination that the Government was not aware of some few individuals having become both socially and educationally above the general average and entered in <ORG> or any other <ORG>.
Precedent	Despite the above fact, the Government has accepted the listed groups of <ORG> as annexed to the Report and it has not thought it prudent to eliminate those individuals.
Precedent	Therefore, in such circumstances, I have my own doubt whether the judicial supremacy can work in the broad area of social policy or in the great vortex of ideological and philosophical decisions directing the exclusion of any section of the people from the accepted list of OBCs on the mere ground that they are all 'creamy layers' which expression is to be tested with reference to various factors or make suggestions f or exclusion of any section of the people who are otherwise entitled for the benefit of reservation in the decision of the <ORG> so long that decision does not suffer from any constitutional infirmity.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	Added to the above submission, it has been urged that some pseudo communities have smuggled into the backward classes and they should be removed from the list of OBCs, lest those communities would be eating away the major portion of the reservation which is meant only for the true and genuine backward classes.
Precedent	There cannot be any dispute that such pseudo communities should be weeded out from the list of backward classes but that exercise must be done only by the <ORG> on proper verification.
Precedent	369.
Precedent	The identification of the backward classes by <ORG> is not with a seal of perpetual finality but on the other hand it is subjected to reviewability by the <ORG>.
Precedent	<ORG> itself in paragraph 13.40 in Chapter XIII has suggested that "the entire scheme should be reviewed  after 20 years."
Precedent	Mr. <NAME> suggested that the list may be reviewed at the interval of 10 years.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are judicial pronouncements to the effect that <ORG> has got the right of reviewability.
RatioOfTheDecision	There cannot be any controversy indeed there is none - that the <ORG> which is certainly interested in the maintenance of standards of its administration, possesses and retains its sovereign authority to adopt general regulatory measures within the consitutional framework by reviewing any of its schemes or policies.
RatioOfTheDecision	The interval of the period at which the review is to be held is within the authority and discretion of the <ORG>, but of course subject to the constitutional parameters and well settled principles of judicial review.
Precedent	Therefore, it is for the <ORG> to review the lists at any point of time and take a decision for the exclusion of any pseudo community or caste smuggled into the backward class or for inclusion of any other community which in the opinion of the <ORG> suffers from soci al backwardness.
Precedent	370.
Precedent	It may be recalled that the petitioner herself in W.P.
Precedent	No.
Precedent	930 of 1990 has stated,"...the <ORG> cannot sit as a super legislature to determine and decide the social issue as to who are socially and educationally backward...."  371.
Precedent	It will be appropriate to refer to an observation of the five-Judges Bench of this <ORG> (which heard initially these matters) in its order dated 8th August 1991 stating:  The validity of the Mandal Commission Report as such is not in issue before us....  372.
Precedent	A three-Judges Bench of this <ORG> comprising of <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>, <NAME>.
Precedent	(as the learned Chief Justices then were) has observed in their order dated 21st September 1990 that the implementation of executive decisions is in the hands of the Government of the day but constitutional validity of such action is a matter for <ORG>'s examination.
Precedent	373.
Precedent	Thereafter, <ORG> of this <ORG> by their order dated 1st October 1990 explained the earlier order stating "Three out of us  sitting as a Bench on the 21st September 1990 made an order after hearing parties wherein we had indicated that the decision to implement three aspects of the recommendations of <ORG> was a political one and ordinarily the <ORG> would not interfere with such a decision."
Precedent	374.
Precedent	Therefore, when this <ORG> is not called upon to lay a test or give any guideline as to who are all to be eliminated from the listed groups of the <ORG>, there in no necessity to lay any test much less 'creamy layer test'.
Precedent	I find no grey area to be clarified and consequently hold that what one is not free to do directly cannot do it indirectly by adopting any means.
Precedent	Therefore, the argument of 'creamy layer' pales into insignificance.
Precedent	375.
Precedent	Further I hold that all SEBCs brought in the lists of the <ORG> which have been accepted and approved by the Government should be given equal opportunity in availing the benefits of the 27 per cent reservation.
Precedent	In other words, the entire 27% of the vacancies in civil posts and se rvices under <ORG> shall be reserved and extended to all the SEBCs.
Precedent	376.
Precedent	In fact, the first <ORG> dated 13th August 1990 does not make any division or sub-classification as in the amended <ORG>.
Precedent	Para 2(i) of the first <ORG> reads, "27% of the vacancies in civil posts and services in <ORG> shall be reserved for SEBCs."
Precedent	In reading para 2 (i) of the first <ORG> in juxtaposition with para 2(i) of the amended <ORG>, no basic difference in the policies of the two Government is spelt out; in that both the impugned <ORG>s have made 27% reservation in civil posts and services under <ORG> for SEBCs" on the basis of the recommendations of <ORG> (Mandal Report).
Precedent	The only difference between the two impugned <ORG>s is that in the amended <ORG> a division among the SEBCs is made as 'poorer sections' and others that the 'poorer sections' is firstly allowed to avail the benefit of reservation of only the unfilled vacancies.
Precedent	Therefore, by striking down para 2(i) o f the amended <ORG> as unconstitutional, I hold that there is no legal impediment in implementing para 2(i) of the first <ORG> dated 13th August 1990 which has not been supersed, rescinded or repealed but "deemed to have been amended."
Precedent	377.
Precedent	Before parting with this aspect of the matter, I would like to express my view that the 'poorer sections' of the <ORG> may be provided with various kinds of concessions and facilities such as educational concessions, special coaching facilities, financial assistance, relaxation of upper age limit, increase of number of attempts etc.
Precedent	for government services with a view to give them equal opportunity to compete and keep pace with the advanced sections of the people.
Precedent	378.
Precedent	Whether 10% reservation in favour of 'other economically backward section' is permissible under Article 16?
Precedent	379.
Precedent	Now I shall pass on to paragraph 2(ii) of the amended <ORG> which reveals that 10 per cent of the vacancies in civil posts and services under <ORG> shall be reserved for other econom ically backward sections of the people who are not covered by any of the existing schemes of reservation.
Precedent	380.
Precedent	This reservation of 10 per cent cannot be held to be constitutionally valid as concluded by my learned brother <NAME>, J. for the reasons, mentioned in paragraph 115 of his judgment.
Precedent	I am in full agreement with his conclusion on this issue of 10% reservation.
Precedent	381.
Precedent	Whether Article 16(4) contemplates reservation in the matter of promotion?
Precedent	382.
Precedent	<CITATION_734531>  Supp.
Precedent	1 SCC 594, a three-Judges Bench of this Court to which I was a party has taken a view that once candidates even from reserved communities are allocated and appointed to a Service based on their ranks and performance and brought under the one and same stream of category, then they too have to be treated on par with all other selected candidates and there cannot be any question of preferential treatment at that stage on the ground that they belong to reserved community though they may be entitled for all other statutory benef its such as the relaxation of age, the reservation etc.
Precedent	Reservation referred to in that context is referable to the reservation at the initial stage or the entry point as could be gathered from that judgment.
Precedent	383.
Precedent	It may be recalled, in this connection, the view expressed by Chief Justice <NAME> in <NAME> that "efficiency has been kept in view and not sacrificed".
Precedent	384.
Precedent	Hence, I share the view of my learned brother <NAME> J. holding that " Article 16(4) does not permit provision for reservation in the matter of promotions and that this rule shall, however, have only prospective operation and shall not affect the promotions already made, whether made on regular basis or on any other basis" and the direction given by him that wherever reservations are provided in the matter of promotion such reservation may continue in operation for a period of five years from this day.
Precedent	385.
Precedent	In Summation (1) Article 16(4) of the Constitution is neither an exception nor a proviso to Article 16(1).
Precedent	It is exhaustive of  all the reservations that can be made in favour of backward class of citizens.
Precedent	It has an over-riding effect on Article 16(1) and (2).
Precedent	(2) No Reservation can be made under Article 16(4) for classes other than backward classes.
Precedent	But under Article 16(1 ), reservation can be made for classes, not covered by Article 16(4).
RatioOfTheDecision	(3) The expression, 'backward class of citizens' occurring in Article 16(4) is neither defined nor explained in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	However, the backward class or classes can certainly be identified in Hindu society with reference to castes along with other criteria such as traditional occupation, poverty, place of residence, lack of education etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	and in communities where caste is not recognised by the above recognised and accepted criteria except caste criterion.
RatioOfTheDecision	(4) In the process of identification of backward class of citizens and under Article 16(4) among Hindus, caste is a primary criterion or a dominant factor though it is not the sole criterion.
RatioOfTheDecision	(5) Any provision under Article 16(4) i s not necessarily to be made by the <ORG> or <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a provision could also be made by an Executive order.
RatioOfTheDecision	(6) The power conferred on the <ORG> under Article 16(4) is one coupled with a duty and, therefore, the <ORG> has to exercise that power for the benefit of all those, namely, backward class for whom it is intended.
RatioOfTheDecision	(7) The provision for reservation of appointments or posts in favour of any backward class of citizens is a matter of policy of the <ORG>, of course subject to the constitutional parameters and well settled principle of judicial review.
RatioOfTheDecision	(8) The expression 'poorer sections' mentioned in para 2 (i) of the amended Office Memorandum of 1991 denotes a division among SEBCs on economic criterion.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, no division or sub-classification as 'poorer sections' and other backward class (non poorer sections) out of the identified SEBCs can be made by application of 'means test' based on economic criterion.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a division in the same identified and ascertained unit consisting of  SEBCs having common characteristics and attributes, the primary characteristic or attribute being the social backwardness is violative of Clause (4) of Article 16 of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Hence, the division of the SEBCs as 'poorer sections' and others, brought out in para 2(i) of the impugned amended Office Memorandum dated 25th September 1991 is constitutionally invalid and impermissible.
RatioOfTheDecision	Accordingly, para 2(i) of the said amended Office Memorandum is struck down.
RatioOfTheDecision	(9) No maximum ceiling of reservation can be fixed under Article 16(4) of the Constitution for reservation of appointments or posts in favour of any backward class of citizens "in the <ORG> under the <ORG>".
RatioOfTheDecision	The decisions fixing the percentage of reservation only up to the maximum of 50% are unsustainable.
RatioOfTheDecision	(10) As regards the reservation in the matter of promotion under Article 16(4 ), I am in agreement with conclusion No.
Fact	(7) made in paragraph 121 in Part VII of the judgment of my learned brother.
Fact	<NAME>, J..  (11) I also agree with  conclusion No.
RulingByLowerCourt	(8) of paragraph 121 of the judgment of my learned brother, <NAME>, <NAME> exception to the rule of reservation to certain <ORG> and posts.
RulingByLowerCourt	(12) The reservation of 10% of the vacancies in civil posts and <ORG> in favour of other economically backward sections of the people who are not covered by any other scheme of the reservation as mentioned in para 2(ii) of the impugned amended Officer Memorandum dated 25th September 1991 is constitutionally invalid and it is accordingly struck down.
RulingByLowerCourt	In this regard, I am also in agreement with conclusion No.
RulingByLowerCourt	(11) of paragraph 121 of the judgment of my learned brother, <NAME>, J.

RulingByLowerCourt	(14) Para 2(i) and (ii) of the amended Office Memorandum dated 25th September 1991 for the reasons gi ven in my judgment and the conclusions drawn above, are struck down as being violative of Article 16(4).
RulingByLowerCourt	(15) The impugned Office Memorandum dated 13th August 1990 is held valid and enforceable.
RulingByLowerCourt	So there is no legal impediment in immediately enforcing and implementing this first Office Memorandum of 1990.
Fact	(16) In Writ Petition No.
RatioOfTheDecision	1094 of 1991 (<ORG> v. <ORG>), there is a prayer (prayer 'b'), inter alia, for issuance of a writ of mandamus directing the respondent to implement the impugned unamended office memorandum dated 13th August 1990.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the light of my conclusions, striking down the amended office memorandum dated 25th September 1991, I direct the <ORG> to immediately implement the unamended office memorandum dated 13th August 1990.
Precedent	(17) The Government of India and the State Governments have to create a permanent machinery either by way of a <ORG> or a <ORG> within a reasonable time for examining the requests of inclusion or exclusion of any  caste, community or group of persons on the advice of such <ORG> or <ORG>, as the case may be, and also for examining the exclusion of any pseudo community if smuggled into the list of OBCs.
Precedent	The creation of such a machinery in the form of a <ORG> or <ORG> does not stand in the way of immediate implementation of the office memorandum dated 13.8.1990 and the purpose of creating such machinery is for future guidance.
Precedent	(18) I am also of the same view of my learned brother, <NAME>, J. that it is not necessary to send the matters back to <ORG>.
Precedent	386.
Precedent	In the result, for the reasons mentioned in my judgment and the conclusions drawn in the summation, the writ petition No.
RulingByPresentCourt	1094 of 1991 is partly allowed to the extent indicated above and all other Writ Petitions, Transferred Cases and Interlocutory Applications are disposed of accordingly.

Precedent	Dr. <NAME>, J.
Precedent	387.
Precedent	The petitioners challenge O.M.
Precedent	No.
Precedent	36012/31/90-Estt(SCT) dated 13th August, 1990 as  amended by O.M.
RulingByLowerCourt	No.
RulingByLowerCourt	36012/31/90-Estt(SCT) dated 25th September, 1991 providing in civil posts and services under <ORG> for reservation of 27% of the vacancies for the Socially and Educationally Backward Classes (<ORG>s) and 10% of the vacancies for other economically backward sections of the people.

RulingByLowerCourt	The reservation postulated in these orders for the socially and educationally backward classes an d also for the economically backward sections of the people in the <ORG> services to the extent of 27% and 10% respectively is in addition to the reservation already made for the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes to the extent of 22.5%.
RulingByLowerCourt	390.
Argument	These orders are made pursuant to the <ORG> submitted by <ORG> appointed by the President of India under Article 340 of the Constitution.
Argument	This <ORG> is generally known by the name of the Chairman of the <ORG>, <NAME>.
Argument	The petitioners submit that the <ORG> leading to the impugned Government Orders is not based on any scientific or objective study of backwardness in the country, and any attempt to make reservation on the basis of the data supplied in the <ORG> is irrational, unconstitutional and invalid.
Argument	They say that the <ORG> is conceived in caste prejudices and motivated by caste hatred.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> does not address itself to a proper identification of true backwardness for the redressal of which the  Constitution permits reservation by quota for the backward classes of citizens to the exclusion of all other persons.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand, the sole criterion on the basis of which backwardness is purportedly identified is caste and nothing but caste.
RatioOfTheDecision	Any order resulting in reservation or other affirmative action on the basis of the wrong conclusions drawn by the <ORG> is bound to be the very antithesis of equality.
Precedent	391.
Argument	The respondents, supporting the impugned Government orders, contend that the Constitution guarantees liberty, equality and fraternity for all classes of people irrespective of their religion, community, caste, occupation, residence or the like.
RatioOfTheDecision	Every citizen is entitled to equal opportunities.
RatioOfTheDecision	For centuries, large sections of our countrymen have been discriminated against on account of their birth.
RatioOfTheDecision	As a result of such inequity, they have been steeped in poverty, ignorance and squalor.
RatioOfTheDecision	To alleviate their misery and elevate them to positions of equality with the more fortunate, affluent and  enlightened sections of our countrymen, the Founding Fathers of the Constitution made special provisions for their uplift.
RatioOfTheDecision	These provisions are meant to protect the truly backward people of this country, namely, members of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes and other backward classes.
RatioOfTheDecision	They contend that <ORG> is a scientific and serious study rationally addressed to the problem of backwardness by identifying it where it is most acutely felt and loudly present, namely, amongst the lowest of the lowly citizens of this country.
RatioOfTheDecision	Those are the members of the low castes as traditionally recognised and identified by the <ORG> and <ORG>.
Precedent	The various classes of people belonging to such castes are identified as socially, educationally and economically backward and it is in respect of those people that the <ORG> have made the impugned reservations.
Precedent	392.
Precedent	The 'indicators' or 'criteria' adopted in <ORG> are broadly grouped as social, educational and economic on the basis of  castes/classes.
Precedent	The <ORG> has identified classes with castes and backwardness with particular castes.
Precedent	Castes which are socially, educationally and economically backward are characterised as backward classes entitled to the benefit of reservation.
Precedent	Persons are grouped on the basis of caste either because they are members of it by reason of their being Hindus or because they were members of it in the past prior to their conversion to other religions.
Precedent	Identification of backwardness is thus made with reference to the present or past caste affiliations of the people.
RulingByLowerCourt	The Report says:-  12.4.
RulingByLowerCourt	In fact, caste being the basic unit of social organisation of <ORG>, castes are the only readily and clearly 'recognisable and persistent collectivities'.
RulingByLowerCourt	12.6.
RulingByLowerCourt	...the <ORG> has also applied some other tests like stigmas of low occupation, criminality, nomadism, beggary and untouchability to identify social backwardness.
Fact	Inadequate representation in public services was taken as another important test.
Precedent	393.
Precedent	I n regard to non-Hindus, the Report says:-  12.11 There is no doubt that social and educational backwardness among non-Hindu communities is more or less of the same order as among Hindu communities.
Precedent	Though caste system is peculiar to Hindu society yet, in actual practice, it also pervades the non-Hindu communities in India in varying degrees...even after conversion, the ex-Hindus carried with them their deeply ingrained ideas of social hierarchy and stratification....  12.14...even after conversion, the lower caste converts were continued to be treated as Harijans by all sections of the society....  12.18 ...the <ORG> has evolved the following rough and ready criteria for identifying non-Hindu OBCs:-  (i) All untouchables converted to any non-Hindu religion; and  (ii) Such occupational communities which are known by the name of their traditional hereditary occupation and whose Hindu counterparts have been included in the list of Hindu OBCs.
Precedent	(Examples : Dhobi, Teli, Dheemar, Nai, Gujar, Kumhar, Lohar, Darj i, Badhai, etc.).
RulingByLowerCourt	The <ORG> has thus treated all persons who belong, or who had once belonged, to what had been regarded as untouchable or other traditionally backward caste or communities or who belong to certain low occupations as socially, educationally and economically backward.
RulingByLowerCourt	394.
RulingByLowerCourt	The particulars of the Mandal <ORG> and other material relied on by the <ORG> in making the impugned orders do not directly arise for our consideration at this juncture as this Bench has been constituted to examine the concept of equality of opportunity in matters of public employment, as enshrined in Article 16 and other provisions of the Constitution, 'and settle the legal position relating to reservation' and thus lay down the guideline by which the validity and reasonableness of <ORG> Orders on reservation can be tested in appropriate cases.
Fact	395.
Fact	The Concept of Reservation:  The fundamental question is, what is the raison d'etre of reservation and what are its limits.
Precedent	The Constitution permits the <ORG> to a dopt such affirmative action as it deems necessary to uplift the backward classes of citizens to levels of equality with the rest of our countrymen.
Precedent	The backward classes of citizens have been in the past denied access to <ORG> services on account of their inability to compete effectively in open selections on the basis of merits.
Precedent	It is, therefore, open to the <ORG> to reserve a certain number of seats in places of learning and public services in favour of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes and other backward classes to the exclusion of all others, irrespective of merits.
Precedent	The impugned <ORG> orders, have made reservation by setting aside quotas in <ORG> services exclusively for backward classes of candidates.
Precedent	396.
Precedent	Referring to the concept of equality of opportunity in public employment, as embodied in Article 10 of the Draft Constitution, which finally emerged as Article 16 of the Constitution, and the conflicting claims of various communities for representation in public administrat ion, Dr. <NAME> emphatically declared that reservation should be confined to 'a minority of seats', lest the very concept of equality should be destroyed.
Precedent	In view of its great importance, the full text of this speech delivered in <ORG> on the point is appended to this judgment.
Precedent	But I shall now read a few passages from it.
Precedent	Dr. <NAME> stated:  ...firstly, that there shall be equality of opportunity, secondly that there shall be reservations in favour of certain communities which have not so far had a 'poorer look- in' so to say into the administration.... Supposing, for instance, we were to concede in full the demand of those communities who have not been so far employed in the public services to the fullest extent, what would really happen is, we shall be completely destroying the first proposition upon which we are all agreed, nemely, that there shall be an equality of opportunity....Therefore the seats to be reserved, if the reservation is to be consistent with Sub-clause (1) of Articl e 10 , must be confined to a minority of seats.
Precedent	It is then only that the first principle could find its place in the Constitution and effective in operation...we have to safeguard two things, namely, the principle of equality of opportunity and at the same time satisfy the demand of communities which have not had so far representation in the <ORG>....  397.
Precedent	<ORG>, Vol.
Precedent	7, pp.
Precedent	701-702 (1948-49).
Precedent	(emphasis supplied) These words embody the raison d'etre of reservation and its limitations.
Precedent	Reservation is one of the measures adopted by the Constitution to remedy the continuing evil effects of prior inequities stemming form discriminatory practices against various classes of people which have resulted in their social, educational and economic backwardness.
Precedent	Reservation is meant to be addressed to the present social, educational and economic backwardness caused by purposeful societal discrimination.
Precedent	To attack the continuing ill effects and perpetuation of such injustice, the Constitution permi ts and empowers the <ORG> to adopt corrective devices even when they have discriminatory and exclusionary effects.
Precedent	Any such measure, in so far as one group is preferred to the exclusion of another, must necessarily be narrowly tailored to the achievement of the fundamental constitutional goal.
Precedent	398.
Precedent	What the Constitution permits is the adoption of suitable and appropriate measures to correct the continuing evil effects of prior discrimination.
Precedent	Over-inclusiveness in such measures by unduly widening the net of reservation to unjustifiably protect the ill deserved at the expense of the others would result in invidious discrimination offending the Constitutional objective.
Precedent	Benign classification for affirmative action by reservation must stay strictly within the narrow bounds of remedial actions.
Precedent	Any such programme must be consistent with the fundamental objective of equality.
Precedent	Classes of people saddled with disabilities rooted in history of purposeful unequal treatment and consequently relegated to social, educati onal, economic and political power-lessness particularly qualify to demand the extraordinary and special protection of reservation.
Precedent	399.
Precedent	Reservation is meant to remedy the handicap of prior discrimination impeding the access of classes of people to public administration.
Precedent	It is for the <ORG> to determine whether the evil effects of inequities stemming from prior discrimination against classes of people have resulted in their being reduced to positions of backwardness and consequent under representation in public administration.
Precedent	Reservation is a remedy or a cure for the ill effects of historical discrimination.
Precedent	400.
Precedent	While affirmative action programmes by preferential treatment short of reservation in favour of disadvantaged classes of citizens may be justified as benign redressal measures based on valid classification, the more positive affirmative action adopting reservation by quota or other 'set aside' measures or goals in favour of certain classes of citizens to the exclusion of others most be narrowly ta ilored and strictly addressed to the problem which is sought to be remedied by the Constitution.
Precedent	Any such action by the <ORG> must necessarily be subjected to periodic administrative review by specially constituted authorities so as to guarantee that such policies and actions are applied correctly and strictly to permitted constitutional ends.
Precedent	4.1.
Precedent	Reservation is not an end in itself.
Precedent	It is a means to achieve equality.
Precedent	The policy of reservation adopted to achieve that end must, therefore, be consistent with the objective in view.
Precedent	Reservation must not outlast its constitutional object, and must not allow a vested interest to develop and perpetuate itself.
Precedent	There will be no need for reservation or preferential treatment once equality is achieved.
Precedent	Achievement and preservation of equality for all classes of people, irrespective of their birth, creed, faith or language is one on the noble ends to which the Constitution is dedicated.
Precedent	Every reservation founded on benign discrimination, and justifiably adopted to ach ieve the constitutional mandate of equality, must necessarily be a transient passage to that end.
Precedent	It is temporary in concept, limited in duration, conditional in application and specific in object.
Precedent	Reservation must contain within itself the seeds of its termination.
Precedent	Any attempt to perpetuate reservation and upset the constitutional mandate of equality is destructive of liberty and fraternity and all the basic values enshrined in the Constitution.
Precedent	A balance has to be maintained between the competing values and the rival claims and interests so as to achieve equality and freedom for all.
Precedent	401.
Precedent	The makers of the Constitution were fully conscious of the unfortunate position of the Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes.
Precedent	To them equality, liberty and fraternity are but a dream; an ideal guaranteed by the law, but far too distant to reach; far too illusory to touch.
Precedent	These backward people and others in like positions of helplessness are the favoured children of the Constitution.
Precedent	It is for them that ameliorative and r emedial measures are adopted to achieve the end of equality.
Precedent	To permit those who are not intended to be so specially protected to compete for reservation is to dilute the protection and defeat the very constitutional aim.
Precedent	402.
Precedent	The victims of prior injustice are the special favourites of the laws.
Precedent	Their plight is a shameful scar on the national conscience.
Precedent	It is a constitutional command that prompt measures are adopted by the <ORG> for the promotion of these unfortunate classes of people specially to positions of comparative enlightement, culture, knowledge, influence, affluence and prestige so as to place them on levels of equality with the more fortunate of our countrymen.
Precedent	403.
Precedent	Reservation must one day become unnecessary and a relic of an unfortunate past.
Precedent	Every such action must be a transient self-liquidating programme.
Precedent	That is the hope and dream cherished by the Constitution Makers and that is the end to which the <ORG> has to address itself in making special provisions for the chosen classes of people f or special constitutional protection, so that "persons will be regarded as persons, and discrimination of the type we address today will be an ugly feature of history that is instructive but that is behind us"; Per Justice <NAME>, Regents of <ORG> v. <NAME> 438 US 265, 57 L Ed.

Precedent	417.
Precedent	The 1950 Order of the President (as amended) shows that in the territories of the States mentioned in the Order no person who is not a Hindu or a Sikh or a Buddhist can be regarded as a member of the Scheduled Castes.
Precedent	Article 15(4) speaks of 'socially and educationally backward classes of citizens' and 'the Scheduled  Castes and the Scheduled Tribes' while Article 16(4) speaks only of 'any backward class of citizens'.
Precedent	The 'backward class' mentioned in Article 16(4) is a synonym for the classes mentioned in Article 15(4); <NAME> (supra); <NAME> and <NAME>.
Precedent	(supra).
Precedent	These two provisions read with the President's Order of 1950 (as amended in 1976) show that the benefit of Article 15(4) and Article 16(4) extends to the Scheduled Castes (which expression is confined to those professing the Hindu, the Sikh or the Buddhist religion) and the Scheduled Tribes as well as the backward classes of citizens who must necessarily be such backward classes of citizens who would have, but for their not professing the Hindu, the Sikh or the Buddhist religion, qualified to be notified as members of the Scheduled Castes.
Precedent	This means, all those depressed classes of citizens who suffered the odium and isolation of untouchability prior to their conversion to other religions and whose backwardness continued despite their conversion c ome within the expression 'backward classes of citizens' in Articles 15(4) and 16(4).
Precedent	Untouchability is a humiliating and shameful malady caused by deep-rooted prejudice which does not disappear with the change of faith.
Precedent	To say that it does would imply that faith is the ultimate cause of untouchability.
Precedent	This is, of course, not true.
Precedent	If backwardness caused by historical discrimination and its consequential disadvantages are the reasons for reservation the Constitution mandates that all backward classes of citizens, who are the victims of the continuing ill effects of prior discrimination, whatever be their faith or religion, or whether or not they profess any religion, receive the same benefits which are accorded to the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes.
Precedent	Backward class is composed of persons whose backwardness is in degree and nature comparable to that of the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes, whatever be their religion.
Precedent	There can be no doubt about the identity of the Scheduled Castes and the S cheduled Tribes.
Precedent	Nor can there be any doubt about the identity of backward classes other than the Scheduled Castes and the Scheduled Tribes, if this identifying characteristic, bearing the stamp of prior discrimination and its continuing ill effects, is borne in mind.
